And place it in the middle of the ulceration until it penetrates to the skin: and let the cauterization be from what follows, so that the flow of pus may be alleviated.
Then treat it until it heals.
About the method of cauterizing in corroding evil.
Head 54.
It is not a corrosive unless it is a decay in a walking limb, and it devours it: just as fire consumes dry wood.
If therefore you see a corrosive in a place that can tolerate cautery with fire, then apply many small and large cauteries with fire, according to what is convenient for that place where the corrosive is.
Then cauterize it on every side until you eradicate the entire corruption, and nothing of it remains deeply.
Then leave it for three days, and apply sulfur powder mixed with oil to the cauterized places, until all scabs are eradicated, and all corruption.
Then treat it with ointments that generate flesh.
If therefore you see that after three days healthy flesh is born, in which there is no corruption, it is good.
And if not: then repeat the cautery on what remains of the corrupted places.
And a corrosive is treated with acute medicine whenever it is necessary. It itself rises up in the place of the cautery.
However, a cautery with fire is of quicker help; and I have already mentioned its cauteration with acute medicine in the division: therefore take it then when you need it.
On the method of cauterizing in nails and warts.
Head 55.
It often happens that this disease occurs in the lower parts of the feet: and it is a thick, filthy thing that hurts the foot.
And indeed, cautery on them is according to two methods: cautery with fire, and cautery with hot water.
As for the cautery with fire, it is like a hollow cautery iron shaped like a bird's feather: which is made from iron, according to the quantity that contains a nail on every side: and let its edge be subtle.
Then put it, ignited, on a nail. Then wrap the hand with a cauterizing iron around the nail, until the cautery reaches the depth of the nail; and leave it for three days, until it begins to discharge pus. Then apply a poultice made with forest herbs, mixed with salt; and leave the poultice on it at night. For it will be eradicated from its roots.
Then treat the place with a flesh-producing ointment, until it heals.
If they are not converted, and often occur on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet: then administer to them cautery with hot water: and it is, that you take a tube made of copper, or iron, or from the quill of a bird: and place it on the nail, or the flesh around it. Then pour a small amount of hot water into the tube: hold your hand, and wrap it with pressure from your hand for a short time, so that the mark of the tube is imprinted on the root of the nail, and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the root of the nail: and wait a little while over the bite of the hot water for an hour.
Then leave it, for the nail is eradicated with its roots.
Do it thus to each one after another, until you are free from all of them that are on the body.
Then treat them after they are eradicated, with what generates flesh from ointments.
On the method of cautery for tremors.
When a tremor occurs from cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or from something else: then it is necessary to cauterize it with four cauteries, or five on the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra with a cautery, and on its chest with a cautery, and on its stomach with a cautery.
The trembling indeed ceases with the cautery of olive oil, and a healthy recovery is hastened from a cold disease.
On the method of cautery according to Albathir.
Chapter 57.
When there are formed on the body of Albathir's foul abscesses, that is, pustules, which come from corrupted gross materials.
It is necessary, therefore, first of all, when they appear, to apply a subtle cautery with a burning stick of myrtle wood to every pustule on the head. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lentricular cautery.
And when they are cauterized to the point of induration, at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner: and they do not increase: and the excess is dispersed, making them healthy, and they are healed from them.
However, it is necessary that it be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
On the method of cautery for a flow of blood from an artery in a vein.
It often happens that there is a flow of blood from an artery, which has been cut near the vein, whether it occurs externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or the cautery of a limb, and similar things: and it becomes difficult to stop it.
When it happens to someone in that way, then quickly put your hand over the orifice of the artery, and place your index finger on it: and press it strongly, until the blood is obstructed under your finger, and nothing comes out from it.
Then put small olive oil cauteries on the fire, and a large number of them: and blow on them, until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, or a small one, or a large one, according to the vein and place where the artery is disrupted, and apply the cautery to the vein itself, after which you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
If it is expelled with elevated fingers from the orifice of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then take another cautery, which has been prepared in the fire, quickly.
Therefore, do not cease to make that with one after another, until the blood is staunched.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve which is there, and a worse evil falls upon the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to stanch it: especially since the artery is large, unless with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it has not been extracted. For when it is extracted, the extremities of the artery contract, and the blood is staunched. Or it is tied with a strong thread of ligature. Or medicines of suitable properties are applied to it, which are for staunching blood, and are bound with a decent poultice of constriction.
Whoever administers the staunching of it with a ligature, and a binding with cloth, or a positioning of burning remedies, and similar things, does not confer a complete cure to him, unless rarely.
If it happens to anyone in that way, and there is no present doctor, nor medicine: then let him begin with the positioning of the index finger on the orifice of the vein itself, as we have narrated, and a strong constriction, until the blood is stopped from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger is not removed, but instead remains on it with vehemently cold water, always, until the blood congeals, and coagulates, and is staunched.
And meanwhile, let him consider in that, what he needs from a cautery or from medicine.
On Incisions, Perforations, and Bloodletting for Wounds, and Similar Things.
We will discuss in the First Chapter all kinds of diseases for which it is suitable to use fire-cautery and burning medicine, and their occasions, their causes, and instruments, and forms of cauteries.
And I have placed chapters on the vertebrae up to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter by the same method: to alleviate the sufferings of the sufferer.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you to learn, oh sons, what is in this chapter, which is about the terror of that which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary to be more cautious, since in the operation which is in this chapter, often evacuation with blood occurs, on which life depends at the opening of a vein, or incision over an abscess, or opening of an exit, or curing of a wound, or extraction of a arrow, or incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of which tremble, fear, and death often occur in many of them.
And I warn you, lest it falls upon you that which is hesitation, for when they fall to you in this art of men with modes of infirmities. For there is one who becomes angry with his own disease, and death is light to him: because of the violence of that which he finds from his own infirmity and the length of his evil. And the disease is from fear which signifies death.
And there is one who gives his own physician, and says to you with him, hoping for health, and his disease is pernicious.
Therefore it is not necessary to consent to him who comes to you from those, whose manner is such. And let our caution be greater than our desire and our own grasping avarice. And let us not presume upon anything from that unless after certain knowledge, which verifies with us what has been attained through successful experience.
And let us use judgment in the treatment of all our patients, in prognosis and indication to that which may conveniently be their salvation.
For in it lies your help towards acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with His intellect, and may He not deprive you of direction and inspiration; for that is in His hand.
And I have indeed arranged this Chapter by distinguishing it into sections, according to what preceded in the Chapter of Cauterii, from the head to the feet: so that you may be alleviated of what you wish from it, and of what you need.
On the treatment of Water that accumulates on the heads of children.
Chapter I.
You should know that this illness often happens to children at birth: or when the midwife tightly grips the child's head without gentleness.
And it sometimes happens also from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have indeed not seen this illness in others than in children: and to all whom I saw among them, death hastened; therefore it seemed to me that it was necessary to abandon the operation in these.
And I have indeed seen among them a child whose head was filled with water, and the head was enlarged every day, until he could not sit up by himself, due to the greatness of his head: and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed collects either between the skin and bone, or it collects beneath the bone above the skull.
And the operation in that case, if there is humidity between the skin and bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary to make a fissure in the middle of the head, about two inches in length, until the humidity flows out.
And this is the form of a spatula-like instrument.
If therefore the humidity is greatly increased and the abscess is larger, then make two fissures, according to this form.
And if the humidity is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the commissures of the head open on all sides; and the water is pressed out when you press it with your hand inwardly, and it does not remain hidden from you: then it is necessary to make three fissures in the middle of the head, according to this method.
And after the incision, extract the entire humidity. Then close the fissures with bandages and poultices. Then apply a compress of wine and oil to it, until the fifth day.
Then remove the bandage, and treat the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound tightly. And give the patient light food with little humidity, until the member is comforted and healed.
And another method of incision is to consider where the greatness of the abscess appears, and the collection of water.
Since sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the forehead, or on the right, or on the left.
Ascend therefore with the incision where you see the abscess and the collection of water, and divide it according to what is possible for you.
And take care not to cut the artery, and a flow of blood occurs, and the patient dies from that flow with the evacuation of humidity.
Concerning the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Head 2.
When some flux falls onto the eyes or chest, and it becomes chronic, and medical treatments do not help: then the last resort is to cut into those two arteries.
It is necessary, therefore, when you want to make an incision in them, that a weak head should be shaved. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries may appear. Then the weak person should constrict their neck with any cloth of theirs. Then consider where the vein pulses. And both places are located behind the ears, depressed areas; and they are rarely hidden, unless in certain individuals.
Then mark with a branding iron on both sides; and make an incision on each side with a three-edged scalpel, making the incision up to the bone: and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it upwards with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' width joined together.
For when a vein is cut, blood flows out in a pulsating flow, which jumps inward with frequent leaps.
If the artery does not appear to the senses, then it is necessary to measure from the ear the quantity of a three-finger space. Then mark with a branding iron, and find your way up to the bone.
And what is necessary to emit from the blood is six ounces, or perhaps more or less will be emitted from it. All that according to the quantity which appears to you from the strength of the weak person and the replenishment of their artery.
Then consider the ulcer. Then if something remains to the bone from the abscess, then cut into it, so that a hot aposteme does not occur: then constrict the ulcer with a linen bandage.
Then treat it with an ointment until it heals.
Concerning the removal of arteries that are in the temples.
Head 3.
When an old hemicrania afflicts a person, or acute catarrh due to acidic and salty humidities, and heat in the temples, or an old soda, which is vehement, and similar things: and they are treated with the methods of medical cure, but it does not help: then we have recourse to extracting the arteries from the temples, or cauterizing them, as we have described.
And the method of operating in extracting them is to shave the weak hairs that are in the temples, and to make an incision towards the apparent artery in the temple. It is shown to you by its own pulse, and is rarely hidden unless in a few men, or due to the vehemence of cold.
If it is hidden from you, then he should tightly bind his weak head with any cloth of his; then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or warm the place with hot water, until it appears to you in a clear manifestation. Then take the spatula that I formed a little while before. Then scrape with it the skin with ease, until you penetrate to the artery. Then insert a hook into it, and draw it upwards, until you extract it from the skin, and make it emerge from the tunics that are under it on every side.
If the artery is fine: then envelop it with the extremity of the hook. Then cut from it a part according to the quantity by which its extremities are lengthened, and contracted, so that there is no flow of blood. For if it is not cut, and if it is not separated, the blood does not ascend deeply.
Then evacuate from the blood about six ounces, until there are three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it off in two places with double thread, strongly, from silk or from Al-od chord, so that putrefaction does not occur at the wound before consolidation, and a flow of blood happens.
Then cut away the excess, which is between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone, with a cautery knife: until the extremities of it are cut away, and it stands firm in its place of operation.
And it is a better work, unless the patient has a fever, or is of a hot constitution. For the cautery is one of those things that help to end humidity. Therefore it is a better remedy.
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries, that the place be filled with old cotton: and that pieces of lint, decently made, be put over it.
And after the wound is cleansed with dry medicines that promote the growth of flesh, and with bandages, until it heals.
If during the operation a flow of blood occurs from the artery, then hasten to detach it with a cautery, or fill the place with cotton: and bind your hand tightly, until the bleeding is stopped. If none of these things is available to you, then put your finger on the place, until the blood congeals. And sprinkle the place with water of vehement coldness, until the inflammation subsides, and bind according to what is necessary.
And of what is lighter and easier for the extraction of the artery, it is to cauterize the vein with a cautery having two blades: and let the inflammation be similar to that of two-bladed Al-magdah. However, there are many of lesser acuteness than two scalpels. Because if they are sharp, like the two scalpels, they require corresponding coolness; and they do not cut into the flesh with swiftness. Since therefore there is a certain amount of roughness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut away swiftly; since this is better than any operation, and easier, and more facile.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let there be a space that is between the two scalpels the amount of roughness of a finger, after you have marked two places with ink; and apply both ignited scalpels strongly, until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between the extremities may be elongated. For they do not consolidate with this cautery thoroughly.
Chapter 4.
On the treatment of chronic and acute tears flowing from the eyes.
When there are persistent tears and their flow is from the veins that are on the outer surface of the skull; and it does not help to apply any medical treatment with medicines, and you see the face of the patient already reddened, and he feels a sensation of walking in his forehead, as if it were the walking of ants; and his eyes are exhausted, moist, so that they have already been corroded by corrosion: and his eyelids are heated up from the acuteness of the tears. Then the ancients sought help in the treatment of this operation, and named it a cure, with Ense.
And it is so that you should advise the patient to scratch the hairs that are on his forehead. Then make three equal distant incisions in the forehead along its length: and let the length of each incision be about two fingers, and one incision should be equidistant from the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length of a thumb's movement, and the third on the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries that are in both sides: and extend your hand at the continuation of the two jaws, and let the space of each incision be the amount of three joined fingers: and have a piece of prepared sponge or many dry cloths ready, with which you will dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula of two acute extremities, whose following shape is conical. From the incision which is on the temporal part, up to the second incision: and excavate with it all the skin, which is between the two incisions, along with the sfac, which is above the bone. Then do the same also from the middle incision up to the second incision. Then extract this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp scalpel, being blunt on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its shape.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its blunt side towards the bone: and push it, until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut away with it all the vessels, which descend from the head to the eyes: except what is contained in the incision up to that which appears from the skin.
Then do the same in the middle fissure up to another fissure.
And after a quantity of blood equal in amount has flowed out, press out the places from the clots of coagulated blood.
Then put into every fissure a linen cloth soaked in old cottages, and put over them soft cotton pads imbued with wine, or oil, or vinegar. And take care that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day loosen the bandage, and administer a plentiful infusion with lukewarm water. Then treat it with royal ointment, and other remedies with which wounds are treated: until it is healed.
Head 5.
 On the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs in the eyes from the internal parts of the head.
When acute catarrhs occur, fierce, persistent: and you see the eyes of that person exhausted, small, and already their sight is weakened: and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hair falls out from them, and in the depths of the head there is sharp pain, annoying, and sneezing follows: then take it from these accidents, that those materials and catarrhs do not come, unless from many veins.
And better treatment for them is this. And it is, as you should gently shave the forehead of the weak patient. Then make an incision in the middle of the forehead, or somewhat wider along the width, and begin from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision be up to the bone: and extend your hand from the temporal muscles, which move during mastication: until the bone is exposed: and you will dry up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with a cloth of old linen, or with a thread from a linen fabric. Then sprinkle above with powders: and soak the powders with wine and oil, so that a hot aposteme does not occur.
And when you dissolve it, and you see that a hot aposteme is already imminent: then it is necessary to apply cold to the bone, until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes the growth of flesh: as if you take two parts from wheat flour and four parts from rose oil: and prepare an ointment from them, and administer it to promote the growth of flesh on these wounds.
Chapter 6.
Of the treatment of what falls into a fistula.
Everything that falls into a fistula is of one of four species: either a mineral stone, or similar to a stone, such as iron and glass: or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans: or it is a fluid substance, such as water, vinegar, and their likes: or it is an animal.
When therefore a stone falls into a fistula, or a type of stone from those that do not adhere nor grow in the fistula, expose the fistula to the sun: if you see the stone, then distill into the fistula something from violet oil or iris. Then administer the extraction of it, and make it sternutate with a poultice, and apply a warm compress to the approach of the sternutation, after you place on the surrounding area of the ear a frusta made from pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear to the upper parts.
For it often exits with this treatment.
If, however, it does not exit, then administer the extraction of it with gestures, that is, with subtle finger movements: whose form is this.
If, therefore, it exits with gestures, it is good.
And if not, then administer the extraction of it with a gentle hook of subtle and slight duplication.
If, therefore, it does not exit through that, then make a cannula from copper, and insert the extremity of the cannula into the ear hole as much as possible, and apply what is around the cannula with soft and moistened ceramic cloth oiled, so that there may be no passage except through the cannula: then extract with your strong attractive force.
For it often exits with what we have described. If, however, it does not exit in this way: then take from helkalimbat, or from uisco, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, a small amount: and place it at the extremity of the radian after you have covered it with a decently positioned cot; then insert it into the ear hole with ease, after you have freed the ear from humidity.
If, therefore, it does not exit with all that we have described, then hasten to cut, before a warm aposteme or spasm occurs.
And the method of cutting is, that you make an incision on the weak part first in the head: and extract from it blood according to the measure of its strength. Then make the weak part sit between your hands, and turn its ear to the upper parts, and find a small cutting in the root of the ear near its fat at the depressed place itself: and let the cutting be a lunar figure, until you reach the stone: then lift it out with that which is possible for you from the instruments.
Then make an incision with your hand quickly, and treat it, until it heals.
What falls into the ear is from one of the grains that grow and swell; then administer its extraction.
If it does not obey you at the outlet, then take a thin, subtle spatula, according to this form.
And administer with it an excision of that type from the grains which fall into the ear, and do not make it, when you think that that grain has already been moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you apply many gentle thrusts: then extract them with hooks, or with subtle gestures, or with suction, as we have said, for its extraction is alleviated.
In the water that enters the ear, it is necessary to use a weak sternutatory with condiments at first: and already fill the ear with some old cotton: and it lies on top of that ear, in which there is water. If therefore it comes out through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many small stones, according to the length of the finger, thin, smooth, and warm them with fire for a short time, and insert one of them gently into the ear opening: and let it rest on its base on that side, and strike with another stone against the stone that is in the ear. Do not cease to do that with stone and stone, until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out thus, that you take from a small paper, or from one reed, and insert one end into the ear: and apply the other end to the fire, until it burns most of it. Then take another reed, and do that many times, until all the water comes out: and extract it with a cannula according to what has been mentioned about the stone.
In the extraction of an animal entering into it, if it is a small body, such as a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I have said in the division.
But if it is a large body, which appears to the senses: then administer its extraction with gestures and hooks: and its removal is easier than all those that are held in a golden vessel.
In the extraction of a worm generated in a golden vessel, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatment of distillations, and your treatment does not succeed: then it is necessary to gaze at the gold in the sun. If therefore something appears there from the worms, then extract it with gestures or with subtle hooks.
If however something does not appear there, then take a cannula, whose shape is this, narrow below, wide above: and insert the subtle extremity into the gold according to the quantity that the weak part can tolerate: then suck with strong suction. Do that many times, until all the worms come out.
If however they do not obey you for exit, then smear the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said in the stone.
What if they do not come out with what we have said: then administer the distillations which the ancient ones have already experienced in the treatment of worms: and you will find it in the treatment of distillations.
And let your pouring out be with which you pour oils and medications into the gold with this instrument, whose shape is depicted here. As you see make it from silver or copper, narrow below, wide above, in which there is a small hole.
Let it be so, pushing what is in the concavity of the cannula, made decently from copper. And if you wish, take a rod, and wrap the cotton around its extremity with decent wrapping: then pour oil or juice or what you wish from these medications into the cannula: and let it be in the gold: then insert the rod with the cotton above, and express it by hand pressure tempered moderately, until the oil is pushed out into the concavity of the ear, and the weak part feels it inwardly: and what is poured into the gold, let it already be warmed up with fire for a short time: and beware lest there be something that distills into it cold and worthless: for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of accidental wounds to the ear.
When sometimes infants are born from their mothers' wombs unharmed.
And it has also happened to some men that they have suffered a wound in the middle of their ear from a blow or from flesh born in it.
And this wound sometimes occurs deep within the ear's passage, which does not impair vision; and sometimes it happens at the outer passage of the ear, and then it impairs vision.
Therefore, those wounds that occur deep within the ear, which do not affect the sight, are in many cases difficult to heal.
And in those cases where vision is impaired, you should place the ear of the injured person in a sunny spot and gaze into it; then if you see an apparent wound, open it with a subtle spatula whose shape is as follows: its extremity should have a small width, one part of which is acute, and the remaining spatula should be gentle on both sides, so that it does not harm the ear.
If therefore the wound is from flesh born in the middle of the ear, then hold it with a subtle hook and cut into it with a gentle instrument, until all the flesh is removed.
If the wound is deep within the ear, then take a subtle rod, gentle and warm it with fire for a short time; then insert it into the middle of the ear.
If therefore you feel the wound with it, which the injured person has discovered with difficulty of hearing, try to open it with ease; and beware, lest you injure the nerve of the ear.
Then put on the ear a layer of lichen according to the quantity of the width of the passage, which you have wrapped in Egyptian unguent; do this for several days, until you are certain about the consolidation of the wound.
Take cotton and soak it in rose water, and sprinkle powdered zegi on top of it, and apply it to the patient.
If you see that the abscess is already apostemating, then it's necessary to extract the cotton and replace it with another piece of cotton that has been wrapped in a cloth treated with rose oil, until the warm abscess subsides; afterwards, take care of it until it heals.
If a hemorrhage of blood occurs: then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the abscess; and use the remaining treatment that is described in the chapter on bleeding, which stops the flow of blood.
On the treatment of eyelids affected by Althel (i.e., trachoma).
Chapter 8.
These are the Althel that occur on the eyelids when they are moist and become dry.
Therefore, it is necessary to hold them with a bandage: or with an unguent, and to bind them with a plaster made from their roots. Then apply powdered zegi on top of the affected areas.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a subtle lenticular cautery.
And it is better to cauterize them after making an incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
On the treatment of pustules on the eyelids.
Chapter 9.
It often happens that something occurs on the eyelids which resembles Grandines in its strength and hardness, and because of this it is called a Grandine: and it is an accumulation of thick humidity on the upper and lower eyelids.
The operation for them is therefore to consider then, if it is a Grandine that is forming on the eyelid, which appears as a lump on the eyelid, and they move towards every part, and its treatment is easy.
Find therefore above it a fissure along the width. Then scrape it away from every part with ease, until it is evacuated. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
If, however, the cutting off of it is not prepared for you, unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then it will cause harm to the weakened part.
What if the fissure is large: then bring it together with a suture: and care for it, until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For the ointment restores it, and puts flesh on it.
And if the perforation is sloping towards the interior of the ear against the inner surface, then invert the eyelid, and suspend the perforation with a hook: besides what you need an incision, and press it from every part.
What if the eyelid is lengthened by an incision, it will not harm that part.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the perforation with salty water: and care for the place with that which puts flesh on it, until the weakened part heals.
On the treatment of eyelids.
Chapter 10.
Axirnath is a thickening that forms in the upper eyelids: and it indeed often happens to children: and it weighs down their eyes, and they have a morning discharge, and cannot gaze at the light of the sun, because their tears flow forth towards them, and on account of this they are seen to lie prostrate upon their faces always, and sleep with their eyelids beneath their humid eyebrows, which have already been affected by inflammation: and indeed Axirnath appears evident to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the method of operating is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare the threads on it, and make from those threads a circle according to the quantity that the tumor contains on all sides. Then place it on top of it, and press with your fingers on all sides, so that the tumor is gathered in the middle of the circle.
Then make an incision in the middle of that humidity with a spatula-like instrument, making lateral cuts, and let there not be a larger cut than is made in deep phlebotomy.
Or it is necessary to make a complete incision, until you reach the tumor in many dispositions, for the tumor proceeds from the incision according to the form of a white, fatty crust.
So extract it with the cloth that you had placed between your fingers to the exterior; and you turn your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed; and beware, lest you touch the incision, so that you do not reach with the spatula-like instrument to the eye and harm it.
If however the tumor does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you should add a little to the incision with ease, until the tumor proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then soak the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the site, and bind it with poultices.
And there are those who apply salt, and place it in the concavity of the incision, so that what remains from that humidity may be dissolved.
Then treat it, until it is healed.
If however a warm aposteme occurs at the site, then treat it with cooling plasters, until it is healed.
Concerning the methods of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid becomes soft, whence it happens that it bulges out as it should not.
Chapter 11.
When hairs grow in the eyelids beyond their natural course under the natural hairs, and they become entrenched: then they harm the eye, and cause to occur modes of sickness, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and a white opacity, until it becomes the cause of destruction of the eye.
And Ataxmir indeed cures the eye according to four modes. Or through cauterization with fire. Or with acute medicine, according to what was mentioned in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir cures through incision and suture. Or through tubes, according to what I will say.
It is necessary that you put the head of the patient on your lap. Then turn back the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it is turned back, it is good. And if not: introduce a needle into it, where there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid, where the hairs grow: and draw the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it back with the needle.
Then find on the inner part of the eyelid beneath the added hairs with a spatula-like instrument incident from the larger lacrimal to the smaller one.
Then extract the thread, and place a small poultice under the eyelid made from cotton or cloth: then mark on the eyelid with ink, which should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be according to the size and elevation of the eyelid: because it is already diversified in humans.
For there are those who need to be cut from the eyelid a large quantity, according to the quantity which is softened by the eyelid.
Then, when you find the spatula-like [instrument] above two lines that you marked, and begin at the larger lacrimal duct up to the smaller one, and let there be a single fissure near the natural eyelashes, just like the thickness of a radius.
Then introduce a hook into one of the two angular skin flaps. Then remove the whole thing entirely. Then bring together the two lips with a suture, using a needle and thin woolen thread: and stop the bleeding, and make it adhere by what is superfluous from the threads above the eyebrows, with certain hidden things, if you want to do that: and if not, don't worry.
Then leave the suture and threads until three days or four: afterwards take care of it.
And if you want, leave the wound without a suture, and take care of it with that which dries and constricts. For the eyelid is elevated at the sign of the wound and its aggregation: but the future will be better.
The ancient Ataxmir taught this method of healing.
However, there is concern for the weak one, and it is from good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is to make a mark on the eyelid in the shape of a figure, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then elevate the eyelid with three separate hooks, or gathered together, according to this shape that follows, then cut away what is superfluous with small forceps, according to this shape, with equality of incision.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it does not conform to that: then take a needle in which there is a thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert one thread near the larger lacrimal duct, and another thread near the smaller one: and gather the threads together between your fingers with equality.
Then elevate your hand in elevation and make the elevation equal: and elevate the skin above what is marked on the scalp, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: and take care of it, until it heals.
If an abscess should occur at your incision, or your fissure: then treat that abscess with a poultice, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And when it happens to the eyelids as well, so that their hairs may be turned away.
It is necessary therefore that you use in this what we have said about the incision: but a suture is more artificial for both. On the treatment of ataxmy with canes.
And it is, that you turn away the eyelid, and separate the fissure that is within, according to what I have narrated. Then skillfully make two canes, or two thin sticks: whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and the width of them is less than the width of the spatula: and you have now incised at the extremities of both, from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then bring together each one with subtlety above what is overflowing from the eyelid of the eye, and bind the two canes from both sides with a strong stricture, and leave it for some days. For the skin will be constricted, and will become black, and will putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If therefore it is delayed, then cut into it with forceps. Then treat it, until it heals.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is elevated: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two canes, which you make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter 12.
On the elevation of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation is to receive a subtle needle and insert into it a thread from a subtle silk, fine. Then you bring together the extremities of it, and tie them with a subtle knot, so that the length of the threads is one palm.
Then you place another subtle thread beneath it in a brief space, and tie its extremities if necessary.
Then the patient places his head on your lap, and it should be near a window so that the operation is illuminated: because this is a subtle operation, or minute.
Then you insert the needle with the thread into the root of the eyelash, and make it penetrate the eyelid firmly, until you extract it above the natural hair.
Then pull out the hair up to the thread: and insert that hair into the thread: if it is one, or two, or three, no more.
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair emerges with the thread in the eyelid with the natural hair.
If the thread is pulled and the hair does not emerge with it: pull the thread downwards, with the thread that you composed in it, until the thread emerges from the superior opening, and you see it with the vision of the eye: then indeed pull the thread, and the thread: and extract both, and close the eye: and keep it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and grows over it.
If the hair is short, then associate with it a long hair from the natural hairs, and elevate it with it: or leave it, until it elongates after a space, and then elevate it.
Chapter 13.
When this laceration occurs in the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either innate or accidental.
Therefore, it is accidental, resulting from the consolidation of a wound, or from a cut, or from cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operating on it is to find that consolidation, and to separate its lips, and to put between them a linen bandage, and to tie it, until it heals.
And it is not suitable to use on its cure things that dry and constrict; for if you do that, the Alxatrati will return worse than it was. Instead, use on it things that soften, such as fenugreek: and apply with water in which there is cooked alum, flaxseed of linen; and anoint with dyapallon, which has been liquefied with some oil; and wrap with a linen bandage, and take care with it.
And the treasure of your cure is to strive with all ingenuity, that it not be consolidated, according to the form that it had at first.
And this operation which we mentioned is not anything but a certain rectification of the Alxatrati, so that it does not return to the form of the place according to what it was altogether.
Concerning the cure of the Alxatrati that occurs on the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter 13.
This Alxatrati that occurs below is what is truly called Alxatrati. And it is innate, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one occurs from a wound, or a crack, or a cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operating on it is to receive a hook, in which there is a double thread, and to insert it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left lacrimal to the right lacrimal, until the thread passes through both extremities of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with a hook, and cut it with a spatula-shaped instrument. If then the shape of the eyelid returns to what is fitting, it is good.
And if not, take a rod, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from two angles of the incision, until they converge and an angle is formed: until when they come together, this figure is formed, and it is a praiseworthy Greek letter.
Then remove that flesh in proportion to what is the sharp edge below it, from what follows the eyelid. Then bring together the separated parts: sew them with a thread of wool.
Then treat them with what we have said, with softening medications and bandages, until they heal.
And if a scar forms from the fissure and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary to make a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, also according to what has preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a bandage, according to what we have said.
And the summary of the sermon on the treatment of Alxatrati, when it is above or below, is that one should proceed with the operation according to what is prepared in the form of Alxatrati.
This indeed is of many diverse forms: and the skilled craftsman prepares his ingenuity with whatever method is possible for him, until he makes it return to its natural form, or near it, and himself, infirm, according to what is convenient for him from the operation and instruments in many arrangements.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the eye with conjunctiva or cornea.
When this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid becomes consolidated with the whiteness of the eye, and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore, you should introduce the edge of the scalpel under the eyelid and elevate it upwards; or extend it with a hook. Then make an incision with a fine spatula, which is not acute at the point of the incision, but rather obtuse for a short while: so that the weak patient may not be disturbed during the operation, and the spatula may be removed, and the eye may be freed from its acuteness. And let your incision be as if you were scraping off that adhesion with ease, until the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salty water into the eye, washing it with water; or distill into it a concoction made from zinc oxide dissolved in water.
Then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen thread; and place on the eye a wool soaked in egg white: and after three days use zinc oxide, and with solids, until it is healed.
Concerning the Incision of the Nail-like Projection and Elevation of Flesh around the Lacrimal Glands.
Chapter 16.
You should know that the nail-like projection is to be treated in two ways. Either it is nerve-based: and it is similar to a hard, fine callus. Or it is not nerve-based, similar to a congealed white humidity: which when touched with iron, or attempted to be grasped with a hook, becomes detached, and the hook does not stick into it.
And both types do not begin unless from the larger lacrimal gland, until it grows gradually, until it covers the sight and prohibits light, and disturbs the motion of the eye.
And the method of operation for its incision is: that the patient should place their head on your lap. Then open their eyes, and elevate the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a few teeth of a scalpel, and extend it to the upper parts. Then take a needle and insert the horsehair thread through it, or that of a goat: and let the thread be strong, and double back the extremity of the instrument, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and penetrate it with the instrument, and tie it with a thread to the nail, and extend it to the upper parts: and clear away with the horsehair thread the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you were to sew it with the thread up to the posterior part of the eye.
Then cut the remainder in the root of the tear duct with a subtle spatula, or with small forceps: and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct, so that the flow of tears is not impeded by the incision.
And when the nail is extended with a scalpel alone: or with a thread, as we have said. Then it is scraped out with a gentle spatula, which has this shape.
And beware, lest the spatula touches the corneal tunic, and a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is perforated.
Then after the incision, apply to the eye some ground salt, or made from zinc, and leave it until another day. Then treat it with what is necessary, until it is healed.
What if the nail is not nerveless, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is it fixed with a hook, then this type of nail is often treated with medications, and healed as I described in the division.
What if you want to cut it, then open the eye of the weak one, and take a subtle, gentle, and slightly sharp spatula, according to this shape. And scrape the nail from above with a subtle scraping. If you see that it is rarefied and congealed, and an impression is made on it with a red or yellow or salty poultice, then distill it from hour to hour in the eye, whether made from sifted [powder] or rubied, or trito salt: and apply it to the eye until another day.
Then repeat the same operation over it, until everything is removed from it. Unless there is a hot aposteme in your eye: then indeed leave it alone and treat the aposteme until it heals.
Then repeat the operation over it with a poultice, until it heals.
The prominence of the tear-producing flesh, if it hurts in the eye with great annoyance, should be suspended with a hook, and a portion of it should be cut out: and not a final incision, lest the flow of tears be disturbed.
Then apply to the tear-producing [area] a red poultice, or one made from sifted [powder], until it heals.
On the Incision of Al-Guardenic and Flesh Born in the Eye.
Chapter 17.
A red fleshy growth is born in certain eyes of men, composed or superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches, or presses on the eyelids, and perhaps turns them outward: and it resembles a rose-like pimple.
And the operation for this is also such that he places his weakened head on your lap. Then open his eyes, and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks for that purpose: and hold it with one forceps, or a handle.
Then cut the flesh first, until everything is finished with an incision. And be careful not to hurt the eye during the operation: and let your incision be such that you cut with it, or with a spatula, which we described in the incision of the nail: or with small forceps, with which one gathers the asperity, according to what its form will be after this chapter.
When your incision is complete and the entire algardi nerve is removed, then fill the eye with a contrary salty powder; or distill into it a reddish powder, and similar things from corroding medicines; and place over the eye a cotton pad soaked in cooling albumen: so that it may be secure from a hot aposteme.
If something remains from the algardi nerve and a hot bloody flux dominates you, and a hot aposteme threatens: then abandon the eye, and take care of it with what soothes the aposteme.
Then repeat over it with the operation, until it is healed.
And do similarly with the flesh that has grown in the eye from this same kind.
However, it is necessary to avoid strange cures for all weak eyes, either from natural weakness or accidental injury, operating with iron: and do not introduce your hand into any of these operations, until you see that the member is tolerant of that operation.
On collecting pus from the eye.
Chapter 18.
Pus is a reddish vein that forms on the eye, and prevents the power of vision from operating: and weakens the eye over time.
It is necessary for you first to consider whether the eye in which there is pus is strong and has no other ailment except the pus: then collect its pus.
And it is, that you should advise the weak patient to put his head on your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your prudence: and let the hook be subtle and doubled, according to this form, or let there be two graduated hooks on one body, according to this form.
Then collect with subtle forceps those veins subtly, and drain the blood hour after hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye, and are resolved: and beware not to harm the eye with excessive spatula-like extensions.
And let your operation be in the morning, before the sun is high, and you should be firm in your operation always: so that you do not cut unless it is those veins.
And when you have made an incision, then pour into the eye a mixture of green or red: so that its sharpness corrodes what remains from the pus.
If however it is not possible for you to gather all of that at that hour, then apply a plaster to the eye with what soothes the pain of the eye: and leave it there for some days, until the pain subsides: and protect it from a warm aposteme; then treat it again in the same way, until it is healed.
And this is the method of forehead operations.
On the reduction of a nose fracture.
Chapter 19.
The doctor calls a nose fracture an Arissat, which is treated with cauterization or with sharp, burning medicine, according to what was previously mentioned in his account.
And if it does not heal, then there is no cleverness in that: unless you make an incision over the aposteme at its birthplace, and extract all the moisture which is in it and pus, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see in it decay or blackness, then scrape it with an instrument, whose shape is this.
And it is called a scalp scraper.
Let it be made from Indian iron, and its head should be round like a file, which is marked with the marking of a lime file or the subtle marking of an alkilferi file: and put it on the corrupted bone area.
Then turn it around between your fingers, and you press your hand firmly for a while, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the area with drying, astringent medicines.
If therefore the area consolidates, and flesh is born in it, and the flow of blood is cut off: and it remains for forty days, and does not break open again, and does not become inflamed, and no accident occurs in it: then know that it has already been healed.
And if not, then there is no ingenuity in it, unless one reduces the fistula to the nostril opening, according to this form.
And it is thus, that you detach from the bone with an iron instrument, or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone is detached, then take the trephine according to this form, let it be an iron extremity, triangular: and its wood should be a pyramid extended to the extremity, as you see.
Then place that on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and extend your hand a little from the eye. Then turn your hand with the trephine until it penetrates the bone, and let the patient feel that; through this, he finds a way to exhale out of it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with drying, astringent medicines, as I instructed you.
When therefore flesh grows, and the place hardens: then the matter that was poured out to the outside returns to the nose: it is therefore lighter on the patient.
Of the Reduction of the Prominence of the Eye.
Chapter 20.
When the eye bulges out with its entirety, and no harm occurs in the sight, nor diminution: then it is necessary to soothe the patient: then let blood be taken from him: afterwards, apply cupping glasses on the nape of the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place on the eye an ointment made from acacia, and aloe, and olive oil, and sarcocolle. Then bind the eye above with a plaster containing many powders.
And the method of powders is: that you take soft cloths, many, and each cloth is doubled over four times; make many from them: then place one on top of another, according to the quantity which the eye needs: then bind them together with a strong, tight wrapping.
And he should make gentle suction without scratching.
Then apply gentle suction, and loosen the constricted eye by day and night.
If, however, the ligament is again softened, then it is necessary that it be tightened much: then loosen the ligament by day and night, as we have said.
If, therefore, you see that the eye has already returned to its own tissue, it is good: and if not, then apply a plaster, and poultices, and a constricting bandage, and suction, until it is healed.
On the Incision of the Tunicles.
Chapter 21.
When a disruption occurs in the tunic of the Vein, then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like a grain of the Vein, and its shape is deformed on account of that, and it is necessary to incise it: then the operation is, according to what I relate: and it is, as if you insert a needle at the root of the Vein from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle where there is a double thread on the part of the lacrimal, and make it penetrate: and loosen the first needle according to its own arrangement.
Then incise the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the Vein around the needle: and tighten it much.
Then extract the needle, and place on the eye a soaked cotton pad on the white of the eye: and loosen it, until the thread falls with the Vein.
Then treat the eye with that which comforts it, until the wound is healed.
And when the Vein is tightened from all sides with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then it should be pierced with a subtle spatula, which reaches to the depth of the eye, and the moisture of the white is drained, and the eye subsides, and it immediately enters: then tighten it, until it is healed.
On the Treatment of Ulcers.
Chapter 22.
This disease which is called Alcomnati is not, unless it be a humor that accumulates in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is, that you should make the weakened part sit upon a prepared or directed seat.
Then take his head with your hands from both sides, and move it until you feel the humor passing to the lower parts of his eyes. Then it is treated, and does not descend, and the light is freed: and the weakened part appears as it appeared: and if it does not descend below, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the humor is not prepared with what we have said, then make the weakened part sit between your hands. Then take a subtle spatula, and find with it above the cornual pannicle for a short time at the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the crown, until the humor exits.
When it exits, then pour warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then it should be treated with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
On the Treatment of Water that Descends into the Eye, or Cataracts.
Chapter 23.
We have already discussed the types of water in the division, and what is suitable for piercing with a declaration and exposition of the sermon; then take that same thing according to health.
Then therefore it is necessary to make the weakened part sit between your hands, squared, opposite the light, near the sun; and bind the healthy eye, and hold it firmly; then touch the eyelid of his eye with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then take with your right hand Almagda, if the eye is left; or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
Then place the Almagda next to the crown by the thickness of the radius in the same whiteness of the eye's apparent smaller lachrymal canal: then push the Almagda with force: and you will revolve it with your hand, until it penetrates into the whiteness of the eye: and you will feel that the Almagda has now reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the measurement of the journey of the Almagda be to the depth, the measurement of the space that is from the pupil up to the end of the blackness: and this is the crown of the eye. You will see them in the same pupil vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the corneal tunic.
Then put the Almagda up to the place where there is water. Then press it downwards with one vein after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the weak one will see that above what has opened its vein immediately, and the Almagda in its eye.
Then rest a little while. If therefore the water descends, then put it down a second time, except that the Almagda goes out.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend: then extract the Almagda with ease: and you will revolve your hand little by little.
Then dissolve something in water from the clear salt of a gem: and wash the eye within with it. Then put outside the entire eye a cotton pad, or a wool infused with rose oil, and of the whiteness of the eye: and bind it with the healthy eye.
We, however, and those who are of our time, put on top a triturated cumin with the whiteness of the eye.
What if the alumga (eye salve) does not obey you at its entrance into the eye because of its hardness, since it is from a man whose eye is hard and strong: then it is necessary to receive the spatula, which is called Alberid, whose shape is this.
You should therefore join it (the spatula) to the conjunction itself, and not put your hand ultimately in the orifice; and it is only that you may make a way to the alumga place subtly.
Then introduce the alumga, according to what we have said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare a comfortable dormitory for the patient, well-made and situated in a dark room, where they can sleep on their back; and prohibit them from all movements and coughing; and place their food, that which soothes their nature; and let them not move their head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its own arrangement until the third day.
Then release him in that dark room, and experience his strength: and make him see things clearly. Then reduce the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting to do that at the time of curing, or immediately after the incision; on the contrary, it is necessary to delay it: because water ascends quickly through a violent aspect.
But if a hot aposteme occurs: then it is necessary to release the eye before the seventh day; and correct it with what soothes the aposteme until it is soothed: then restore his sight completely, and place an oral application on his face which regulates his sight under those days, he himself being in that dark room.
Then let him gradually go out of the house, and take care of himself at home.
And know that in this matter the student is not exempt from seeing many times: then indeed he will rely on the operation.
And now it reaches us from one of those who are from Alayrach, who said that a perforated wound was made in Alayrach, which is treated with water.
Therefore I did not see anyone in our land who had done that: nor did I read about it in any of the ancient books; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of surgical treatments for wounds, so that they may stand above those who are ignorant of them.
They should be made from copper properly: and their extremities should be with the same subtlety triangular, and the extremities acute.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of a flesh-born growth in the nose.
When various fleshy growths are born in the nose, among which there is something that resembles a scorpion with many feet; and from these is the cancerous flesh, stony, of dark color; and from these is the soft flesh, not of dark color.
In this case, therefore, where the soft flesh from these growths is not deceitful, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you place the weakened patient between your hands opposing the ground, and open their nostrils, and insert a hook into those growths: then draw them outwards, and incise what you have comprehended from them with a subtle, acute spatula on one side, until you know that the entire flesh has now been removed.
If something remains of it from which the excision is not possible: then scrape that off with one of the subtle instruments with ease, until nothing remains of it. If, however, the blood dominates you, or a hot aposteme occurs: then oppose it with what is necessary: or if it is one of the malignant apostemes, then cauterize it promptly, until the blood is separated and all the growths are removed.
Then there is poured into the nose after the incision vinegar and water, or wine. If, however, the nose is opened and humidity proceeds from it to the palate, then you will know that he is already healthy.
If, however, the humidity does not penetrate, according to what is fitting, then you will know that there is a carnal birth within the upper nasal bones, which did not come forth with the incision of the instrument.
Then therefore it is fitting that you introduce a thread of linen with some thickness, and knot many knots on it: and place between each knot the quantity of a finger or less: and the weak one will ingeniously send one end of the thread into the nose with a rod, or as much as possible after he has made it similar to a silver rod: and draw out its odor until it reaches the guttural region, and emerges above his throat.
And many times indeed, boys in schools make something similar to this, Atomi's device; and it is an easy thing for him who wants it.
Then place two ends of the thread, one end that emerges above the nose, and another that remains in the nose. Then the minister is shut off from the flesh with knots which are on the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is already gathered with the knots of the thread.
Then extract the thread: and put into the nose after the blood has been purged, a licinium that you have steeped in Egyptian unguent; do this for three days or more, until the unguent corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then put into the nose at the end of the matter a tube of lead for some days, until it is healed.
If however it needs drying treatment, use that.
For making a head-cleansing device, with which oils are distilled, and other medicines into the nose, make it from silver or copper: something like a small lamp, open like a shell: and its passage similarly.
And if you want to make a closed canal, like a reed.
And you should make an instrument for cleansing the head, full and detected: which has a handle at its end, with which you hold it, when you heat it in oil or what you want to extract from juices, and fluid matters.
Chapter 25.
A polyp often grows at the tip of the nose, and it gets bigger and increases with time, until its foul appearance becomes evident.
And because of that, it is necessary to remove it at the beginning of its appearance, and to eradicate it entirely. Then apply to the place either a cautery or a burning medicine that stands in its stead.
If therefore the cutting of it is delayed, until it grows, then consider if it is stony, hard, dark-colored, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron: because it is a cancerous aposteme.
For I have often seen those who cut into an aposteme, and great evil befell them because of it to the one who had it.
And if the aposteme is of soft touch, not dark-colored, and you see that cutting into it entirely is possible, then evacuate the weak part, and cut into it without fear, without trembling: and treat the place with what draws out and constricts, until it heals.
Chapter 26.
On the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
You should know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, it hardly helps in the operation, unless in certain men.
Therefore, when something happens to someone from that, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood: and then apply double-layered bandages to the wound with a suture: then treat it, until it heals.
And if the lips of a continuous wound are already separated and each part is healthy, then it is necessary to remove every part from its own apparent cut, until it bleeds. Then bring the two lips together with a suture, and tie them, and sprinkle dragon's blood powder on top of it, and triturated olibanum; and place a fragment of diafinicon emplaster above the powders, and another consolidating emplaster; and leave it tied for two days, or three. Then dissolve it, and change the medicine; and leave it, until the threads separate by themselves. Then treat it with an ointment, until it heals.
The method of suturing is to bring together a continuous wound, or with thread, as we narrated in the suturing of the belly; or bring together the wound with a thread, as I made you know.
Chapter 27.
On the extraction of lip tumors.
Many people indeed suffer from hard, grain-like swellings on the inside of their lips, similar to knots or seeds; and some of them are smaller, and others larger.
Therefore it is necessary to invert the lip, and place a thread above each tumor, and tie it with a hook, and cut it away from every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with tragacanth powder, until the blood stops.
Then it is washed with vinegar, and the place is treated with it, where there is a sting, until the wounds heal.
Chapter 28.
On the incision of flesh born on the gum.
A flesh growth often appears above the gum, which the ancients call "abuluss".
Therefore it is necessary to tie it with a hook; or grasp it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the blood flow, or the serum.
Then put on top of the affected area a ground powder, or other powdered styptics, evacuating.
What if the flesh returns after the cure, for it often returns, then cut into it a second time and cauterize it: for it will not return after the cauterization.
Chapter 29.
On the scraping of teeth with iron.
When they [tartar etc.] accumulate on the surface of teeth from the inner and outer part, and between the gums and rough, bitter cortices, and when they become black, and turn yellowish, and greenish, until corruption reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul through that.
It is necessary therefore that you make the weak person sit between your hands, and their head in your lap, and scrape gently with a file and a tooth, where you see the rough cortices, and similar to sand: until nothing remains of them, and similarly do for blackness, and greenishness, and yellowness, and others until they are cleaned.
If therefore what is in them from the beginning of scraping is good: and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another day, a second time, and a third time, until finally the desired goal is reached.
And know that a tooth needs various forms of scrapings, many figures: according to what your operation is prepared for: therefore, the scraper with which a tooth is scraped from below is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And let these forms of scrapers be prepared by you, which you will need.
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of pain from teeth.
It is necessary that you cure the tooth of its pain with all ingenuity, and be slow in its eradication. For it is not that the juice of the tooth is replenished when it is eradicated: because it is a sublime substance: until when there is no excuse for its eradication, then it is necessary to eradicate it when it is considered weak, so that it may be strengthened and certified by you as a painful tooth.
For often indeed deceives the weak pain, and it seems that it is in the tooth's root and eradicates it; then the pain does not recede until the rotten tooth is eradicated.
For we have already seen that from the operation of bloodletting.
When therefore the aching tooth itself is certified to you, then it is necessary to scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is some virtue, until the gum is loosened on every side; then move it with your finger or with subtle forceps, first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place the large forceps in a good position on it, and hold the head of the rotten tooth between your knees: what you have held well, so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight out, so as not to break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it beneath it on every side with ease, and try its movement, as you did at first.
If the tooth is perforated or corroded: then it is necessary to fill that hole with cotton; and tie it with a subtle radian extremity, so as not to be broken in the hour of your constriction on it with forceps.
And it is necessary to thoroughly clean with scraping around the gum on every side many times.
And preserve your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns to the rotten tooth from that greater evil which is in its first pain.
And beware, lest you do what ignorant bloodletting practitioners do in their haste, and the boldness of theirs in eradicating it, beyond what we have described.
For often they cause great infirmities to happen to men, which are more difficult to break, and all their roots remain, or some of them; or so that they extract it with certain bones of the jaw: as we have often testified to that.
Then it is washed out after the excision with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
What if a flow of blood occurs from the places (for it often happens), then scrape off something from the zegi, and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if it does not conform to the zegi.
The forms of subtle forceps which are moved inward at the beginning should be long in the extremities, brief in the handle, thick so that they are not doubled when pressed with them on a tooth: they should be like you see thick handles, until when you squeeze with them, they do not slip, nor are doubled: brief in the extremity and made of iron or forged from a straight center, direct extremities: and in their extremities there should be teeth which some insert into others and constrict with decent constriction, strongly.
And sometimes they are made in the form of a bird's beak, which is called a ciconia, and they are strong in constriction.
Chapter 31.
On the eradication of teeth and the extraction of jawbone fragments.
When something remains in the eradication of a tooth which is broken, then it is necessary to put a cotton pad on the spot with butter for one day or two days, until the place softens: then introduce an instrument, or forceps: whose extremities are assimilated to a bird's beak, which is called a ciconia. The form of this is that of forceps.
Their extremities are made like a file inside, or like an aleuskilfegi.
If therefore it does not obey you in the exit with these forceps, then it is necessary to hollow out above the root, and to detach the entire flesh, and to introduce an instrument which is called a small palate, whose form is this.
It should be brief in its extremity, somewhat thick: and it should not be soaked, so that it does not break.
If therefore the root comes out through that, it is good; and if not, it is necessary to help with these instruments, others of which have the same forms.
The form of the first triangular extremity, in which there is a certain roughness.
The form of the triangular extremity, subtle.
And with other instruments and iron tools which preceded in the filing of teeth.
It is also a help with this instrument that is similar to a large hook, of this form, a triangular twisted extremity, in which there is a certain roughness, so that it does not break; and let it not be soaked.
And know that there are many instruments for teeth; and likewise the remaining instruments are not counted, and the skilled craftsman, subtle in his art, finds by himself the instrument, according to what the operation and the very difficulties signify to him.
For they are from the troubles which are for those things that the ancients did not speak of as an instrument, on account of the diversity of their forms.
But if the bone breaks from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or putrefies: then probe deeply into its place with what is suitable to it from the instruments and forceps that I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with gestures, whose form is this.
Let there be in it a certain roughness gradually, so that it may be grasped or held with the bone, and not escape and slip away, until the bone emerges; and restore the place with suitable medicines for that purpose.
But if there is a small decay in the bone, then scrape it of its decay, and cure it, until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the serration of prominent teeth above others.
When teeth do not follow their natural course, they become deformed in that shape; and especially when it happens to women and girls, namely those who are weak.
It is therefore necessary to consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and does not occupy its place, or if it is possible for its serration or polishing: then extract it; and if it is connected with another tooth, then cut it with that instrument, whose shape is this.
It is similar to a small file, made from iron, with a sharp point, strongly fixed.
And let your cutting be such that you cut it on many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, so that another tooth is not disturbed.
If, however, it is prominent and exposed to its polishing: then polish it with a file made of iron, whose shape is this.
It is entirely made of iron, and its handle has many small punctures, just like a file with which awls are made: with which the tooth is polished gradually on many days with ease, so that the tooth is not disturbed and falls out: then smooth it, finally scrape it with certain rasps.
Since if it is a tooth from which a part breaks off and hurts the tongue: then it is necessary to polish it also, until the roughness of that broken part is removed, and it is made smooth: and it is flattened, and does not hurt the tongue, nor corrupts speech.
Chapter 33.
On the retention of teeth with golden wires.
When the front teeth receive a concussion and commotion from a blow or fall: and the weak cannot hold onto what they chew so that it does not fall out: and you treated them with styptic medicines, and the treatment did not help: then the idea is to fasten them with a golden wire: because silver turns greenish and decays after days; but gold never changes.
And let the middle thread be subtle and thick according to the quantity that it takes between the teeth.
And the form of retention is such that you take a thread and pass its double between two healthy teeth: then you weave with both extremities of the thread between the moving teeth, whether there is one or several, until you reach the fabric up to the healthy tooth of the other side. Then repeat the weaving until you reach the part from which you started: and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom, so that it is not dislodged forthwith: and let your constriction be where you tighten the thread, at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not come loose.
Then cut the superfluous extremities of the thread with scissors: and gather both, and turn them with gestures: and hide them between the healthy tooth and the moving tooth, so that they do not harm the tongue: then let it be tightened thus until it remains.
If however it becomes loosened or is cut, tighten them with another thread, and let it stand firm with that for the entire time.
And this is the form of Teeth, and the figure of retention of two healthy teeth, and two moving teeth, as you see.
And when it happens that one tooth or two return to their place after a fall or other accident, and are retained as we have described, and remain: and no one does this unless he is a skilled craftsman, prudent, and experienced.
And when something is prepared from the bones of small animals: and it is made into the form of a tooth, and placed in the location, where it is strengthened and tightened, as we have said: and it remains, and is preserved with it for a long time.
Chapter 34.
When the ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which man is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already consolidated.
And the operation is to open the weak bone in its head in your sinus, and lift up its tongue; then cut through that nerve ligament along its width, until the tongue is freed from its retention.
