If there is something hard and knotted in it, and it comes from the consolidation of a wound, then push in that hook, and feel it along its width, until you cut through the ligament, and the knot is freed.
And beware, lest there be a fissure in the deep flesh, and you cut through an artery there, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then let it be washed with rose water in the wound's incision, or with vinegar, or cold water.
Then put under the tongue a linen pledget, which holds the weak part during every night: so that it does not consolidate again.
If a flow of blood occurs, then put crushed zinc on the spot. If the blood overflows you, then cauterize the spot with a gentle cautery, which is suitable for it; then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
When an abscess occurs under the tongue that resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural operation: and perhaps it grows so much that it fills the mouth.
And the operation for it is to open the weak bone before you, and examine the abscess.
If you see that it is of a dark or black color, hard, and has no weak sensation: then do not touch it, because it is a cancer.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then push into it a hook, and cut it with a subtle spatula, and free it from every part. If blood flows out during your operation, then apply a compress of crushed ice on it until the bleeding stops; afterwards return to your operation until you extract it with its surrounding tissue.
Then let it be washed out with vinegar and salt; and take care to treat the rest of the wound in a suitable manner, until it heals.
On the treatment of two abscesses of the gums.
Chapter 36.
When two lumps occur in the throat, that is, glands, which resemble almonds, which occur before: and they are called two Tonsils: which when you treat with what we said earlier in the division, and do not heal, then consider.
If it is a hard, dry abscess, of a foul color, with little sensitivity: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of a red color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it with iron either, fearing a flow of blood, or rather leave it alone until it ripens. And then you will either pierce it: or it will burst open by itself.
And if it is of a white color, round, and its root is subtle: then it is necessary to cut into it.
And the operation on it is, that you should consider beforehand your own operation, if the abscess is warm and quiet, or if it is diminished by some reduction: then thus make the patient sit comfortably in front of a sunbeam, and his head should be in your lap: and open his mouth, and receive carefully between your hands, and press his tongue down below with an instrument, whose shape is this.
Let it be made from silver, or copper, which should be as subtle as a scalpel.
When you press with it the tongue, and the abscess is shown to you, and your gaze falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and extract it as much as possible outward, except that you draw out something from the tunics with it.
Then cut into it with an instrument, whose shape is as follows. It is made similar to forceps: however the extremities of these are curved, so that the opening of each one is directed towards the other acute angle: therefore take care that it be made from iron or tempered steel.
If this instrument is not available, then cut into it with a spatula, whose shape is as follows. It is sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have cut into one tonsil, cut into the other according to the same pattern of cutting.
Then afterwards gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If a flow of blood occurs, gargle with water in which the bark of granate trees has been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and similar things from astringent remedies, until the flow of blood stops: then take care of it, until it heals.
And sometimes other abscesses will form in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Cut them therefore equally according to what I said about the cutting of the two tonsils.
And I have indeed already treated a woman who had an abscess, which was born in her throat, which was pulling her towards phlegmon, with little sense, which had almost closed off her throat: and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and it had already prohibited her from eating, and drinking water: and she had already come close to death, if it had remained for one day or two days.
And the abscess had only risen so far that it had sent out two branches, until they issued forth through two openings of the nose.
I seized them quickly and fixed one of them with a hook. Then I pulled it out, and a large portion was drawn out with it.
Then I cut it off, where I grasped it through the nostrils.
Then I did to it what was protruding from the nostril of the other one.
Then I opened its bone, and pressed its tongue: and afterwards I fixed a hook into the aposteme itself: then I cut off from it a part of it, and hardly any blood flowed from it: thus the throat of the woman was freed, and she began to drink water immediately, and then took some food.
I did not cease to cut into that aposteme many times over a long period: and the aposteme succeeded in taking the place of what I was cutting, until it was prolonged excessively and with it.
I then applied heat, and cauterized the aposteme in the throat.
It stood firm therefore from the application: then I walked away from there, and did not know what God did with it afterwards.
On the Incision of a Aposteme on the Uvula.
Chapter 37.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula and becomes an aposteme, and it is long: then it is called perpendicular: and if it is in the lower part thick and round, then it is called a uuva.
When it is treated with what we said in the division, and the treatment does not help: and you see that the hot aposteme quiets down from it, and it is subtle: then it is necessary to cut into it.
And what is gathered from it is round, and it has no length, and it is bloody, or of a dark color, or black, and it has no sensation: then it is necessary to make an incision in it. For there is fear about the patient's condition.
It is necessary therefore, when you see it according to the method which I described, and its length, to make the patient sit in front of a sunbeam: and press his tongue with an instrument, whose description preceded: then fix a hook into the uuva, and draw it downwards: and cut into it with one of the two instruments which I described in the incision of the two amigdale.
And it is necessary that you do not cut except from that which is added above the natural termination without addition.
For if you cut more than that, you will cause pain and inflammation.
Then after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of the two almond-shaped [parts], and care for it until it heals.
If, however, the patient is weakened by the incision, then it is necessary to use caution in cauterizing it without fear and trembling.
And the method of cauterization in this case is not with sharp water, but rather you should place the patient's head on your lap. Then press their tongue with the instrument that we mentioned. Then take the water that I mentioned to you in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with a non-extinguished lime, and place it not too thick, nor too subtle, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, whose shape is this.
Let the extremities of it be where the medicine is placed, concave like the concavity of a spoon's handle.
And place the instrument with the medicine on the same ulcer: and let the patient lie on their side, so that saliva flows out of their mouth from the inwardly administered medicine, so that nothing descends from it into their throat, and hurts them.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you should apply it to the ulcer in a moderate quantity for a half hour, until you see it already blackened and calmed down by the biting of the medicine.
And if you wish to receive the crust, and wrap it around the extremity of the handle, and pour the crust with medicine, and insert the handle with the crust into a tube from above, until the crust adheres to the ulcer.
Do that many times, until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of your ulcer. Then leave it: it will wither and fall off after three days, or four.
What if you need to administer medicine, administer it thus: and after cauterization, wipe the area around the wound with a cloth soaked in butter, and clean it with what is around it from the medicine; and cool it with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with poultices, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This therefore is a more healthy and longer-lasting method of treatment, free from fear.
And sometimes it is treated with what is more subtle than cauterization and incision: for it is treated with fumigation, according to this method. Take calament, and hyssop, and origan, and rue, and sseah, and chamomile, and abrotanum, and similar herbs: and gather all of them together, or some of them in a pot, and soak them in vinegar, and boil: and let the pot be well-covered with clay: and let there be an opening in the middle of the pot, over which an instrument concave according to this method is placed.
Let it be made of silver, or of copper: and let the extremities be introduced, in which there is a grain, into the mouth of the patient, until the vapor rises to the wound through a canal, until the wound is greatly obscured.
Then repeat it many times over it, until it withers.
And beware, lest you perform this treatment at the beginning of an acute abscess. For it increases in the abscess: and it is not proper to do that unless at the decline of the abscess's heat.
If you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a reed, and place a layer of sheep's cortex on its extremity: so that the mouth of the patient is not burned: for the cortex of the sheep prevents the vapor from burning the mouth: and this is good treatment with safety.
On the extraction of spinal marrow and bones from the throat.
Chapter 38.
Many times a bone sticks in the gullet, or a fishbone, or something else.
It is necessary therefore to extract what appears above what has fallen by the sense of sight, after you press the tongue with an instrument at the root, and it is revealed to you what is in the gullet.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the gullet, it is necessary that you make a weak vomit before food is digested in the stomach. Perhaps indeed the attached thing will come out through vomiting.
Or swallow a weak piece of turnip, or the root of lettuce, or swallow a small piece of dry bread: or take a piece of dried seaweed sponge and tie it with thread, then swallow it. When therefore it reaches the place where the bones or the bone are, and it is extracted, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have said: then use an instrument made of lead according to this form.
Let it be thicker than a radius of a finger, and at its extremity let there be a curvature, which easily introduces itself into the throat, and let it have its head elevated upwards.
And let it be cautious of the touch of its epiglottis, so that a cough does not occur to it, and it drives out with it the bone, or spine, or the physician introduces it with their hand.
Indeed what the weak person introduces is better: on account of their knowledge of the place of the attached thing: and they drive downwards, or pull with their hand and instrument upwards, all that according to the quantity which is prepared for them, until it comes out.
On the extraction of leeches.
Chapter 39.
When a leech is treated with what I said earlier in the section on cures with medicines, and it does not succeed, then examine the gullet of the weak person in front of the sun, or at the root after you press their tongue with the instrument that I described to you.
If then your sight falls on the blood vessel, extract it with a small hook, or with delicate instruments that are well-divided.
What if it is not possible with these: then take a curved cannula, and insert it into the throat of the injured person up to the throat; then insert into the curve of the cannula a fiery iron heated with fire. Do that many times: and let the injured person abstain from water for the whole day.
Then let him receive a full treatment of cold water, and open his mouth in it: and let him gargle with it, and not swallow anything from it: and let him move the water for one hour after another with his hand. For the blood vessel falls down at that place when it feels the water.
If it does not come out with what we have narrated, then fumigate the injured person with bug-like things, or with a reed with an instrument that I have narrated in the fumigation of wounds.
Do that many times, until it falls out.
And the method of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot, in which are live coals with fire: and let the pot be covered with a lid, in the middle of which is a hole: and let the extremity of the instrument be composed in that hole.
Then pour out the fumigation, and let the injured person put his mouth at the extremity of the cannula, and close his mouth, so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has already reached the blood vessel. For the blood vessel will immediately fall out.
What if it does not fall out, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the injured person endure hunger, and eat salty things, and garlic, and not drink water.
It is necessary therefore that it comes out with this regimen.
And this is the shape of the instrument with which a bloodsucker is extracted from the throat, when it falls upon its own force.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is introduced into the throat. And its extremity is similar to the beak of a bird called a stork, in which there is roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not release it at all.
On the opening of abscesses, and their section, and care in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many types of abscesses, of many modes, according to what comes to mind about them, namely one, and one in this book: and they differ in their perforation and sectioning from two modes.
Of which one is of the type of abscess that is in itself, and that which comprehends from humidities.
And the second mode is on account of places, in which it happens through the body.
Because an abscess occurring on the head is different from an abscess occurring on the anus: and an abscess occurring in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in a joint: and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And among the abscesses there are those which ought not to be perforated, unless after maturation of the pus in them, and its completion: and among them are those which ought to be perforated, while they are raw, and do not mature according to their completion.
Like abscesses that are near the joints.
Because an abscess, when it occurs near a joint and prolongs its being, until the putrid matter that is around it perhaps corrupts the ligaments or nerves of that joint; therefore it is a cause of more proximate contraction of that limb.
Or it is an abscess near a principal member: because if you delay the perforation of it until it matures, it will harm either that member or through that principal member.
It is near the anus, and therefore it is not mature: and it is not suitable, as if you make perforations, a crude apostema, which does not complete digestion, unless it is near the anus, so that it does not putrefy deeply, and extends to the interior of the bowel, and becomes a fistula, or becomes in its termination what is not healthy.
It is suitable that you know the time of perforation of the apostema, which has matured according to its complement: and it is at the sedation of pain, recession of fever, and diminution of redness, and pulsation, and acuteness of the head of the apostema: and the remaining signs.
And it is suitable that you make the perforation in the inferior place of the apostema, and of the more prominent elevation: and let the perforation be made along the length of the body, if the apostemata are in the parts of the hands and feet, and in the places of the muscles, and tendons, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflex.
In those however that are reflected, let the perforation be made according to that membrane.
When indeed the apostema is in fleshy places: then it is better to delay its perforation, until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to its complement.
Because if you perforate it before that, the course of the virus is prolonged from it, and there are many filth and foulness, and perhaps its lips will harden, and its depth.
And some apostemata are perforated according to the width of the body, according to necessity: or according to that membrane which it needs.
And it is suitable that you administer in small apostemata a small perforation, and in large apostemata an ample perforation: or many sections according to the similarity of the magnitude of the apostema.
And there are those from apostemata, in which it is suitable that the skin be sewn together: and an incision should be made when it has already been made like a cloth, and has been made at the termination, which is now dead: just as it happens in many cases, such as ulcers, and buboes.
And there are those which are cut with a section having three angles. And one of them is that which is incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf figure.
Like an inguinal abscess.
And there are those in which a round section is administered, and a lunar section; and similar to them from these sections.
That one indeed which has no head, such as are superficial lenient abscesses, it is fitting to pierce it with a superficial perforation, or merely a flat one.
And it is fitting, when the abscess is large, and has already gathered much matter, and you pierce it, so that you do not press and extract all the pus at that hour; rather, extract from it a portion of its own. Then apply a poultice to the abscess until another day. Then extract some of the pus again.
Do that many times according to the gradation, until it goes out; especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or a decrepit old person: for the animal spirit is often resolved with the exit of sanies; and perhaps the weak person will die, and you do not perceive.
Therefore, this chapter requires great caution.
And after your perforation, by which you pierce the abscesses, it is fitting to cleanse the wound; and consider. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or it is a flat section, then administer a dressing made of linen, or an old cloth. And if it is a large abscess, and there are many perforations of the fissure, then it is fitting to introduce a dressing into every fissure, until they reach the bottom.
And if you have cut away a part of the skin in the abscess, or have hollowed it out around it: then it is fitting to fill it with an old cloth, or as when a cloth is woven, or wrapped with a layer of linen without moisture: and bind it up until the third day.
Then apply it, and take care of it with what is necessary from the ointment, until it heals.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs during your operation, then administer cold water and vinegar, after it has been soaked into those rags; and apply it repeatedly to the place where the flow of blood is.
If, however, the flow of blood persists, then it is necessary to administer a regimen and treatment, and powders, which I have described in many places in this our book, and from division.
If, however, it is in winter time, and the place of the abscess is of many nerves: then it is necessary to pour poultices with wine, and hot oil; and apply them to the places.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy: then it is necessary to apply poultices soaked in water, and oil, or with wine and oil, and all of that is cold. Until when it will be the third day, as we have said, then it is necessary to dissolve the abscess, and clean it, and administer in its treatment what is suitable for it, until it heals.
This is therefore what is necessary from the knowledge of universal treatment of abscesses.
According to the path of distinction, I have already spoken about every abscess, how it is a way to its treatment, individually and in chapters.
Head 41.
Concerning the section on abscesses that occur on the skin of the head.
Abscesses occur on the skin of the head, small ones, and they are from the species of Knots, which are contained by tunics, which are like little sacs: as if they were Hausolated glands.
And there are many species of them.
Among them are fatty ones; and among them are those that contain humidity similar to slime; and among them are those that contain humidity similar to frost and pus; and similar to these, and among them are those that are hard and stony.
And in the cutting of all and their extraction there is no fear, as long as at their cutting he does not make the arterial counter-flow.
And the operation in their cutting is, that you may try it with an instrument, whose form comes after this, which is called Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it is that which contains moisture, then dry it in the cutting according to the length of the superficial cutting: according to this form.
And begin with the cutting from line b to line g. When therefore the moisture is evacuated, then scrape out the cyst which contains that moisture, and do not allow anything to penetrate deeply from it.
For it often returns, if something remains from it.
Then submerge the wound in Egyptian unguent, if it is present: and if not, in water and salt: and fill with it a dressing, and leave it until another day. For it corrodes what remains from the cyst.
Then apply the Egyptian ointment to the wound a second time and a third time, and if you desire it, until you are confident that nothing remains from the cyst, then treat the wound with an ointment until it is healed.
What if the apostema contains a fatty node: then dry it according to this form, with a cutting having the form of a cross: and apply olive oil between the lips of the cutting, and scrape it out from every part: and test your skill in the extraction of the skin which contains it.
If, however, the artery makes a counter-flow against you, then do what I will tell you afterwards.
And similarly do in the case of a stone-like apostema, from the cutting and curing, according to what we have said.
And the cutting above a stone-like apostema is easier: because it has little blood and moisture. And when there are certain abscesses that are on the head in which some people have, with which there is not deep humidity.
That is: because I cut above an abscess on the head of a little old woman, and I found the abscess like a hard and solid stone, rough, white, which could not be broken: and if someone were to strike it with something projecting, they would wound themselves.
In other abscesses, however, which occur on the head, apart from these, namely the abscesses that occur on the heads of children and at the roots of the ears, the section of all of them should be a superficial section.
Or make the perforation always from the lower part of them, so that the flow of the discharge may be directed downwards.
Then treat them with what is suitable for their cure.
Concerning the surgical incision of scrofulous swellings on the neck.
Chapter 42.
These abscesses often occur on the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin area: and sometimes they occur on other parts of the body.
And above those that occur on the neck, there are one and many: and they are generated from inward corruption: and every scrofulous swelling is within its own proper tunic, just as it is in nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have narrated.
And there are many types of these scrofulous swellings. And among them are stone-like ones: and among them are those that contain humidity: and among them are malignant ones, which do not respond to treatment.
Therefore, when you find one of them in good condition on touch, whose color is close to that of the skin, and it moves to every part, and is not connected with the nerves of the neck, nor with the tendons, nor with the arteries, nor is it deep: it is necessary to cut it out by a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body in a straight line, and to remove it from every side: and stretch the two edges of the skin with forceps or with many hooks, if you need them, as we have said concerning abscesses on the head, and extract it gradually.
And it is above the caution, lest a vein or nerve be cut.
And let the instrument not be sharp: lest you add to yourself a hand with the incision, or disturb the weak, and cut what is not necessary.
If you cut a vein or an artery, and it prevents you from operating, then apply to the wound a triple bandage, or certain powders that absorb blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acute inflammation subsides, and the wound softens: or it begins to suppurate.
For blood is absorbed by a two-layered bandage; therefore return to your operation, until you complete it.
Then examine with your index finger, if anything remains from the small curved hooks; therefore cut them, and cleanse them.
What if there is in the root of the Strophula a large vein: then it is necessary that you do not cut that Strophula at its root; rather it is necessary to tie it with a double thread, and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton, which you had already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it.
If the entire Strophula is cut, then it is necessary to bring together the two lips of the wound: and sew them themselves immediately, after you see that no excess remains.
If you see that excess remains because of the size of the Strophula, then it is necessary to proceed to cut its root, and extract it: and administer the suture as we mentioned.
And what contains moisture from the Strophula, pierce it with a superficial piercing: so that you may see the place of its maturity: and put the piercing from what follows below in the body, as we mentioned.
Then apply a dressing with ointment after the perforation, and similarly to those things, so that it corrodes what remains from the corruption, until when you see the wound is already clean: then treat it with a generating ointment of flesh, until it heals.
Chapter 43.
About the section of the epiglottis from an abscess that occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this section of the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has performed it: and this is the writing of their sermon.
However, in those having a severe cough, it is necessary to remove the section of the epiglottis: since no benefit comes through that: because all the veins and lungs are weak.
In those indeed who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or two tonsils, when there is no sickness in the canal, it is necessary to perform the section of the epiglottis, to flee from the destruction which occurs due to suffocation.
Therefore, when we want to do it, it is necessary to cut the epiglottis under three or four circular rings of the epiglottis, with a small section in width, in that which is between two circular rings in quantity, so that the section is in the sifa, not in the cartilage: and this place is convenient for the section, since it is deprived of flesh, and the blood vessels are distant from it.
If the surgeon is timid, then it is necessary to stretch out the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he should open the skin, until when he reaches the canal: he should look for a blood vessel, if he sees something from them.
Then he should cut the sifa that we have described: and it is indicated above the section of the canal from the phlegm which flows out from it, with that which is disrupted, and by the removal of the voice.
And let the open wound be diminished at some time.
When therefore the time arrives in which suppuration is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound's skin, and separate it itself alone without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow, until it is healed.
Said Albucasis. The sum of this sermon which we have narrated is not an exposition, unless when they see that some apostematum has already affected the throat of the infirm person; and the infirm person has been elevated above death, and wishes to intercept his breath: they hasten to cut off the Epiglottis, so that the infirm person may breathe over the place of the wound with a certain respiration, and be saved from death: and for this reason they have ordered to diminish the open wound, until the evil of the disease is consumed, and it is an evil of three days, and as if itself: then therefore they have ordered to sew up the wound, and to cure it, until it is healed.
And that which I have seen through myself is, that a certain girl seized a knife, and sent it with her own hand into her throat, and cut with it a part of the windpipe canal.
And I was called to her cure, and I found her groaning, as is the groaning of the dying.
I therefore examined the wound, and I found blood that had flowed out from the small wound. I was therefore confused, because she had not cut a vein, nor an artery: and air was escaping from the wound.
I therefore treated the wound, and I cured it, until it was healthy: and nothing happened to the girl, except hoarseness in her voice: and she returned after days to a better condition of her own.
Therefore I say: that in the cutting of the Epiglottis there is no fear.
On the surgical section of the hernia in the throat.
Chapter 44.
This abscess is called a hernia of the throat, it is a large swelling, according to the natural color of the body; and it occurs frequently in women.
And it is in two modes: for it is either natural or accidental.
In the natural one, certainly there is no ingenuity.
The accidental one, indeed, is according to two modes: of which one is similar to fatty nodes. And the other mode is similar to an abscess that occurs from the nodule of the artery. And in this case there is fear, and it is not fitting that a premature assumption be made about it with iron, unless it is small; and you should try it, and investigate it with a gentle touch. If you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not suspended with any of the veins: then cut it off, just as nodes are cut off; and remove it with what it contains from the cyst, if it is in a cyst: and if not, then thoroughly examine the entire area. Then treat the place with what is fitting for its cure, until it heals.
Chapter 45.
On the surgical section on the types of Nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division; and it is necessary to narrate here the difference between a Node and an Exitus, since it causes doubt.
I say therefore, that when there is an Exitus, there is heat, and fever, and violent pains, until the swelling subsides due to the abundance of matter, and the putrefaction is completed: then indeed the fever and heat cease.
But with a Node, there is no heat, nor fever, nor pains, and it contains them in a cyst-like sac, which is their own proper bag, and it is according to the natural color of the body: and their reception is like that of a chickpea, and they are formed like a swelling, and they are larger and smaller.
And there are two secondary types: either fatty: or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, according to what I said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the treatment of a node, you should test it and examine it first with an instrument called Intromissum, according to what its form will be in the chapter that comes after this.
And the method of examining abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and insert it into the moist place which you find in the abscess: and you turn your fingers with it slowly, until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then, finally, you measure the size of the abscess with your hand according to its magnitude.
Then, extract the Intromissum, and examine what comes out in its track.
If, therefore, a flowing moisture comes out, whatever the color may be: then dry it with a superficial incision, according to what I said about the remaining abscesses.
And if no moisture comes out in the track of the Intromissum, then know that it is fatty: therefore dry it with a cross-shaped incision, according to this form, as I taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and scrape off the skin from all sides with ease: and keep it still if you can above it, so that you may extract the healthy part along with the node.
But if the kernel is dislodged during the operation and it is not possible to extract the healthy part, for this often happens, then remove it fragment by fragment, until nothing remains of it.
For if some small or large part of it remains, the node returns according to its fullness.
If it overcomes you and some part of it remains: then fill the wound at your completion with certain corrosive powders that are sharp: and bind the wound, and put above it what sits as a warm abscess, and treat it with the remaining treatment, until it heals.
If then there is a large knot, then dissect its two lips, and cure it with that which incarnates.
If however there is a contrary pulsating vein, or not pulsating: then prepare, and fill the place of the trito with triturated (material), and leave it tightly bound for two days, or three, until the ulcer putsrefies, and the ebullition of the blood subsides: then return to incising that which remains from the Knot.
Chapter 46.
Concerning the forms of instruments for section and perforation.
These are the forms of Intromissors, and they are of three species.
For one of them is large, and one of them is medium, and one of them is small.
Let them be made from iron with a square, angled extremity, so that they may firmly enter into the affected part.
And these are the forms of Explorers, and yes, that is, perforators: and one of them is named "bers", that is, a perforator: and they are of three species.
For one of them is large, and medium, and small.
They are suitable, so that they may investigate through those affected parts, and ulcers, and fistulas, and abscesses from what is between them, which is among the bones.
And others are made round, smooth, slender, like large needles which are used for subsellions, namely from citrus wood, or from copper, or iron, or silver.
And the better ones of them are those that are made from citrus wood.
And Explorers are also made from black lead, which are suitable so that they may explore through those fistulas, whose concavities are reflections, so that they may reflect with their own gentleness along with those reflections.
And they are again of three species. Since there are some that are long, some that are medium, and some that are short, according to the quantity of the amplitude of the fistula and its constriction.
The forms of wounds, and they are of many species.
Because some of them are simple, namely those with a single cure; and they are of three species, as you see, large, medium, and small.
And some of them are stagnant wounds; and they are of three species.
And some of them are wounds having two curvatures, and they are of three species; and some of them are wounds having three curvatures, and they are of three species; and all these are necessary in their own place.
And these are the forms of scalpels, with which they cut and scrape away knobs and abscesses; and they are of three species: because some of them are large, medium, and small.
Their extremities with which a section is made are acute, other extremities are not acute; and they are not placed thus, unless so that with them there may be relief in scraping away knobs with fear of incision into the vein or nerve: and so that the weak one may be healed through them, and find tranquility for a short time, which he finds with the scraping away of abscesses.
They are made of copper, and they are similar to a file, with which an alkaline substance is made: and at the broad extremity there is a hidden, pointed spatula; and it runs inward and outward when desired, as you see.
The forms of spatulas which are hidden between the fingers for piercing an abscess; and they do not perceive them, the weak ones: and they are of three species, large, medium, and small.
The forms of bleeding cups with which the flow of blood is stopped; and they are of three species, large, medium, and small.
They are made of copper or brass, rounded, somewhat long, as you see; and they should be to subtlety.
And it is necessary that these types of bleedings are great and small with you: so that you may cut off the blood quickly in case of necessity, and then when medical help is not present for you.
However, you should not make use of these blood-lettings in every part of the body, and you should not use them except in fleshy places: such as the muscles of the legs, and hips, and muscles of the arms, and breasts, and belly, and groin, and similar fleshy places.
And sometimes they are made into small instruments similar to those of the fisticules, according to their shape: with which blood is cut off when it flows from the place of phlebotomy, or at the incision of a vein, or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this shape.
Chapter 47.
Concerning the treatment of breasts in men that resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of certain men become inflamed as a result of excessive sleep (that is, when they begin to be defiled in their sleep) until they resemble those of women: and they remain foully discolored.
Whoever therefore abhors that, it is necessary to make an incision above the breasts in a lunar shape, according to this form from line to line, b. to line, g. Then scrape away the entire whitish skin. Afterwards fill up the wound with medicine of flesh incrustation. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture, and take care of it, until it heals.
But if the breast declines to the lower parts and becomes soft due to its size, as happens in women: then it is necessary to make two sections on each side, similar to a lunar shape, which are connected to each other at the edges of both sides, so that one line contains the smaller, according to this form from line to line, b. to line, g. Afterwards scrape away the skin that is between the two lips, and remove the whitish skin, and administer what we have said about suture and powders, which is necessary for healing, until it heals.
If you do not complete the incision as it is necessary to make, on account of the patient's anxiety or because of a flow of blood, then it is necessary to fill up the wound with a submerging corrosive ointment and leave it, until it corrodes away what remains from the suppuration. Then treat it, until it heals.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess that occurs under the arm pit.
Chapter 48.
These abscesses that occur under the arm pit are of the type of hard Strophuli, which contain cysts, and among them are those that contain fluids.
Those therefore that are among them which contain fluids, it is necessary to section them with a lunar incision, according to this form, from line b to line g, when thus the entire fluid in the abscess is evacuated, then fill it up with an old dressing, and leave it until another day.
Then the dressing is removed, and it is treated with a suitable ointment for that, until it heals.
If the abscess was of the type of nodular Strophuli, then split it open above, as we described in the section on Strophuli, equally.
If, however, the wound extends to this point so as to emit pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we have said previously.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of an abscess that occurs because of an Artery and a Vein.
When an artery is broken and the skin that is above it becomes consolidated, then often an abscess occurs there; and similarly it happens with veins as well, so that an abscess and inflammation occur in them.
And the signs by which it is recognized that an abscess and inflammation are due to an artery and a vein are: because when an abscess is due to an artery, it is long and aggregated deep in the body; and when you press on the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a grating sensation.
And that which is because of the Veins, is rounded in the manifest body.
And the section above this abscess is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, and in the groins, and in the neck, and in many places of the body: and it is a great fault. It is necessary therefore that its treatment should be undertaken with an iron instrument, and since it is also in the extremities or in the head, it is necessary that it should be treated.
Above this therefore, which is from the inflammation of the arterial orifice, find in a gentle section in length. Then open the section with hooks, afterwards the artery: and free it from its coats, until it is exposed.
Then introduce a needle underneath it, and make it penetrate until it reaches the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread at two places, according to what I have taught you in the extraction of the two arteries that are in the temples.
Then prick the place, which is between the two ligaments with a spatula, until all the blood that is in it comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
Then administer a treatment that generates pus, until the ligaments fall off: afterwards treat it with suitable ointments, until it is healed.
If there was an abscess because of the section of the Vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand what is possible from the abscess with the skin. Then introduce a needle underneath the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate: and let there be a double thread in it, until you extract it from the other side. Then tie it with the same abscess with a good ligature: according to what I said to you about the ligature of the vein, from two parts with two extremities of the thread.
If however you extract the thread, then introduce another needle with another thread under the entire abscess at the incision of the instrument: press, and tie your threads in four places.
Then dry the apostem in its middle until when it goes out what is in it; then cut into the redundant skin and release what is tied from it.
Then put on top of it a poultice which you have soaked in wine, and oil. Then administer treatment, which is done with bandages and ointments, until it heals.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the apostem that occurs from twisting of the tendons.
As it happens that an apostem occurs in the Artery and Vein, similarly an apostem occurs in the Tendons: which occurs from a blow, or occurs from excessive labor, and similar things: and it most often affects those places of joints, or ankles, and every place where the joints are moved: and it is a serious apostem.
Its color is similar to that of the rest of the body, and it is in many dispositional states without pain: and when it is tightly bound with a bandage, one feels in it a weakness similar to numbness.
And it is not an accumulated apostem deep within the body, but rather it is under the skin, and it moves towards every part, and does not spread to the front, nor forward, nor backward to the rear.
Therefore, since it is in the joints, it is not necessary that it be opened with an iron instrument. Perhaps a contraction will occur instead.
In that case, because it is in the head or in the forehead, make an incision with a spatula-shaped instrument. If therefore it is a small apostem, then hold it with forceps, and cut it off at the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and excavate it: then raise it, and gather the wound together with a suture: and treat it, until it heals.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the surgical removal of an apostem that occurs in the belly.
It happens to certain men that many times in their entrails and in other parts of their bodies, there are growths which are called Fungalia, by similarity with mushrooms.
Whose root is subtle, and their head is large, and their lips are turned inward. And it is small among them: and it is magnificent among them indeed.
And I have seen a man, and in his belly there were two growths, which were more similar to mushrooms than anything else: there was no difference between them, white, with minute roots, and their lips were turned inward, and they were rounded, and moisture always flowed from them.
I cut into them, and I found that one weighed almost 10 ounces and the other almost 8 ounces. And the operation for cutting into them is, as you consider then, if the patient is moist and humid, and the color of the growth is white, moist, with a subtle root: then cut into it with a broad scalpel, and let your cauterizations be present in the fire.
For often a lot of blood is expelled at its cutting: therefore be quick if the blood overcomes you, then cauterize it.
If you see a patient who is faint and timid after the incision with iron, then take a thread made from lead, carefully prepared, and tie it on with a stick: this method is what we have mentioned, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the constriction of lead. Do not cease to tighten the lead, as often as it is impressed on the growth, until it is cut off, and falls away by itself without effort.
If, however, the growth is large and rooted, then at its cutting there is something to fear and be afraid of: especially because it is in the belly.
Truly, it is possible to cut into half of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it, until the wound is healed.
And beware lest you suppose that a certain hard, dark-colored, insensitive, foul-smelling tumor is not a cancerous aposteme; for I have named it with the memory of Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of umbilical eminences.
Umbilical eminences occur from multiple causes. Or they come from fissures of the sifac, which is above the navel: and there emerges from it either a gut or an intestine, according to what happens in other ruptures. Or they come from blood that flows from a vein, or from an artery, according to what has been mentioned before. Or they come from a constricted flatulence in it.
If it is because of a fissure of the sifac and an emergence of a thinbi (tumor), then it has a color similar to that of the body, and is smooth, without pain; and it appears in different locations. And if it is because of an emergence of the intestine, then its location is with what we have narrated, of a more vehement diversity. When you press it with your fingers, it is hidden; then it returns, and perhaps there is a noise with it; and it is greatly magnified upon entering the bath, and a vehement labor.
If indeed it is because of humidity, it is smooth, then it is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor does it contract, nor is it added to.
What if it is because of blood, then it appears as a dark-colored aposteme with these signs from the gland.
If indeed it is because of born flesh: then it is a grave, thick, hard, and shaped according to one quantity.
If indeed it is because of flatulence, then its touch is smooth.
And the operation in that case is such that you should consider then, if the prominence of the navel is due to blood from an artery, or vein, or flatulence: then it is necessary to prevent it from being cured by its treatment. For there is fear and anxiety, as I taught you in the chapter where I spoke about abscesses due to an artery and a vein.
Because if the prominence of the navel is due to the intestine or a tumor, then it is necessary to prescribe for the patient, so that he may hold his breath, and stand upright with extended limbs. Then apply signs with a cautery around the entire circumference of the navel. Next, instruct him to lean back between your hands on his back. Then encircle with a broad spatula around the circumference of the navel over the place which you marked with a cautery. Next, extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then tie him around with a strong thread or with a silk cord, and let the knot of the ligature be away from the abscess.
Then open the middle of the abscess extended above the ligature. If you have found it already caught by the ligature: then extract it away from the ligature, and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine towards the interior of the belly: and if you find a tumor, then take it with a hook, and cut above its excess.
If, however, the opposite is true for you, an artery or a vein: then encircle it many times. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads: and insert two needles around the circumference which you made around the abscess in the form of a cross, which you make penetrate.
Then tie the abscess in four places above the needles: and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the extended flesh putsrefies: and it falls away by itself: or cut it out when it is subtle and putrefied.
Then treat it with what is suitable from ointments until it heals.
What if the swelling of the navel is from thick flesh in it, or from moisture: then it is necessary to reduce the abscess, as we have said. And extract the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat it with what soothes the place.
Chapter 53.
Of the treatment of cancer.
Now we have spoken in the division of the species of cancer, and how it is a way to its cure with medicines: and beware of it with its cure by iron, lest it ulcerate: and we have spoken of cancer generated in the matrix, and caution from its cure.
And the ancients said that when cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible: such as cancer which is in the breasts, or in the hip, and similar members located for extraction of the whole thing: especially when it is beginning small.
But when it has grown old and is large, it is not suitable to approach it.
For I myself was not able to heal anyone of them: nor did I see another before me who had ventured to attempt that.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is to amputate, and to release the weak part from black bile many times. Then bleed it if you find it with obvious fullness.
Then prepare the weak part for preparation, in which place it is located with the operation.
Then apply to the cancer unguents that are suitable for it. Then scarify it on every side with a knife, according to the investigation, until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood flow, and do not cut it quickly, but press out the place, and wipe away all the gross blood with your hand, or with an instrument if possible.
If, however, you cause a countermovement of the flow of thick blood from the incision of the artery or vein, then cauterize the vein until the blood is stopped. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Of the treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter 54. We will now discuss the division of the species of Dropsy, and how the collection of water occurs: and the signs of each species, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron is not only a single species of ascites: and it does not closely approach the species of tympanic dropsy with iron: nor the deeply seated species of hypochondriac dropsy: for that is mortal.
When you have already treated this species of Tympanic Dropsy with medicines and your treatment has not been successful: then consider. For if the patient is already weakened by debility, or has another infirmity besides Dropsy: such as if they have a cough, or looseness of the stomach, and similar things: then beware of treating them with iron: for that is dangerous.
If, however, you see a patient overflowing with vitality, and there is no other sickness besides Dropsy alone: and it is not a child, nor an old person: then the method of operation for this case is that you place the patient standing between your hands, and after that, press their belly with your hand, forcing water down to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a sharp pointed instrument, according to this form. It is of two acute parts, long, with an acute extremity like a spatula. However, let there be something of moderation in it, lest you should penetrate too deeply with it during the operation and injure the intestines.
Then consider. For if there is a generation of hydropsy from the part of the intestines, then it is necessary to elongate with a section from the navel at a quantity of three fingers, down to the lower direct part above the pubic bone.
If indeed the generation of hydropsy is due to a disease of the liver, then let your left section be from the navel at three fingers.
And if the generation of it is due to the spleen, then let the section be on the right side: and it is not necessary for a section to be above where the patient lies, so that superfluities do not flow back to that weak place.
Then pierce with an instrument through the entire skin. Then insert into that section, and raise your hand with a spatula between the skin, and scrape, as if you were excavating it: and let the quantity that is excavated be the quantity of a fingernail, or around that.
Then pierce deeply until the spatula reaches the empty place: and it is the place of water. Then extract it, and insert into the opening an instrument, which has this shape. And it is similar to a cannula: let it be made of silver, or copper, or gold, smooth and polished: which has a small opening at its lower part, and on its sides are three openings, two on one side and one on the other side: and its extremity is incised, according to the manner of incising a reed, as you see: and on its upper extremity is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it immediately descends onto the instrument, and thus evacuates from the water at that hour a moderate quantity.
Since if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, at that hour perhaps the patient dies through a failure of his vital spirit: or a syncope occurs which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of its strength, and according to what is indicated to you by the disposition of the patient from the virtue of his pulse, and from his goodness.
Then extract the instrument and retain the water.
That which is, because it is retained immediately on account of the skin that retains the opening that is above the wound: concerning which I spoke, so that you may perforate it in accordance with that method.
Then try the instrument on the other day, if you see that the patient can tolerate that: and extract also from the water a small quantity.
Do that for some days without fear, and guard yourself against error: until there remains only a little from the water.
If however the time is upon the patient, and you allow much water to flow out: then treat him with warm sand baths, and with much sweat in a hot bath and sun, and wait above the wound: and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed: and cauterize above the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the extraction of water: according to what I described beforehand.

Chapter 57.
Concerning the purification of infants and the treatment of their accidental injuries.
Circumcision is not a solution for continuity, just like other wounds; however, since it arises from our operation, our will, and its use is especially in boys, it is necessary that we mark out a better operation and a lighter way leading to health.
I say therefore that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is nothing except what we have acquired through experience.

Because in purification with a razor, the skin often escapes cutting. Cleansing with a sharp instrument and binding with a thread has given me good experience; because the edges are proportionate to the incision: therefore, what is above is like what is below. When you press your hand, the two edges are proportionate, and they meet according to one operation at one time; and a compression is made in a similar figure, containing the skin of the wound on all sides, with which there falls no error whatsoever.
And the method of your own operation is to make the child, especially if he is one of those who understand a little, do only what you bind with a thread on his wound itself; and leave it until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be happy in every way possible to you, because he receives his own understanding. This indeed is a formula for purification, this is it.
Let the two extremities be numb and let them be inclined: so that no branch of a stake goes out from them: and let the length of the two be equal, just like the length of the two arms is equal.
The error indeed falls in purification: because perhaps the entire internal skin has fled, or a part of it at the incision.
Therefore it is necessary to stretch it out from that hour with your finger before the apostema of the place, and to cut it according to equality.
But if you cannot hold it with your finger, then extract it with a hook, and cut it.

Heal it therefore with what we have said, until it is healthy.
Chapter 58.

Then extend the thread with a little wool gradually. For the wool will follow the vein.
Then extract it, and make the vein flow through the passage of urine; and repeat the siring, and do not cease to do that until the bladder is evacuated, and finds an alleviation of the inflammation.

Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a stone.

And the healing of children is easy, because they recover until fourteen years. And it is difficult in old people. In young people, however, it is medium, because it is between that: and its stone (lith) which is larger, the treatment is easier; and if small, the care of its reversal.

Perhaps indeed the finger compresses, which is in the anus, the stone at the section, and it exits without difficulty.
