When therefore it is so, then it is necessary to warm it up, by applying a sponge or a moist cloth soaked in lukewarm water alone. Or you boil it in water with cyperus grass, and spikenard, and embrocations, until the inflammation of the intestine is resolved.
When you make a wine in which there is astringency; and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
When you see the swelling has already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which the altaea or mallow has been decocted. For through that alleyway of his, an entrance is made with a small bandage.
If, however, the return of it is excavated after this cure, then find a small opening in the disruption, with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first cure is better when it is prepared, than cutting: and there is no need for forced cutting after necessity and failure of the instrument.
One part of it is twisted, acute, and the other part is not acute, and the extremity should not be with the subtlety of a Spatumilis, but rather more obtuse for a short time: and that is what resembles a reed, as you see.
When therefore the wound is enlarged and the intestine is ingressed, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its own proper place, if possible, because it is better.
When the disruption is wide in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary to make the weak patient lie on his back, and place his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head, and his chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if there is a disruption in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your attention and your consideration always in this, that the part where the wound is may be higher than the other part.
And so it is necessary to administer it thus in great wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
In small wounds, however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then make sure that the pious minister is present among your hands, who holds the entire disruption with his hand: and brings together its lips. Then he should touch those who follow after the suture with something after something: and this is the common method of a single suture.
And it is, that you should receive a needle, or a number of needles secondly according to the quantity of the width of the wound. Then let fall from the extremity of the disruption the quantity of the ring finger, that is, of the doctor: and fix a needle beyond what you introduce into it a thread in two extremities of the skin, with two extremities of the suture which is beneath the skin from the inside, until you make it penetrate from another part, and now you have added two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the suture: and they become four orders.
Then bind with a double thread around the instrument several times from two parts together, until they are added, the two lips of the wound, an aggregate gathering decently.
Then let fall the thickness of the finger also, and fix in them another needle: afterwards tie it with a thread, as you did with the first instrument. Do not cease to do that with what you are skilled from instruments, until you complete the constriction of the entire wound.
And let the instruments be medium between thickness and subtlety: because subtle instruments indeed quickly fall into the flesh: and thick ones are difficult to introduce into the skin: on account of that it is necessary that they be medium in subtlety and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the instrument be fixed in two extremities of the skin close together much: so that the flesh does not quickly fall into a wound, and the wound does not open before its consolidation, and they are not elongated also with the suture, so that the wound is not prevented from consolidation.
Then cut the extremity of the instrument, so that they do not harm the weak part during their sleep: and put pillows from cloths on every side, which hold the extremities of the instrument: and let them be, until you know that the wound is already consolidated.
And this type of suture with acupuncture is more convenient in small wounds. Because a single thread, or two, or about that many are sufficient for their suture.
The second common suture is, as it were, to combine four extremities: two skin extremities and two superficial ones in one vein, with a thread that has an equal winding in subtlety and thickness.
Then when you penetrate with the thread to these four extremities, reduce the thread from the part where you inserted it, so that the returned thread falls on the upper part of the wound: so that it is a suture, just as a suture of cloth which strings together cloths, and put in all the sutures the amount of thickness of the little finger.
Men know this suture.
And with this suture I myself sewed up a wound that had occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and there was a major disruption of the wound the size of one palm: and about two palms of intestine had come out of the middle of the intestines, and there was a disruption in the middle of the belly.
I restored it afterwards, after his intestine had stood outside the wound for 23 hours.
The wound was therefore consolidated in a period of about 15 days: and I treated it, and he was healed: and he lived many years after that, enjoying his own dispositions.
And the physicians had judged that it could not be healed.
And it was a miracle, because I did not treat it with an ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment could be found, nor anything from medicines. I applied therefore an old cotton cloth over the wound twice a day, and I continually cleansed it with honey water, until it was healed.
I will describe two special types of suture according to the narrative of my speech.
And it is, as you begin with an acute suture, and you insert a needle from the outer to the inner part.
When the needle penetrates into the skin, and into the flesh along the length and width of the entire body: leave the extremity of the suture on the side where you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate from the inner part to the outer part in another extremity, mirroring.
When you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate according to this in the same extremity, mirroring from the outer part to the inner part: and leave the extremity of the suture, which is on that side, and make the needle penetrate into another extremity from the inner part to the outer part: and make it penetrate with your penetration, as you make it penetrate in the extremity mirroring, which is on that part, until it penetrates into the entire thing.
Then begin also from the same side, and sew it with the extremity that is on the other side of the suture, which is in another side, and extract the needle from the skin where it penetrated.
Then bring it back into the skin, and sew the extremity of the suture that is on another side, with this mirroring extremity: and extract it from the skin which is on that part, and make it one thing, and another: and make it one thing after the seam, until you have restored the entire surface according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that you sew the skin like those who sew pelles: so that you make one thing from one part of an extremity of the skin: and that you leave it as another thing on another side, until you complete.
The second type of proper suture, which Galen mentioned.
This his sermon is narrated, that you sew it according to similarity, as some physicians sew them, so that they gather all the parts together to compare their natural convenience: and apply the extremity of the suture to its other extremity, and the mirroring extremity to its other extremity. And that is secondary because I will narrate it to you.
It is necessary to place the scalpel's point near the extremity of the Mirach propinquum to you from the outer side, and make it penetrate to the inner part alone; and let two extremities of the skin be relaxed. Then turn the scalpel around, and make it penetrate from the outer side to the inner part in the two extremities of the skin, namely on both sides.
Then turn them both around, and also make them penetrate both from the inner to the outer, in the two extremities of the Mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is a suture which precedes reminiscence.
This is therefore the discourse of Galen also.
And some have said. The suture of the belly is straightened with two hooks. That is, to introduce into them both a single thread: and begin with the introduction of the hook, from that which is at your hand, and make it penetrate up to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew leather evenly.
And know that when there is a disruption in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than those of other places of the belly.
The judgment of the medicine for wounds is the judgment of the rest of the wounds.
That is, because when you treat a fresh wound with its own blood, before it changes to air: and you reduce the intestine, and sew it up, and adapt it: then put it on a fleshy powder.
If the air has already changed the wound: then put on it certain ointments which are present to you, until they make pus: and the threads fall away, and the skin is consoled, and the Mirach. Then treat it as it is the treatment of other wounds, until it heals.
But if you do not have medicines present, then put on it immediately, from which it starts to discharge pus, an old poultice: and change it twice a day, as I instructed you, until it heals.
For you do not need other medications in various preparations, when wounds are simple.
But if the wound is situated on principal members in a lesion, then it is necessary to immerse a soft woolen cloth in tempered warm oil, or in rose oil: and put it around the area between the thighs, and under the armpits.
If you feel pain, or stitches, or putrefaction in your intestines (for it often happens thus) then give a clyster with wine, sharp, warm and black: especially if the putrefaction has already penetrated into the intestine, and a penetrating evacuation has been made up to its concavity.
And know that what is from the thick intestine is of easier healing: and what is from the subtle ones is of more difficult healing.
The intestine which is called the Jejunum does not receive healing from a wound that occurs in it at all: and this is because of its multitude, which is from its veins, and their greatness, and because of the subtlety of its body, and because of the proximity of its natural nerves.
If however what proceeds from the wound is serous matter, and you collect it freshly: reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
If indeed the space has passed for it, and it has already become green or black: then it is necessary to bind it with a thread above the place that has been denigrated by it, so that there does not occur a flow of blood. For in serous matter there are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament, and place two hanging threads from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that the attraction to it may be alleviated, and the extraction of the pus from the wound may occur, and the emission of the pus from the wound.
The remembering of a wound that occurs in the intestine.
When there occurs a disruption in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it can be restored in certain men; therefore, because I have seen one who was wounded in his belly with a blow of a lance, and it was a wound on the right side of the stomach, and an old wound: and it became a fistula, and there came out from it a discharge, and flatulence.
I applied a treatment, until I was not confident in his healing: nor did I cease to carefully tend to it, until he was healed and the location was consolidated.
After I saw the location already consolidated, I feared for the weak person, lest a bad accident should occur in his belly: and it did not happen to him from that bad accident. And he remained in his better physical condition, eating and drinking, and evacuating, and he took a bath, and was exercised in his diets.
And now some experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is necessary to bind it together according to this method. And it is, as if you receive forms of great heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place a formica (ant) one of those which have their mouth open upon the two lips of the wound. When it therefore takes hold on them, and closes its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not come loose.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do that with an ant following an ant according to the quantity of the entire wound.
Then reduce it, and sew up the wound. For those things that remain attached to the intestines, as long as the intestine is being healed and not receiving a continuous flow of noxious material.
And now it is possible to tie off the intestine also with a subtle thread: which is taken from the intestine of an animal, and attached to it after it has been inserted into a narrow point.
And it is that you should take the end of this thread from the intestine and remove much flesh. Then tie the linen thread, subtly wrapped around the end. Then insert that thread into a narrow point, and sew the intestine to it. Then reduce it into a fissure.
And this method of suture with twigs and intestine is not reliable except through the way of faith and hope.
You know that a wound which is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the slender intestines, is not curable except by subtlety, nor is its healing complete.
On the treatment of wounds and fistulas.
You know that every wound or abscess, when it becomes old and chronic, and becomes an ulcer, and does not close up, and always discharges pus, and is not separated: then it is universally called a fistula in whatever member it may be, and we call it Zuchemen.
And indeed a fistula is a hardened, white, filtering node, with no pain, which has a concavity like the wing of a bird: and because of that some people call it Risati, that is, a wing.
And it is in certain hours humid, which discharges pus. And perhaps the humidity is separated at certain hours. And sometimes this humidity is abundant, and sometimes it is scarce: and it is thick, and it is subtle.
And I have previously mentioned in the division, then when I spoke of wounds, that every wound which is not treated, nor does flesh grow in it, is not that except on account of a single new cause.
There are some which are, either because of the scarcity of blood in the body; or because of its malice; or because within it and above its lips is hard, dense flesh, preventing the growth of new flesh; or because there is much filth and foulness; or because the wound itself is putrid, and the matter that is applied to it is of bad quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable for its cure; or because a corrupting cadence falls upon the region, or through the property of the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta; in which there occurs difficult digestion of diseases, and the healing of apostemes is delayed by nature; or because there is one bone, or a number of bones.
And I have already shown all these causes and signs of them, and their cures with medicines in the division; and it is necessary here that I make you know the cure of Fistulas and Ulcers, which is done through an operation with the hand.
Know therefore, that a Fistula occurs in all parts of the body. For there are Fistulas that extend to large veins and arteries, or nerves, or skin; or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to some of the ribs; or to some of the vertebrae of the back, or to some of the compound joints of the limbs: such as the hands and feet; and there are those that wander near the principal member and its similars.
Those therefore among these Fistulas that are of this kind, are from diseases of difficult healing, and do not receive a cure, and the occupation with them is labor and ignorance.
But if it is from Fistulas of a certain profundity that does not extend to those places which I mentioned; and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to penetrate to the knowledge of it, then take a probe made of copper or iron, if the Fistula tends according to straightness, and explore it with the same.
If there is a twisting in the fistula, then probe it with a thread of lead that has been subtly divided. Because the lead, with its own slight softness, adapts to the body and curves around the twisting.
What if the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to mark it out with a thread. Therefore, probe through one of its openings, from its own openings.
For the moisture where you probe it, leads towards other openings, and flows out through them.
Then, you should carefully inquire according to which method is possible for you: so that you may know what is there, whether it is a bone, or a nerve, and the fistula is concave and far off, or near; or if it is a fistula with many openings, and stands throughout all of it, with the greatest strength of yours; with your questioning, in which you question the weak, and in the discovery of pain upon compression with your hand over the place, and similar signs and accidental causes are apparent.
When you stand therefore over all those true stations, then proceed to curation according to confidence: and it is, that you may consider then, if the fistula appears near, or in a healthy place far from a junction, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I have told you about: then cut the fistula according to what has been previously mentioned in my narrative, and remove what is in it through filtration, and corrupt flesh, which is born in its opening from the flesh, or thelul, and similar things: and heal it, until it becomes healthy.
If the fistula is of great depth and is according to straightness: then it is necessary that you cut it as deeply as possible. Then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then administer enwrapped licinia in sharp medicines: and introduce them up to the concavity of the fistula which you comprehended with the iron: do it several times, until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remains in the concavity of the fistula from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If, however, it does not heal through that, then cauterize it according to what we have narrated.
If, therefore, the cause of the fistula is bone, and it is certified by you in that respect: then investigate it, and dissect it according to what I have narrated to you: if nothing prohibits you from a vein, or nerve, or principal member, as we have said.
If, therefore, the bone is disclosed to you, and there is something of decay in it, and blackness: then scrape it, until all the corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which puts flesh on it, until it is healed.
What if it does not heal, and emits pus as it was doing: then know that you did not reach total eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, uncover again on top of it a second time, and make a precise examination of its reasoning, and cleansing of the same with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If, therefore, it is healed, that is good: and if not, then know that the corruption is deep within the body, which you have not reached. There is no ingenuity for you in this case, unless you abandon it to nature.
What if the small bone is corrupted, and it is possible for you, extract it with subtle forceps that are suitable for that.
If, however, there are many bones, then make an exact extraction of all of them, and do not leave anything out with your strength.
If, however, something opposes you from the moistures, and there is no fear of cutting into them, or error: then cut into those moistures and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If, however, you do not have the preparation to remove the bones at that time, then put on the wound what will putrefy it: and leave it, until what is around those bones putrefies from the flesh and moistures.
And take care that the wound is not consolidated. And let the incision be constricted as you have divided in your operation. Apply to the same place, if there is pain, a burnt coton soaked in sulfur mixed with oil; or a burnt coton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or some other green ointments.
For when those parts putrefy, namely the moistures, and the bones are separated from them, their removal is alleviated, and their attraction.
If there is a large bone, such as the thigh bone or hip bone, and similar ones, and what has become corrupted from it is a great part: then scrape that very bone with a final scraping, until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
If what is corrupted in it is a great part, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it, and cut into the whole thing up to the place where the corruption reaches: then treat it, until it is consolidated.
And I relate to you a case that happened to a man on his thigh: so that you may put it as an example and aid on your operation.
There was a young man of about thirty years of age, to whom a pain occurred in his thigh, from a cause that was moved upon it within the body, that is, from the interior, until the matters reached the thigh and became an abscess with a large swelling: and there was no external cause for him.
It was extended therefore with him for a long time with the error of the doctors, until the abscess opened, and many matters flowed together with it. And his treatment was put off, until the thigh became fistulous, and many openings were made in it: which all emitted pus, and bodily moistures.
Therefore many doctors treated him for about two years, and there was no one skilled in the art of healing, until I was called, and I saw his thigh, and the matters that were flowing out from those openings in a great stream: also the whole body of the man was already weakened, and his color was citrine.
I will introduce a probe through one of the openings and it will reach the bone.
Then I carefully examined all the openings, and I found that they reached the wound from every part of the leg.
I made an incision, and I cut above one of the openings, until I reached a part of the bone: and I found it corrupted; for it was already decayed, blackened, putrid, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
I removed what I had discovered, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I put aside what was not in the bone except that corruption which I had cut away and removed. And I had now eradicated it.
Then I applied a dressing to the wound with consolidating medicines for a long period of time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I probed again, and I reached above the bone a second time, above the first discovery: and I found a continuous corruption with the bone; and I removed what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted to restore it. And it was not restored.
I reached above it again, and I did not cease to cut into the bone in parts, and to try to restore it: and it was not restored, until I cut out about a handful from the bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was consolidated quickly, and healed: and this iteration was not necessary in the operation, and section of itself, except due to the disposition of debilitated infirmity, and scarcity of its tolerance, and my fear concerning its death: because there was a risk to it at all times from an excess of bad evacuation of syncopic flux.
It was therefore fully healed: and a hard, fleshy growth was born in the place of the bone, and a good arrangement was made in its body, and its strength returned: and it was established in its dispositions: and it did not cause any impediment to its walking.
If the prominent bone in the body part is already fractured, then it is necessary to treat it in this way: take a bandage and bind it around the extremity of the prominent bone; and place a pad, which extends it upwards; and put another bandage over the first one made of thicker wool. Then bind it over the flesh that is above the bone; and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh downwards; and take care to detect the flesh in the place where you want to make an incision, so that the incision does not harm the flesh.
Put a splint or a plate under the bone below, because when you do that, it does not prevent the corrupted flesh from being cut.
And it is necessary for the incision to be above the corrupted place for a short time, so that the corruption of the bone does not spread; and it does not appear in its appearance, and it will not lead to another incision.
If the bone is corrupted and not prominent, but rather continues as a whole with its part, and the corruption is in the middle of it or in its own part, then remove the flesh from all sides completely. Then put a splint below.
After that, make an incision from the first part where the corruption is, until the incision continues from the other part; and let the incision be according to the length of the corruption for a short time, as we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut through the corrupted joint, and make an incision in the bone where it continues from the other part.
If indeed the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy for it except scraping away.
If the corruption is in the comb-like structure of the hand or in the comb-like structure of the foot, then the treatment for it is very difficult indeed. However, it is necessary to investigate the corruption as it appears to you. And you should anoint it and cleanse it according to whatever possible disposition is for you, and with whatever craft is rightly directed towards you, when it does not cause opposition from the Vein or Nerve.
And you should know that the incisions and saws for cutting into these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and their subtlety, and their size, and their smallness, and their hardness, and their rarity. Wherefore it is necessary that you prepare an instrument of every species of operation convenient to that operation.
And you should know that these operations signify to you the type of instrument which you need, when there is a prolonged study with you. And knowledge of the methods of this art is a testimony to the methods of health; for then you will already have found through yourself that which is convenient to him from the instruments for every infirmity.
And I am setting forth to you at the end of this chapter a number of instruments, which you will put as examples on those which you cut into, and a test which you experience on others.
The form of another Saw.
The form of the Razor for joints. Let the head of this Razor be according to the form of a star-shaped key: and its points be according to the form of Elschotz's fleam.
And it is not suitable unless the heads of the joints are rubbed with it, when they become corrupted, or the bone is large and massive.
The shape of the larger rasp.
The shape of the broad rasp.
The shape of the rasp in which there is a concavity.
The shape of the other subtle rasp.
The shape of the rasp of the curved extremity.
The shape of the small rasp, subtle for stretching.
This is similar to a file, and the extremity is like a saw: it is shown in many places for filing bones.
The shape of the rasp that is suitable for filing what is perforated from bones.
Its extremity is triangular, and it has a sharp point.
It was made in India. And similarly it is necessary that all things which we have spoken about are made from rasps, and incisors, and saws.
Concerning the cutting of extremities and the sawing of bones.
The extremities putrefy, either from an external cause or an internal one.
When therefore you have treated that corruption with medicinal cures and it does not respond to treatment: and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, which does not allow anything to penetrate: then it is necessary to cut into that limb up to where the corruption reaches, so that the patient may be freed from death or from the evil which is a greater defect of the limb.
And the sign that it has reached this point is that the limb turns black, until it becomes putrid: as if fire had burned it, or it had rotted after turning black: until the putrefaction is cut away up to what follows in the limb, and spreads into the whole body.
Therefore it is necessary to make an incision with that, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the bite of certain venomous animals: such as the marine scorpion, and the viper, and the rutella, and similar ones.
If therefore there is a corruption or bruise on the extremity of the finger, then cut into the finger, and do not leave space for the corrupted part to spread to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut into it from the root of the affected part, and do not leave space for the corrupted part to spread to the forearm.
And if it has invaded that, then cut into the arm at the elbow in its joint.
If however the corruption has spread, and you see it surrounding the elbow: then there is certain death for the patient. And to administer beyond that from the treatment, is a quantity of strength.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption has invaded the toe, then cut into it at one of its nodes: and if it takes hold in the arch of the foot, then cut off the entire foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut into the leg at the joint of the knee.
Since if the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no ingenuity in that, unless abandonment and leaving the patient to death.
And the method of cutting into a limb or sawing it is to tighten a ligature below, which you wish to cut: and tighten another ligature above the location. And the assistant stretches one ligature downwards to the lower parts: and the assistant stretches another ligature upwards to the upper parts. And you separate the flesh that is between the two ligatures, with a spatula laid flat, until the entire flesh is exposed. Then it should be cut, or sawn.
And it is necessary to put a linen cloth around all parts, and the saw should not touch the healthy place: and the patient may suffer additional harm, and a hot aposteme.
If however a flow of blood occurs internally while operating, then cauterize the place quickly: or apply some powders that absorb the blood to it. Then return to your medication, until it is complete.
Then bind the wounded member with a suitable bandage, and care for it, until it heals.
And I will tell you what happened to a certain man with his foot, this very thing that I am telling you.
What happened was that his foot became blackened, with a kind of burning that resembled fire: and the corruption first occurred on his toe, until it took over the entire foot.
Therefore, the man was freed from himself, after he saw that the corruption was spreading to his limb with its ugliness, which he found from the pain and burning: and it ceased at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time had passed for him, that same corruption happened to him again on the index finger of his hand.
He came to me, and I tried to remove that excess with what I had placed on his hand from my medicines after purifying his body: but the excess did not recede, and began to spread to another finger: until the corruption began on his hand.
He called upon me to cut off his hand: and I was unwilling to do that, hoping for a retraction of that excess from me: and I also feared death itself at the cutting of his hand. Because the man's strength was superior in the case.
After he despaired of me, he returned to his own land: and he came back to me about it, because he was freed, and his whole hand was cut off: and he was healed.
And I did not add this narrative, unless it should be an aid concerning that which falls out from this kind of illness: and so that it might be a signification by which it is signified, and according to what is done.
Of the treatment of wounds, and the quality of cleansing with medicines.
When an abscess forms in certain fleshy members and the space of the abscess is prolonged, until it collects pus: then it bursts or perforates, and what was in it exits, leaving a vacant space as if it were a vessel; and the skin that is above it is like a thin membrane that has been stretched out; and it does not reach its ultimate corruption, neither in the mouth nor in the nerve, nor in the ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus; and it is not called a Fistula. However, if its space is prolonged until it impresses corruption on one of these members, then it is called a Fistula or zuken.
And its treatment is to cut away the entire skin as it revolves; and especially when it has been stretched out and made like a thin membrane; and you should be confident that it will not adhere to the place, on account of its own corruption.
But if you think that the skin will adhere and the ultimate corruption does not follow from it as a consequence; and if there is thickness in the skin from the flesh, then treat it with suppositories; and it is, as if you were treating a Sinus.
Then if it is large and the pus that is emitted from it has a foul odor; then inject it with an Egyptian medicine, which is made of acetum (vinegar), oil, and zuinar (a type of resin), taking equal parts, mixed together in a vase; and they are decocted over fire until the medicine becomes red-hot; and it penetrates into the thickness of the honey.
Then take from it what is necessary for you; and dissolve it with water and honey, and inject it into the Sinus; and close its opening, and leave the medicine in it for a period of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure: do this for several days, until the Sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And when you inject with water of ashes, when this medicine is not present to you: and it is, that you should take the ash of vine wood or the ash of holm oak wood; and you pour it over the water. Then strain it, and inject with it, until you are confident that the sinus is already cleansed.
What if the patient is not tolerating the Egyptian medicine, nor the water of ashes, then inject it with water and honey, to which something has already been mixed from crushed zuinar. Or inject it with honey and mixed wine, because of the property of honey that cleanses and washes; and the wine makes the sinus adhere, especially when there is an excess of dryness and roughness in the wine.
When you do that several times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus is already removed: then inject it with what causes flesh to grow in it.
And it is, that you take from the emplaster dyafinicon; and dissolve it with rose oil and wine of astringency; or inject it with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I wrote about in the treatise on ointments.
What if the orifice of the sinus is narrow, in which the cannula of the injection does not fit: then enlarge it with an iron instrument for a short time; or put in that same licinium wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in basilicon ointment, until it is enlarged.
And similarly if the orifice is also wide, then bring together its lips with a suture: and allow from it a quantity in which it can be injected without addition.
And similarly if the orifice from which pus flows is elevated to the upper parts: then dry it in the depressed place that is in it, so that the pus flows out of it downwards: because when pus is confined in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from being generated in it.
What if it is not possible for you to dissect the sinus around the lower part of it according to your judgment: then try, so that you prepare the member with a preparation by which pus flows out easily, as it is prepared for you. Try, so that nothing of the pus remains in it at all: and take consolidating medications which are applied to the wound, suitable for what you want. And spread them out over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it makes contact with the entire sinus. Then cut with forceps directly into the opening of the sinus's foramen, a little more widely than the opening of the sinus itself. Then spread out a gentle ointment on another cloth, according to the size of the foramen: and place it over it in a position far from the opening of the sinus, so that the flow of pus is eased out of it: and do not remove the large cloth unless after several days, or about one.
That one, however, which is over the opening of the sinus, is the one that you remove at every hour, so that what has accumulated in it from pus may come out: and so you may know, whether what flows out of it is a great deal, or a little: or digested, or undigested.
And when you do this repeatedly, consider the place of the sinus itself, whether the master of it feels pain in it, or if there is no pain in it, and whether there is an abscess in it, or if there is not an abscess in it.
When therefore your medication is extended in this way, and you see the material that flows out over the opening of the sinus, then you know that the sinus is already adhering, or approximating to adhesion: then therefore dissolve it in the morning, or on the third day: and when you dissolve the wound, then change the cloth to another, and renew the ointment.
If, however, a subtle and thin pus flows out of the sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, because there are people who slow down the generation of flesh in their wounds, and among them are those who act contrary to that.
If, however, a foul pus emerges from the wound after many days, then you will know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, therefore, the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its process is prolonged, then add in the desiccation of medicines, which make physicians truly effective, so that they may be medicines in their humid substance and in their dry virtue: such as the dyafinicon ointment, when an excessive addition from zegi is added to it.
And from the most ultimate goal of treatment, it is to receive the dyafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil, and then apply it to the old wine equal in substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it thusly.
And now make something similar to this action, when you do not have the dyafinicon ointment at hand, but rather, when you prepare it, until it thickens, and use it thus. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and galbanum: grind all of it, and powder it over the honey while it is over the flame. Then spread it over the linen cloth, and bind it over the sinus: and mix with honey which you have prepared, until it thickens: and powder over it the spices, and put it on the linen cloth, and bind it.
And when it is administered with aristolochia longa, and the roots of heavenly lily, and it is a lily: and flour of herb, and centaury. Use them as simples, or aggregates, grind them, and strain them: and powder over the honey which I have narrated, and use it thus.
What if the process of the sinus is prolonged, and it does not heal with this treatment: then you will know that there is corruption deep within it: or now it makes an impression on the bones, or in the remaining hard members, or in the ligaments: then treat it with a fistula treatment, according to what has been previously mentioned.
About the treatment of wounds on the fingers and toenails, and the cutting into of a digit.
A panaritium is an abscess that grows under the toenail of the hand or foot, and perhaps it grows on the other fingers.
When therefore its matter is prolonged and neglected in its treatment, and it becomes an abscess with a hot apostema; or it becomes corrupted and discharges pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps it corrupts the whole finger, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until there is a foul smell: and the extremity of the finger becomes thick, and its color becomes dark.
When therefore you treat it, with what we said earlier in the division, and your treatment is not effective; then it is necessary to cut away with an iron all the excess flesh that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound afterwards. For cauterization is a very helpful remedy indeed.
If indeed the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the empty spaces of the nail are pressing on the flesh inside, and they start to sting it, and hurt it: then it is necessary to put a subtle file under the angle of the nail that stings the flesh, and push it upwards; and cut away the flesh with ease, and apply what remains of the flesh from burning medicines, corroding, until everything is removed. Then treat it with ointments, until it is healed.
If however the corruption has already spread to the bone, then it is necessary to cut into that bone and extract it: for it will never be healed as long as the corrupted bone remains.
What if you see corrosion and corruption advancing on the finger, then cut it off at one of the nodes, according to what has been mentioned above. Then treat him, until he is healed.
If, however, a nail injury or a blow occurs, and there is intense pain in that case, then it is necessary to bleed the patient at the beginning. Then cut the nail with a sharp Scalpel's edge, crossing from above to below.
And beware, lest the cutting reaches the flesh that is beneath the nail, for you will cause a severe pain to occur through that to the patient, and it will be a cause of inflammation of the added flesh at that place.
Then treat the area with what alleviates pains.
However, an extra finger which is born on some people's hands is entirely fleshy: and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps there is a nail on it: and some of them grow from the root of the joint, or its growth is in certain knots of the finger. Therefore, that which grows on certain knots of the finger does not move: and that which grows near the joint of the finger perhaps moves.
Therefore, that which is fleshy from those, its cutting is easy. That is, to cut it off at its root with a broad Scalpel.
But that whose growth is at the root of the joint, its treatment is difficult. Therefore, refrain from cutting it.
On the other hand, that which grows on the finger near the last knots, then it is necessary to cut into the flesh first with a round incision up to the bone. Then saw through the bone with one of those suitable saws for that purpose. Then treat him, until he is healed.
Moreover, the consolidation that occurs on the fingers is often due to an innate defect of man, that is, born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or burning by fire, and similar things.
It is necessary therefore that you divide that consolidation, until the digits return to their natural shape. Then place between them a strip of cloth soaked in rose oil, so that they do not consolidate too quickly and separate from each other; or place between them a thin sheet of lead, until the location is consolidated as needed.
And similarly if a consolidation occurs on certain digits with the palm, then divide that consolidation, according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
On the Incision of Veins.
Veins are twisted, thick, full of superfluous melancholic fluids which occur in many parts of the body; and their most frequent occurrence is in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel, labor, and carry heavy loads.
It is necessary therefore to administer a purging of the body from black bile many times with strong purging.
Then perform bloodletting on the patient's basilic vein.
The treatment of them is indeed with iron, and it is according to two methods. One method is that they be cut, and black blood be extracted. And the other method is to evacuate the vein, and extract everything.
The section of it is indeed according to this method. First, bathe the leg with hot water abundantly, until the thick, foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the weak leg from the upper part of its hip to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then divide the vein at one place, or two, or three sections widely.
Then evacuate, that is, extract the blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and it is necessary to perform bloodletting on the weak one first. Then dry the nail with a fire until it draws out a sufficient quantity of blood which you see is enough and which the virtue of the weak part can tolerate.
Then bind it: and order him to abstain from foods that generate black bile; and repeat the evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are full, and it does not harm the weak part.
The evacuation of it is according to this method. Scrape the affected area, if there are many hairs on it. Then put it in a bath: or immerse it in its own basin with hot water, until it becomes red and the veins overflow: or exercise strong massage, if there is no present bath, until the member becomes warm.
Then dry the skin directly over the vein's section along its length: or at the end of it near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and excavate the vein from every part, until it becomes apparent to the sense: and it is at its appearance, that you see it turn from redness to blackness. When therefore it emerges from the skin, you see it white as if it were a cord.
Then introduce the scalpel underneath it, until when it is elevated and emerges from the skin: suspend it with a blunt hook, gently.
Then dry another section near that section, three fingers' breadth away. Then excavate the skin from the upper part of the vein, until it becomes apparent. Then elevate it with scalpels as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then dry another section, or several sections, if it is necessary for that. Then elevate and incise the same at the last section near the heel.
Then extract it to the section that is above it.
And do that, until you extract it from the section which is superior, or higher than all sections, until when it emerges whole; then cut it.
If it does not obey you to extract and elevate, then insert a strong double thread, and tie it, and extract it; or insert under the Radius; and revolve your hand to every part, until it emerges. And be careful, lest it is cut. For if it is cut, it will be difficult for you to elevate it greatly, and it will become inflamed above the weak part from that injury.
When you have extracted it whole, then put on the wounded place a soaked linen cloth in wine and rose oil, or oil; and take care of it, until it is healed.
If the vein is contracted, having a twisted condition where there is an involution to the parts, and it is not as we have said according to rectitude: then it is necessary to find it on every part of its twistedness, and the place of its involution.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you elevate it whole; and be careful at your section above it lest you cut the vein, or wound; for it will be difficult for you to elevate, or extract it. Preserve therefore your prudence.
The method of treating a wounded vein so that it heals.
The method of hooks for the blind.
It is not curved like the other hooks, nor is it of acute extremity, so as not to wound the vein; and it is of coarse flexibility, gentle: because if it were subtle, it would cut the vein by its subtlety; indeed, there is a superfluous coarseness in it, as we have said.
On the extraction of the femoral vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot and dry lands, such as those of Alizegi, and the land of the Arabs, and in dry and hot lands with little tenderness, that is, in which neither herb nor tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body besides the thighs.
And its generation indeed is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, just as it happens within the bodies of snakes and worms called ascarides, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And a sign of the inception of this vein is that which occurs in the thigh as an intense inflammation. Then the place is sewn up. Then the vein begins to emerge from that place of sewing, as if it were the root of a plant, or an animal.
When therefore the extremity of it appears from there, then it is necessary to apply a wrapping of plaster over that fragment of lead, whose weight is one dirham and two nodules; and suspend the lead from the thigh.
Whenever something emerges from there to the outside, wrap it in lead, and nodulate it. If it is prolonged too much, then cut off a part of it, and wrap up the remainder. And do not cut it at the root until it has emerged completely. For if you cut it, it becomes constricted, and grows into the flesh; and it causes an abscess to occur, and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary to handle it gently and extract it gradually, until it emerges from its very end, and nothing of it remains in the body; and indeed this vein emerges in some people which is five palms and ten units long. And now it has reached me, for it had gone out from a man twenty palms long.
But if it is cut off during the time of your treatment: then introduce a radish into its opening, and perforate it with a long opening, along with the body, until everything that is in it from the material is evacuated; and administer putrefaction to the place with medicines for several days. Then treat the post-operative swelling.
And this vein indeed has many branches, especially when it appears at the junction of the foot, or in the foot itself; and many openings belong to it, and a branch emerges from every opening.
Treat it therefore, as we have said in the division, and with what has been mentioned beforehand.
About the excision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called "Cow's Disease".
This disease is named "Cow's Disease" in certain lands among us; because it often affects cows, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh.
And it wanders throughout the entire body, ascending and descending; and it is perceived by sensation during its movement from limb to limb, until it bursts out wherever it bursts out on the skin's surface, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putridity of certain humors. As worms and snakes occur, that is, earthworms, and roundworms in the belly.
And they are feared because of their evil nature. Because when it wanders in the body and rises to the head, and reaches a certain place: then perhaps it appears there, and emerges, and destroys the eye; and this happens many times.
When, therefore, you want to cure it, and extract it, then it does not happen unless through its movement and the appearance of its sensation.
It is necessary, therefore, to bind what is above it, and what is beneath it with a strong constricting bandage. Then sew above it, and extract it.
If it is deeply rooted in the flesh, and you do not find it: then apply a cautery with fire to the place, until it dies.
And what is feared more is the corruption of it, which corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If you see that it has already spread to the head near the eye, then tighten a good stricture under it above the eyebrow. Then cut above it, and extract it.
And it is necessary that the sick person should be purified of his bodily corruption with medicines that dissolve bad putrid humors.
And he should beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
On the surgical treatment for Nakir disease.
This disease is called Nakir in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain members, then it moves from member to member.
And I have indeed seen it myself, according to what I relate to you. I was called to a sick woman in certain villages. And she showed me her arm, and I saw a small swelling on the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I remained for one hour, I saw a swelling increase with a tender movement, as if it were an ascending worm, going up to her shoulder more quickly than possible: so that it was like living silver, when it flowed from place to place.
Then the pain receded from that place, and settled in the shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it flowed into the rest of the body, until it reached another arm.
Then she told me that it had returned throughout her whole body, according to what I saw.
I was amazed therefore at the swiftness of its change from member to member. And I had indeed never seen this disease with my own eyes in this manner before. However, I had seen many who experienced pain moving from member to member, and I did not see it with my own eyes, as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that it is anything other than what is because of that, which a woman was among those who are affected by the dryness of their bodily vessels, from which it appears that the change in her veins has been perceived.
And it is necessary, so that they do not appear according to this reasoning in those who are delicate and have humid bodies, of hidden veins.
When therefore you apply treatment to it, and the patient feels that pain: then if it appears to your eye as we have said, then press above it, and below it quickly: and cut above it, until the confined veins are released: and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it with bodily exercise, and with what expels the veins' windiness, and cleanse them with foul-smelling pills, and pills of serpent's root, and similar things.
On the extraction of arrowheads.
Arrowheads are differentiated according to their own kinds and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is indeed such that some of them have large points, and small ones; and are concave, and rough. And some of them have three angles. And some of them have tongues (or flanges). And some of them have stars (or star-shaped tips).
Those indeed which are according to the limbs in which they fall, are according to two modes. Or indeed the limbs are from principal concave members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and similar ones. When therefore it falls in one of these members, and death signs appear to you, which I will narrate to you afterwards, then it is necessary that you hasten the extraction of that arrowhead from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in many dispositions. And when these bad signs are not apparent to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the limb, then extract it, and cure the wound.
For when an arrow falls on the brain, and penetrates the bone, and exits with a hiss that is above the brain, it is because bad accidents occur from it, such as swooning, and darkness, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasms, and confusion of the intellect, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood flows out from the nostrils or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And a white, cloudy fluid similar to pus flows out from the place of the wound. And there emerges from it something like the decay of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then desist from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not emerge.
The signs of an arrow when it falls on the heart are: that it is near the left nipple; and one feels as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in a empty thing. And perhaps the arrow makes a movement similar to the pulse's movement; and black blood flows out from the wound. And there follows that numbness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore that death is approaching without doubt.
And the sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung is the ejection of foamy blood from the wound; and the parts that follow the neck become inflamed, and the color of the patient changes. And he breathes out a great, high-pitched spirit; and he seeks to attract cold air.
For if the arrow falls on the diaphragm, which is in the chest; then it is near the small ribs; and there occurs a great shortness of breath with vehement pain, with sighing; and all the members of the shoulders are moved.
And if a dart falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested will come out of the wound along with food.
And if a dart falls into the belly and is stuck in it: something foul comes out of the wound, or pus, or intestine which is already disrupted. There is no hope therefore of curing it, nor of extracting the dart.
If a dart falls into the bladder and urine comes out: and something proceeds from it to the outside, and there is strong pain over the weak spot: then you should know that it is mortal.
The remaining limbs, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and upper arm bone, and vertebrae of the back, and shoulder, and hip, and leg, and similar parts from the limbs are saved according to many different arrangements: when a dart does not find an artery or nerve: and it is not a poisonous dart.
And I tell you what I have seen about the nature of these darts, so that you may understand it through this about your treatment.
What is, because a certain man's dart fell into the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. I therefore extracted it from the other side, beneath the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another dart from a Jew, which had fallen into the fat of his eye beneath the lower eyelid: and the dart was already hidden, and I did not succeed in getting it out except for its small end, with which it adhered to his eyelid: and it was a large dart from the Turkish bows, with a smooth iron, on which there were no notches.
The Jew was therefore healed, and no evil accident occurred to his eye.
And I extracted another dart from the throat of a Christian.
And there was an Arab arrow, and it is that which has its points. I cut above it between the two ribs, and it had already sunk into his throat. I operated skilfully with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow which had fallen into his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed for him, and nothing of his symptoms had changed, I operated on the arrow; and I applied my ingenuity to it, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no bad accident befell him.
And I saw a man on whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound was consolidated around it. After seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman on whom an arrow had fallen into her belly, and the wound was consolidated around it, and the arrow remained inside: and nothing of her symptoms changed, and the woman did not find any harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, on whom an arrow had fallen into his face: and the wound was consolidated, and it remained, not causing him much pain: and there were many similar cases like these.
And I extracted an arrow from the Prince's forehead, which had fallen into his nose, and had already deviated to the right side for a short time: and the entire arrow was hidden.
I was called therefore to his cure after the accident of the arrow for three days. I found the wound of the arrow strongly constricted.
I probed it therefore with a subtle instrument, and I did not feel it: and indeed he was finding a puncture and pain under his right armpit. So I spare what was that point of exit from the extremity of the arrow.
I applied a plaster to the place with a plaster in which there was an attractive and digestive power, trusting in myself that the place would apostematize and show me the sign of the arrow, so I might touch it: and nothing happened at the place, indicating that the arrow had not touched the spot.
I treated it continuously with a plaster for many days, and nothing happened, and the wound healed in the meantime. And he remained weak from the extraction of that splinter for some days, until he felt the arrow on a certain day inside his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. I therefore put a sharp corrosive medicine on the wound for many days, until it was opened up: and when I probed it, I felt the subtle extremity of the arrowhead that was stuck in the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine, until the extremity of the arrowhead appeared visibly to us: and it passed with me for a space of time around four months.
Then after the wound had grown larger, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and move them, and it did not obey to come out: then I did not cease to cleverly try and contrive over it with various contrivances, until I succeeded in extracting it on a certain day with well-made forceps, according to the shape of their ends, as described in the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed, physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. I therefore restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and he was healed to full health: and nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the quality of extracting a certain arrowhead, so that you may put that rule and significance on what I did not say.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not understood with narration, nor does the book comprehend it, and the skilled craftsman compares a little over much; and with what is present, over what is absent; and he finds a new operation and a new instrument among the extraneous descriptions of this art when they occur.
I say therefore, that arrowheads do not come out from the members in which they are fixed, unless according to two modes. Or by attraction from the place into which it entered. Or by the opposite part of another.
And that which comes out from where it entered, or is an arrowhead proceeding into a fleshy place: and it is attracted, and comes out.
What if it does not obey at the time of exit from the hour in which it falls: then it is necessary that you leave it for some days, until the flesh that is around it putrefies, and its attraction is alleviated, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in a bone, and does not obey you at the time of exit, then leave it for some days as well; and try it with attraction and motion every day, until it comes out.
What if it does not obey you at the time of exit after days, then it is necessary that you perforate around the arrowhead in that bone on every side with a subtle trephine, until the arrowheads are amplified. Then attract it, and extract it.
If the arrowhead has already been hidden in a place of the body and is concealed and hidden from the senses, then probe it with a tent. If you feel it, then attract it with certain instruments that are suitable for its attraction.
If you cannot get above it, due to the constriction of the wound, and due to the length of the arrowhead in the depth; and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein: then probe above it until the wound is enlarged, and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
What if there are auricles that hold it, then liberate the fixed flesh from them on every side, with all the ingenuity you can, and devices, if you cannot get above the eversion of the flesh in the fracture of the auricles, and their contortion, until it is freed.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrowhead, in whatever place it is, then use the contortion of your hand, with forceps on all parts, until you make it emerge: and be subtle with utmost subtlety, lest the arrowhead breaks, and its attraction becomes difficult for you, and its very extraction.
What if you cannot get above it at one hour, then leave it for some days, until what is around it putrefies. Then touch it again: for the tumor will be alleviated; if indeed the counterflow of blood makes an opposition to you, then administer what we said about the treatment in its chapter: and preserve your strength from incision of the vein, or nerve, or cord.
And administer ingenuity in every way possible to extract the arrowhead: and let it be with facility, and tranquility, or slowness, and firmness, as I have described to you.
And it is necessary that you operate with the arrow-shaped instrument, so that the wounded person may be secondarily shaped according to the figure which he had at the time of the wound inflicted by the arrow. Because it is more beneficial. If however it is not possible for you to do that, then administer what is possible for you according to the figures.
The arrow which emerges from the opposite part of the other, or already proceeds from it to the exterior; or it makes a gibbosity on the extremity of the wound through the sense of the superior part of the adjacent skin, and you see it prominent: therefore cut above it, and let the incision be according to the quantity which the forceps can grasp.
Then draw it out: for it will be easy to extract it afterwards.
If it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to the roundness, until the arrow head becomes fixed in the bone and enlarges itself; then draw it out.
And if not, leave it for some days: then try again, until it comes out.
If the wooden part of the arrow is in it, then push it out with that.
If the wooden part has fallen off, and you are unable to administer an impulse: then introduce into it a concave instrument, so that its concavity may enter into the tail of the arrow: then push it out with that.
If the arrow head is concave, then push it out with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow head is extracted through that.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to concave the entire flesh where the venom is, if that is possible for you. Then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side; and it is near to what you touch with your finger, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and be careful about the incision of a vein, or nerve, and extract it.
Then the wound, if you see it, and treat it, until it heals.
And this is the method of forceps, with which arrows are extracted.
They are like bird's beaks, made as if iron: when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And they are made from them into many kinds, large, small, and medium, according to the quantity of the arrow's magnitude, and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its constriction.
