Of the venous bloodletting.
The veins to be bled, whose course is customary in the body, are thirty.
Out of them, in the head there are sixteen: two pulse veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbae; and two arteries, which appear in the two temples; and two veins which are in the two lacrimal ducts of the eyes, called the Aspicientes; and a vein prepared in the middle of the forehead; and a vein which is at the extremity of the nose. And two Gudgeons, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins that are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins that are cut for bloodletting in the arms are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic vein, and it is from the lateral forested (or wooded) region: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the Brown one. And it is the Middle vein, and it is composed from the branch of the Basilic vein and the branch of the Cephalic vein: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilic vein, and it is placed on the domestic side: and it is also called the Titillating vein. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the Vein of the arm, and it is placed above the bend: and it is the one that is cut for bloodletting, and it is the one that appears above the thumb with a clear manifestation.
And the Salvatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring-shaped (or annular) one and the smallest: and there are two branches to it.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is the vein which is under the curvature of the thumb, from the wooded (or lateral) region. And the second one is the Saphena: and its place is at the heel, on the domestic side. And the vein Nessus: and its place is at the heel, from the wooded (or lateral) region.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
 On bloodletting of two veins behind the Ears.
Bloodletting of the two veins which are behind the Ears is beneficial for old catarrhs, and hemigraine, and sahafatia, and evil ancient head ulcers.
And the quality of bloodletting it is according to what I narrate. And it is, that the head of the sick person should be shaved: and their occiput should be rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, using good friction. Then the weakened neck is constricted with a bandage, until two veins appear. And the places of the ears are behind the aureus (temples) in two depressed areas of the head. Feel therefore with your finger, and where you feel a pulse under your finger, mark it there with a cautery.
Then take a surgical scalpel, which is called an Incider. Then insert it under the vein in the skin, until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then elevate your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and make an incision into the vein with a cutting motion. And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' width, or approximately that: and make it so that blood flows out in abundance.
Then bind it with poultices, and leave it, until it heals.
And now the incision of the ears, and their cauterization, has preceded at the beginning of this book.
The bloodletting of the two Arteries that are in the temples is beneficial for ancient hemigraine: and for worn-out headaches, and persistent eye troubles: and for excessive flow of sharp humors to the eyes.
And the quality of bloodletting them is according to what I will tell you now. The weakened neck is constricted strongly, until two veins appear in a clear and manifest sensation: and the pulse of them is demonstrated under your finger. Then mark it with a cautery.
Then elevate the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the scalpel incidentally from below, and elevate the vein upwards. And make an incision, just as you did on the two other veins: and let blood flow out according to the quantity of your need.
Then loosen the bandage of the injured person and put your finger on the vein for an hour. Next, put a poultice on it and soft pads, and bind it strongly with a tight bandage: and leave it, until it heals.
And now the description of these things precedes, and their incision, and their extraction at the beginning of the book.
The benefit of bloodletting from the forehead vein is after bloodletting of the cephalic veins, for old facial weaknesses: and for hemigraine, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the quality of this bloodletting is, according to what I narrate to you. The injured person should bind his neck with a cloth, until the vein appears. Then take the instrument that is called a Fossorium.
Place the sharp point that is on the head of the Fossorium on the vein, and prick it with a comb or with another instrument in its manner: and let the blood flow according to the quantity you wish. Then loosen the perforation of the injured person, and bind it, until it heals.
And when it is opened with a broad scalpel. However, it is not suitable that the bloodletter should be of acute extremity, like the remaining bloodletters, but rather of broad extremity. And the bloodletter should be according to the transverse section: because the bone is nearby: and perhaps the bloodletter will be broken when it is subtle.
The benefit of the two Veins which are in the two Lacrimal veins of the two eyes, is for an eye affected by itch and redness: and also for filth in the eye, and for facial weaknesses.
And the quality of phlebotomy is such that it constricts the weak neck with its thread. Then the phlebotomist stands: and you stand over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be done crosswise along a short length, with a small phlebotomy instrument, slightly broad, for there is no vein in that place. If the phlebotomy is subtle at the extremity, it will perhaps be fractured.
Then let blood flow out as much as is necessary for you. And put a bandage over them, and constrict them at night with one thread, and loosen them.
The phlebotomy of the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and violent headache, and facial diseases such as hemicrania and redness that occurs on the face: especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that it constricts its weak neck. Then hold its nose with your left hand, and take a subtle, long phlebotomy instrument: and fix it in the middle of the nostril itself, between that which distinguishes the nose according to straightness. Since the vein does not appear to the sense there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you apply your hand with a small phlebotomy instrument: and let blood flow out according to your need. Then bind it at night with one thread. For it is quickly restored.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the veins of the Gizzard is for constriction of asthma, and detention of leprosy, and melancholic diseases, and evil ulcers and corrosive sores, that is, herpes of the gullet.
And the quality of the phlebotomy is that it should tightly constrict the weakened vein under the collarbone with a ligature: and the surgeon should stand above the patient's head, and the patient should be seated on a seat.
Then the phlebotomy vein is opened along the length of the phlebotomy incision with an ample but moderate opening. Then extract from the blood a tempered quantity, or according to what you see is necessary for that which is at hand. Then do similarly with another vein.
Afterwards release the ligature, and constrict the two veins in the middle of the stricture: so that the patient is not harmed. And leave it until morning. The wound should heal.
On the phlebotomy of veins by Algeberich. The veins, according to Algeberich's phlebotomy, are a help: because they bring about an alleviation of bad breath from the mouth, and gum corruption: and evil ulcers: and labial fissures, and evil ulcers, even those that are in the nose, and around it.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is that you make the patient sit in front of you: and constrict his neck with a bandage. Then turn back his lip, and look at the two veins: one of which is on the right side of the lip, and the second on the left side of it: and the demonstration of each is through the blackness of the veins. And that is true: because around them are small, black veins. Make the incision therefore on both sides with an oblique incision.
If however there is doubt in you, and you do not know which of them is: then turn to the incision of the larger one, and that will be more evident.
And do similarly with the two veins that are in the upper lip: and indeed custom has carried it out with him: and make an incision on the two veins that are in the lower lip.
About the venesection of the vein under the tongue after the cephalic venesection. There are two veins under the tongue that require venesection: it is because the cephalic venesection is for prefactions that are in the throat and for the weakness of the uvula, and the weaknesses of the mouth.
And the quality of the one to be venesected is that he should sit weakly between your hands in front of you: and you should lift up his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side for one vein, and on the other side for another vein: and the color of both veins declines towards blackness.
Therefore, venesect one or the other: and be careful, lest your last hand movement in venesecting him causes harm. For under them are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood may occur from those arteries.
The three veins that are venesected in the elbow are those in which the practice of venesection has been established in all men.
And the venesection of them is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a broad and blunt phlebotome, or by shaving to a subtle decline, or by cutting with a razor-like phlebotome, and this is the method of Al-NSR. And this is its form.
And of them there are broad and subtle species according to the amplitude of the veins and their stricture. The form of the myrtle-shaped phlebotome is broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, protruding, apparent, and thick veins, and those that contain gross, turbid blood.
And this phlebotome is of lesser width and more subtle at the extremities.
And it is suitable for phlebotomy of fine veins, which contain subtle, citrus-colored blood.
The royal one, however, which is one of these three veins for phlebotomy, is a help. Because it extracts blood from the diseased parts, which are under the jaw and neck, following the chest, and stomach; and it is suitable for the phlebotomist with his phlebotomy, so that he may beware, and be anxious about the fear of it, because under it there is an artery. If therefore he errs, and adds to the piercing of the phlebotomy, he will cut into that artery, and there will be a flow of blood. Wherefore it is necessary not to perform phlebotomy with a piercing phlebotomist; rather, he should be gentle with the Al-nesil (scalpel).
Because if the royal vein does not appear in a clear and manifest way, then it is necessary to seek another, and to transfer to another, or to explore some other branch of it; or to perform phlebotomy in place of it, the Funem (vein) of the arm. For that is clear, and he should cut it with the Al-nesil phlebotomy, as we have said.
But if you wish to perform phlebotomy on the same one, then it is necessary before the constriction of the arm, that you should touch the place, until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark the spot with a cautery, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein at the curved section with the Al-nesil phlebotomy, as we have said. And beware that the puncture does not slip through the space to the place of the artery.
And when you see that, as you bind the ligature, an inflammation appears at the place which you marked with a cautery, then that inflammation is an inflammation of the artery: therefore, it is necessary to avoid it.
If you see blood in a phlebotomy that is salty, like the urine of a child, and it is subtle, red, then you will know that it is from an arterial bleeding. Then press down firmly, and place your finger on the lengthy space, then lift your finger. If the blood is then let out (for it is often let out), then bind the arm, and loosen it: and beware lest the weak person be moved by its motion: and let it be above his fear.
And do not move it for some days, until it heals.
But if the blood does not flow out, and it overcomes you: and there is no present remedy for you at that moment, then cut into the artery, if it appears to you. For the extremities are constricted, and the blood flows out.
Or take a piece of cloth, and bind it: take one half, and tie it over the place of the stricture of the vein, with a ligature and cotton wool until another day.
Then if the blood flows out, it is good. And if not: then treat it with that which preceded the memory of the place of the powder of blood flow: and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in many dispositions, because of the small place of the wound and the possibility of a ligature from the arm.
The phlebotomy of the Dark Vein is beneficial, because it draws blood from the superior part of the head and the inferior part of the body: therefore, since it is composed of the branch of the Basilic vein and the branch of the Cephalic vein, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the phlebotomist to be above fear from his phlebotomy, for beneath it is a nerve. If therefore an addition is made to the puncture of phlebotomy, and a nerve is found, there occurs in it numbness, and difficult is its healing, and perhaps it will not be fully healed.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. If however it is hidden in certain men, and is subtle, and does not show itself: then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy where the phlebotomists make an incision with Al Nesil, and divide the nerve with your strength.
What if a vein is between two nerves, then find the vein in its length.
Concerning the cephalic vein. The phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is beneficial, because it attracts blood from the head and brings relief to the sufferings of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein particularly, if you wish, to phlebotomize it by pricking with an oily phlebotome or a myrtle phlebotome: because it is the most healthy of all veins, since there is no underlying artery, nor nerve.
However it is fitting for you to proceed with phlebotomy, so that you divide only the skin of the head with a gentle touch, and seek a smoother spot: and it does not harm if it is not found with the first strike, so that it may be repeated over it several times with phlebotomy.
However perhaps an abscess forms in certain men when they are not phlebotomized at the first strike, but that abscess does not harm them in any way.
Concerning the usefulness of Phlebotomy and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of Phlebotomy, and its accidents resulting from it, in whose correction it is necessary for you to think.
Firstly, it is of this that you ought to know, that phlebotomy is not administered unless for the conservation of health, and its perseverance, and caution against the advent of diseases.
And let there be bloodletting on one of the three veins that are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Fusca, and the Basilic. And let there be bloodletting at the beginning of spring, on a fast day, in the morning, and in summer, and autumn, and winter after digestion, when signs of repletion appear.
And let there be bloodletting on the first day or on the third day, after three hours have passed.
Bloodletting that is administered to the sick, does not have a determined hour: but when convenience and necessity call for it, at night, or day, and in every hour, and in every time.
And it is not fitting that bloodletting should be performed on children, until they are fourteen years old. Nor should pregnant women be bloodletted, unless from the fourth month to the seventh. Nor should the old be bloodletted, who have already passed sixty years.
When therefore someone wishes to be bloodletted for any reason whatsoever: it is fitting that they should cleanse their intestines beforehand with a gentle clyster, if there is much retained dung in them: so that the veins may not draw out noxious putridities from the intestines during bloodletting, which are harmful to the principal members.
Nor should the patient be full of loathsome nausea, nor drunk, nor evacuating from a hangover, until that is removed from them. Nor one who is weak with dropsy.
And bloodletting is to be avoided after colic passion, and vomiting, and flux of the stomach, and excessive intercourse, and labor, and exercise, and wakefulness, and fasting, and everything that dissolves the virtue from the bodily or animal strength.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors beforehand, if the blood is thick from food and drinks, and medicines: if it is possible for him.
Then a warm bath is introduced, if it does not prohibit something else; or some exercise is used, so that the blood is subtly loosened.
And apply phlebotomy to his chest, around the third day, as we have said; and take care that his chest is freed from all bad animal influences on that day: such as anxiety, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and excessive flow, and similar things.
And let his own people make present the things that are useful with the customary use of aromatics, and perfumes, and tripping and similar things in proportion to possibility.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion, which is higher than the cushion of the one to be phlebotomized: afterwards he draws out his arm, and rubs it with his hand twice or three times. Then he tightens the ligament with a bandage, and wraps it around twice.
And let the binding be moderate, because when the binding is unequal, or excessive in the binding, then it prevents the flow of blood: or if it is too loose, then it also prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the binding it is necessary to rub the hands of the one to be phlebotomized together gently, until the veins become inflated and are declared to the sense.
Then the phlebotomist wipes the phlebotomy site with a little oil, in the ancient proper way.
Then let him place his index finger of the left hand on that vein under the spot where he wants to perform phlebotomy, gently: so that the vein does not flee and is struck with percussion. Because it is like a fleeing cord when you find it, as if at phlebotomy: and of them there is one that is full of windiness: and when you place the phlebotomy on it, it contracts under the phlebotomy, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and does not open the vein: and if he opens it, it is tight: therefore for this reason it is necessary to make the phlebotomist firm, and to be solicitous in all these things.
Then the scalpel is placed. If therefore a vein is opened from one side, then it is good. And if not, then the other vein under that place is tried a little, or above it with haste: if the place does not become inflamed.
What if it becomes inflamed, or the patient grows weak, then leave him for a day, or two days: and do not bind the dressing tightly. Perhaps indeed it will attract a warm abscess. Nor should a bath be entered.
Then the scalpel is repeated, if it is loved.
What if the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly: and then as not to let out from the blood a quantity that you wish: then repeat the scalpel in the same opening, following the straightness, and add a little in the operation.
And do that with haste, before the place becomes inflamed, for an inflamed place is in many men a small operation.
If therefore you see it already inflamed, then do not repeat anything on top of it at all, for nothing excuses something in your regard. And put on top of it something from the sediment of oil: because it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the sediment of oil in all your bloodlettings, where the bloodletters prohibit the flow of blood: and they make it, that is, the tyrical and sagzenical treatments, when it is put on one of them over the place.
For the blood is subtly purified and resolved, when it is thick.
What if a great abscess happens at the place of the bloodletting, for it often happens, especially to those who are not bloodletted except on that side, or is the opening of the vein small, then prepare and put on the place a sponge infused in water and salt, warmed for a little while: and bind it for one hour: because it dissolves.
And it is suitable to make that opening after the blood has flowed out from the same vein, with its own covering, or from another vein.
If something black or greenish remains in the place after several days, it does not harm: and if you wish, apply to it a solution of aloe, or myrrh, or something from the juice of mint, that is, of calendula or similar.
And indeed it often happens that abscesses and swellings occur at the site of bloodletting according to the method of Basil.
Put your hand therefore upon it, and then if you find softness under your pressure upon it, then that swelling is bad. Take heed therefore, to apply to it something from what we have mentioned. For perhaps blood flows out from the artery.
But make a plaster for it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place may be tightened up. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is suitable to extract from each person a certain amount of blood according to the quantity of his strength. And what appears from the color-changing above the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it flow out until it becomes red, and similarly if you see it as thick, then let it be thinned out until it is refined. And similarly if it is acute, until its acuteness is relieved.
And it is suitable for one who is full, strong, and needs the extraction of blood twice, to amplify the bloodletting of the vein, and let the phlebotomist be skilled. And in one who is weak, let it be the opposite of that.
And it is suitable to extract it in the veins, and that the opening be narrow. And what is better administered in the bloodletting of the vein is a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is healthy for the flow of blood, and from an incision in the vein: and it is more praiseworthy, and healthier than bloodletting by width and length.
And to whom it is customary to arrive at syncopis through bloodletting, it is necessary that they eat before bloodletting a moderate amount of food infused with pomegranate water or rose water, if they are overheated: and let them extract from the blood in three or four veins.
And if they have a cold complexion, then let them take before bloodletting bread infused with warm syrup of honey, or in syrup made from honey and aromatic spices, or in syrup fragrant with aromatics.
Because if syncopis occurs through bloodletting, and it is the cause of much blood flowing out: then it is necessary that they take in drink water of meat, and subtle fragrant syrup: and let them use aromatics with galangal, and anoint their chest with those: and let them use the remaining things we mentioned in the division on syncopis from evacuation.
Whoever wants to evacuate their arm and release their blood a second time: it is necessary for him whose bloodletting is due to much evacuation, and his strength is weak, that he releases blood little by little according to the quantity of his strength on continuous days.
Whoever wants to evacuate their arm and release their blood a second time, and his body is strong: then let him do that after seven hours or nine hours from his first bloodletting.
Whoever wants to attract blood from their body to the opposite part to which it declines: it is necessary that they be evacuated on the second day or the third.
In whom there is already much heated blood in their body, and it becomes sharp, and causes fever to occur: it is necessary that blood exit from them in one vein, and a great quantity be extracted from them: and the opening is widened until syncopis occurs: after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting: and put your hand on their pulse at the course of the blood, so that death does not occur in place of syncopis. For many times it happens that, when the phlebotomist is ignorant and becomes neglectful.
And it is not suitable when you want to release the arm, and release the second blood, since the opening of the vein is already closed, and the exit of blood becomes difficult, so that it bursts out upon it with vehemence, and the curvature extends quickly. For that is a bad thing, indeed to omit it, until it is phlebotomized for the second time.
Or remove it with the acuteness of phlebotomy that which is coagulated from the blood in the opening of the vein, and place upon it something salty dissolved in water. And place upon it something from tyriaca alfaroch or sagzenea, and apply gentle pressure, until the blood flows out.
If indeed the vein has become apostematous, then omit it, and do not touch it, until the apostema settles.
What if necessity provokes the need to release blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either phlebotomize it at that place, or in another arm in another vein.
The veins of the arm are phlebotomized at the place of the Fuscae and Basilicae, when they are not found, and are hidden, because it is composed from them.
And the quality of that phlebotomy is, that it inserts a weak hand into warm water, until it becomes red-hot, and the veins appear manifestly.
Then bind above it with a ligature of moderate constriction. Then make a second phlebotomy across the width gradually, not latitudinally, nor along the length; and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be one that you make above the joint of the hand, just for a short time.
What if the exit of blood is prohibited, then put your hand in warm water, and let the blood flow into the water, until you achieve your necessity.
If you are operating during the summer days, then you should wash your hands repeatedly in warm water. And indeed the flow of blood is greatly promoted by warm water at the time of winter.
And bleeding from this vein is healthier for all veins, since there is no pulsating artery beneath it, nor a nerve.
Bleeding from the vein of the right hand at the salvation of the wrist is beneficial for liver diseases.
And the quality of that bleeding is such that you should bind the wrist with a ligature or your hand, after you have inserted it into warm water, until the vein becomes swollen and is plainly felt. Then you should open the vein by making a transverse incision. And if you cut it completely, it will not harm him in any way.
And be careful, lest you ultimately harm your hand with the bleeding instrument. For beneath it are the naked nerves of the fingers, stripped of their flesh.
Then return your hand to warm water, and allow the blood to flow out in it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein, and its flow will be blocked.
When you have extracted the necessary amount from the blood, then apply oil and salt to the vein: so that it does not congeal too quickly.
And similarly, it is necessary to do the same with every constricted branch.
The benefit of bleeding from the left hand is that it is beneficial for diseases of the spleen; and do the same in this bleeding as you did in the second, equally.
The benefit of bleeding from the Saphena vein is that it is for diseases which are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the womb, and retention of menstruation, and kidney weaknesses, and old sores on the hips and thighs, and similar afflictions.
And the quality of his phlebotomy is that he inserts his ailing foot into warm water and makes friction on it until the veins overflow, and then binds above the joint of the foot with a bandage. And indeed the place of the veins is at the heel, appearing opposite the thumb, and they branch out from it into many ramifications on the surface of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomy is performed on its larger branch, or at the heel in its collection, because it is better and more healthful.
Because if phlebotomy is performed on the surface of the foot, then it should be cautious of the nerves that are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And put your phlebotomy there where you phlebotomize it crosswise, as if you want to cut into it, and let the phlebotomist be skilled.
But if the flow of blood is prohibited, then soak his foot in warm water and let the blood flow into it until it is evacuated.
Because if the phlebotomist makes an error in the first instance, then he should repeat it with phlebotomy at the upper parts for a short time, for the place is healthy and you do not fear evil from it when you are cautious of the nerves, as we said.
And similarly with the Saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The place of the Sciatic veins, as we said, is at the heel on the outer side of the ankle.
And the benefit of his phlebotomy is to alleviate pain in the ankle, when it is due to warm blood.
And the quality of his phlebotomy is that he enters into a warm bath, and fasts; and binds his leg from the side of the ankle up to the heel with four fingers, with a subtle and long thread: for it does not appear except on account of that.
When it appears, then the phlebotomy is performed according to whatever method is possible for you, or crosswise; and it is better, either to cut into it with an incision, or to separate it with a section. The location of it is more hidden, and it itself is deeply concealed in many people.
The location of it is more hidden, and it itself is deeply concealed in many people. Then loosen the constriction, and place a linen cloth over the site of the incision, and bind the area tightly. You will heal quickly.
Of the treatment by cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping glasses are made from horns, and from copper, and from glass.
And cupping is done in two ways indeed. With scarification and bloodletting; and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is in two modes. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the cuppings which are administered with scarification and bloodletting are fourteen places of the body. Of which one is the cupping of Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head; and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck; and the cuppings of Alhagdahim, and they are two layers of the neck from both sides together; and the cupping of the mind, and it is under the jawbone in the lower jaw; and the cupping of the two spatulas; and the cupping of Alachosos above the end of the tailbone; and the cupping of the two fossae, and they are in the middle of the two arms; and the cupping of the two thighs; and the cupping of the two hips.
And indeed the cupping does not extract blood from you through the small scattered veins in the flesh, and because of that, no virtue falls out from them, just as it is the case with phlebotomy. It is not fitting to administer a treatment with fire or without fire for any ailment that arises from excess, until the whole body is evacuated.
Because if necessity provokes ventilation due to ailment or because of habit, we administer it at every hour, at the beginning of the month, at its end, and in the middle, and whenever the time may be.
Because it is so, since there are people who, when the blood increases in them, need its extraction through ventilation, and find heaviness in their head, and swoon.
And there are those who find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And there are those who find itchiness on their face and forehead, and darkness, and dullness in their eyes.
And there are those who scratch the place of their ventilation. And there are those whose laughter increases. And there are those who find the taste of blood in their mouth, or their tongue becomes putrid and spits out blood. And there are those whose sleep increases. And there are those who see in their sleep blood and redness, and wounds, and sores, and things similar to these.
When therefore we see something of these, and it is specifically in the middle third of the month, we prescribe then that ventilation should be done, after two or three hours have passed.
The instrument of ventilation of the neck is this: because it contributes to heaviness in the head and what is poured out to the eyes. But it is fitting that it should be after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this indeed is ventilation in place of cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted that one should use it who is of a cold complexion, or in whom there is a fluxion. For it brings him great harm.
And because of that, it is not suitable for those old people to be treated with those cold afflictions on their heads.
Whoever, however, makes a persistent effort over it, will generate upon himself oblivion.
And because of that, it is suitable to warn the Surgeon, so that he may put his hand with a gentle touch at first towards the lower areas, fearing the generation of oblivion.
Ventosatio or Alkehel is the place of phlebotomy of Fuscae and phlebotomy of Basilicae, and because of that it brings about shortness of breath and constriction of the windpipe, and fissure of the instrument of breathing, and coughing, and repletion.
And it is suitable to apply the Ventosa Alkehel gently, because if it is placed below, it generates debility in the heart and stomach.
Ventosatio indeed is alhagdahim, which brings about painful accidental injuries to the head, and obtalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is the place of phlebotomy of Basilicae.
And it is suitable to warn the Surgeon, not to press his hand with a scarification: so that he may not cut an artery and cause a flow of blood.
Ventosatio under the chin, however, brings about ulcers in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar afflictions from those diseases which are in the mouth: and it stands in place of phlebotomy of alieherich, which are on both lips.
Ventosatio of the spatula shape brings about a tendency to alchafacham, which is caused by the accumulation and heat.
Ventosatio indeed of the internal parts of two veins, brings about an aid to phlebotomy of three veins, Basilicae, Fuscae, and Cephalicae: because it itself attracts blood from all those tiny veins which are in the flesh; and those tiny veins attract from other thicker veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary to be cautious with the scalpel, lest it proceed too far in the incision: because the location is deprived of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
The scalpel, however, which is used to make incisions on the anus, is useful for hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary to be cautious with the scalpel, that it should be large and made of iron: because the location needs a strong incision, and perhaps the subtle veins would be torn apart; and a large scarification would be made by the large incision.
The scalpel, however, diminishes the thighs by a manifest diminution: because they attract blood from the entire body, and contribute to old pains in the kidneys, bladder, and uterus; they provoke menstrual flow, and contribute to bothor, and to ademenul; and they take the place of phlebotomy of the two saphena veins, and of the crab-like [veins].
However, they greatly weaken the body, and cause fainting to occur in many people.
And the phlebotomy of the crab-like veins is closely related to the phlebotomy of the two thighs.
The quality of the position of the scalpel is, that you should place the scalpel in the first empty space, and apply a tempered suction: and do not prolong the position of the scalpel, but place it quickly, and remove it quickly, so that the humors flow to the place of arrival equally: and do not cease to repeat this and continue, until you see the location now red and inflamed, and appears with a reddening of blood: then therefore incise, and apply suction gradually. Then consider the disposition of the body.
For one who has a tender flesh, with rare pores, it is necessary to incise him only once, no more: so that the location does not become ulcerated.
And beware of the scalpel, lest it amplify the incision, and make it too deep: and moderate the suction with ease, and quickness, and gentle movement.
What if there is a thickness in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify it twice. In the first instance, indeed, the way is opened up by subtle refinement of the blood, and its purification; and in the second instance, a thorough and exquisite extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is very foul, then let there be a third scarification: that the ultimate may follow.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or for a short time, one scarification is sufficient for us. What if we wish to extract much blood, we perform multiple scarifications with numerous scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to perform a deep scarification; and the boundary of equal measurement in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is that which needs to be administered from oils at the position of Veins, and what is cautious in vein treatment.
In that case where the skin is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are constricted, it is necessary to anoint the Veins with open, softening, resolving oils: if it is in summer time, such as oil of almonds, or violet oil, or oil from sweet almonds, or oil from cucumber seed.
And if it is in winter time, then oil of narcissus, or oil from lily, or oil of chamomile, or sarpatonin; and similar to these.
If the surplus is thick and cold, then let the oil be bitter, or oil from balsamite, or oil of almonds, or oil of anetine; and similar to these.
If the vein treatment is of large pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary to prohibit the use of oil, and it is necessary for them to wash the Veins with rose water after the vein treatment, or with cold water, or with sun-heated water, or with cucumber water, or with portulaca water; and similarly to these.
That person whose blood is of much humidity, should be purged with vinegar and myrtle water, and sumac: and similarly to them.
But those whose excesses are thick, should be purged with their vinegar and old wine, or with samascus water, or anethum decoction, or chamomile: and similarly to them.
And it is fitting that purging should be done in a bath, or in the steam of a bath, or it is fitting that it should be administered after exiting the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not fitting that someone should sleep after purging.
What is it that one who is to be purged or blood-letted beforehand should be governed?
And after that it is fitting to consider first: then if one who is to be purged or blood-letted is choleric, and they overcome their blood with bitterness and inflammation: then it is fitting to receive cooling things, such as granated unguent: and also endivia with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and juleb: and similar things.
And let his food be chicken, and old beef or goat meat, and seasoned dishes with wild herbs: and similar things.
And he whose constitution is cold, it is fitting to take in a drink a syrup of honey, or of elm, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take a fragrant wine, which is between old and new: and let him be advised to use a small amount of food. And let his food be chicken, and cane berries: and sparrows, that is, small birds: and young pigeons should be sufficient for him.
And it is fitting that the drink of the day of purging and blood-letting be more than food: and it is fitting that there should be given in a drink to certain people at tyriaca hour alfaroch, or medicine from musk, that is, muscat, or alexithe before purging: and before blood-letting, or after: so that the principal members may be comforted, and the blood may be subtly purified. And it is not suitable that they take it in a warm potion.
About wounds without scarification. Wounds which are without scarification are those that are situated on the chest, and spleen, and hip, and on the navel, and kidney area, and groin: because these members do not tolerate scarification upon themselves.
And there is no search through them, unless an attraction occurs from a bloody member to a member: just as we set a wound on the breasts in an abundance of flux of bloody discharge. And we use them, so that we may dissolve the cold wound infection from the member: just as we set a wound on the navel and umbilicus. For it rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes the pain on that account, because it resolves that wound infection.
And also it is applied on the kidneys, when there occurs in them a blockage or stone. For the virtue of its attraction perhaps opens up the blockage, or eradicates the stone from its place.
And it does similarly when applied on the chest and spleen to remove wound infection from both of them.
And these wounds are treated with an ointment alone, without incision. And sometimes they are treated with fire.
And sometimes they are treated with full warm water in abundant fluxes: and that is, so that the wound may be filled, and it be large, with hot water alone, or with water in which herbs that are suitable have been decocted.
The form of wounds treated with fire.
The width of its opening should be two fingers open, according to what we have formed: and the amount of it in depth should be half a palm: and there should be on its side, in the place which is around its middle, a small hole, according to the quantity that enters into the acus: let it be made from copper or tin, with a thick margin, smooth, equal, polished: so that it does not hurt the member at its position upon it: and there should be in its middle a transverse rod of copper or iron, where the candle is placed with fire.
And now this large wound is made, greater than we described, and a little smaller than the other, according to the weakness and age of the one who uses it.
For there are different (wounds) for boys and thin people, which are not the same as those for men and stout bodies.
The quality of placing this wound with fire on the member is such that you should apply a linen cloth with fire to it decently, or a small candle made from wax; and place it over the middle of the transverse vein which is in the middle of the wound: so that the ignited fire may be around the lower part of the wound, so as not to burn the weak body.
Then it should be placed on the member; and the finger should be on the opening which I mentioned, until when we hold the wound: while we are in need, we remove the finger, and the vapor exits through that opening, and the wound is immediately resolved.
Then apply a linen cloth to the fire according to its own method, and repeat this if you need it.
But in the wound which is treated with water on a weak lung, there is no transverse vein, nor an opening; and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and placed only on the member.
And this is its form.
And this wound, the larger it is, so that it may hold a lot of water, is better.
Of the suspension of blood-suckers.
Blood-suckers are not administered in many cases, unless in those members where it is not possible to position wounds, due to their smallness, such as the lip, and gums, and similar ones. Or in a member denuded of flesh, such as the finger, and nose, and similar ones.
And the quality of using them is: that you should choose those Blood-suckers which are in sweet waters, clean and free from putridity.
Then they are left overnight in fresh water, until they become soft, and nothing remains in their belly. Then the body is evacuated at first with bloodletting, and with vomiting. Then the membrane of the affected part is cleaned, until it becomes red. Thereafter, they are applied above it.
When therefore they are filled, and fall, and it is possible to suck the location with vomiting, then it is almost the last resort in healing: and if not, then wash the location with vinegar. Then with much water, and they are rubbed, and pressed out.
What if the flow of blood is prolonged after the injury of the blood vessels, and it is that which is to be washed out: then soak a linen cloth in cold water, and put it above, until the washing out is completed.
What if the blood increases, then powder above it with powdered zeolite or gallic, and similar things from astringent powders: until the blood is stemmed.
Or medio-persing beans are applied above the location, and they are left, until the beans adhere to the location, for the blood is stemmed.
And it is necessary, if repeated bleeding is needed, when possible, to change them.
But if the blood vessels are prohibited from suspending, then the location is cleaned with fresh blood. Or a fig is applied to the locations, until something goes out of the blood. Then they are applied, for they themselves, when they feel something from the blood, adhere immediately.
When therefore they are about to fall, powder above them something from triturated aloe, or salt, or ashes: and they fall immediately.
This chapter is often necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, O sons, that many stupid doctors and the common people boast of this chapter, who have never examined an ancient book, nor read a letter from it.
And because of this cause, it happens that this method is deprived of sciences in our land. And I did not obtain anything good from it at all, and I did not acquire what I acquired except through the length of reading ancient books, and study above their intellect, until I extracted the knowledge from them.
Then I adhered to experience and long study throughout my life.
And now I have described to you from that, in this Chapter, all that contains my science: and about what has passed through my experience, since I abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the occupation of lengthiness, and abbreviated it with a fine abbreviation, and showed it with a fine declension: and I formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments which are administered in it, as it is by addition of declension, just as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
It is fitting, as we say in the prologue of this Chapter, to summarize the discourse and chapters, towards which understanding the artist is first moved to station himself over their truth: namely that you, and those who are prompt, may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture or dislocation or torsion or fall occurs, then it is necessary to phlebotomize him first and to loosen him, or both at the same time: if nothing prohibits it, such as debility of virtue; or if he is one who has experienced something from those things in childhood, or an elderly decrepit person; or if it is a time of vehement heat or vehement cold.
Then let him be content with his usual food, cold dishes, and birds' meat, and game; and prohibit wine, and gross meats, and refrain from foods that fill the veins full of blood. Until when he is secure from the aposteme of heat, and does not at that time have an effusion of matter to a limb; then the sick person returns to his first regime with which his custom had been accustomed.
When therefore the fractured bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods of much nutriment, thick, heavy, in which there is viscosity; such as dishes made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and entrails of goats, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things. For with this regimen the consolidation of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that fractured bones, when they are in people who are in early old age and decrepit, it is not possible for them to be consolidated and consoled according to their natural order forever, on account of the dryness of their members; and they are consolidated and continued which are from them in the end of lenity, such as the bones of small children.
But truly nature adds to the fractured bone from all sides something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds, until they cohere and are tied together, until they come to a state of strength and firmness, as they were at first, so that nothing prohibits them from their operations: and for this reason it is necessary, that you put nourishing food before the sick person, in which there are heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of members; for a fracture of the thigh is different from a fracture of the skull bone, and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone; and similarly all remaining members have distinct differences in their fracture species; and I will come with remembrance of each species of fracture exhibited distinctly in its own chapter. And the species of bone fractures are also differentiated even within themselves.
Because there is an arched fracture, besides what happens to it in its ridges. And when there is a fracture of it according to the length of the bone; and its fractures are jagged, that is, ridges, and additions proceeding, just as they are in a reed, when it is fitted with a knife for writing. And not proceeding; and it is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head; and it is a small fissure fracture.
And every species has its own ingenuity, or proper instrument in its restoration, according to what comes to mind about it, distinctly in its own places.
And from that by which the fracture of the bone is known, is its tortuousness, and its own eminences; and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
When therefore there is no tortuousness appearing in the place, nor sound upon touch with your agitation, nor does the weak one feel great pain: then there is no fracture there, or it is possible that there is a tortuousness, or small fissure; and so it is not necessary to move it with your hand and compression altogether, but rather put medicine on it, whose remembrance comes at the moment, after the opportune time arrives in that place: then let the subtle strictura be tightened.
And know that the place when it is arched and divided into two, besides what happens to it in its ridges, indeed all parts are removed from their comparison: then it is necessary for you to begin from your hour to rectify it, making it equal before a hot aposteme occurs.
If a warm abscess occurs in it, then leave it for some days, until the apostema quiets down. Then treat it equally according to whatever method he can from gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and its equalization are easier than of bones, in which splints are applied.
Then bind it, according to what will seem to you a reminder of it.
If indeed there are splints on the bone, then extension of this fractured member is necessary from two parts, if it is a hand or foot: or with your hand, if it is a small member: or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand.
And let your position be where you place the member above an equal place, according to its natural figure, until when the parts of the fractured member are extended: then strive, that you may restore those additions with all skill, and according to whatever method you can in their places: and beware according to your strength, lest pain or injury occur on the weakened part through your operation: and strive according to your strength, that you may apply one of the two bones with its companion, according to the better forms.
And it is necessary at that time, that you should touch and feel it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it according to the quantity of your strength.
And beware of vehement extension and strong compression, as many of the foolish do. For they often cause a warm abscess to occur through their operation, or a contraction in the member: as we have already seen that from their operations many times.
Then connect after equalization, and a decent operation, and the stricture of that member, and quiet, and tranquility: and warn the weakened part about it. And make it cautious, lest it moves in its hour of watchfulness, and its own sleep at its revolution, and its agitation, and at its evacuation, and all its movements with its ultimate strength. And take care, lest the position of the member be a position that follows pain. What is, because when it feels pain in the positioning of the member or injury: then it is necessary, that you change it to another positioning where it does not feel pain.
And take care with that, so its position is equal and straight: so that no crookedness occurs in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the structure of the broken member is, according to what I relate.
You should know that fractured members are differentiated in their smallness, and their magnitude, and their own form.
Those therefore which are small among them, such as the arm, and finger, and neck, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, fine cloth. Those however which are large among them, such as the hip, and leg, it is necessary that there be a wrapping of broad, hard cloth.
Because the wide bandage connects the large member and binds it from every side with equal tension, so that it does not gird it with a constriction.
Then begin to put a suitable plaster next to the equality of your evacuation, on a soft cushion above the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage above the place of the fracture itself with three or four wrappings, according to what the member deserves: and bind your hand briefly with the bandage.
Then go with it to the upper part above the place of the fracture, and bind less than was your previous tension, by which you bound the fractured place. Then stretch yourself with the wrapping above the place of the fracture briefly, and gradually loosen the tension, until you grasp from the healthy place a great thing.
Then take another wrapping, and wrap it again above the fractured place with wrappings. Then look with attention to the inferior part at the fracture. And let your operation be in the tightening of the involution, and its softening, according to what we said in the first upper involution.
Then put between the involutions a clean linen cloth or a piece of cloth with which the twistedness of the fracture is equalized, if there is twistedness in it; and if not, do not put anything on it.
Then envelop above that same wound another. Then place equalizing splints on these involutions, made to fit exactly, if there is no swelling in the location, nor hot aposteme.
If there is swelling or an aposteme in it: then place on it what will settle that aposteme and reduce the swelling; and leave it for some days. Then bind above it those splints.
And let the splints be made from mid-ribs of broad cane, smoothed, skillfully shaped: or let them be made from wood where cribra are made, which are made from pine, or cedar, that is, palm branches, or walnut: and let the width of all the splints be according to this form and this same shape.
However, it is necessary that the splint which is placed above the wound be somewhat thicker and broader than the other splints: and the length of the splint should be according to what is permitted by the member in terms of size and smallness.
Then bind above the splints with another cloth, according to the first strictura, the same. Then tie above with threads exactly, according to what we said about the strictura: and it is, that your strictura should be more over the place of the fracture: and as much as the fracture is longer, the strictura is smaller.
And it is necessary, that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and let them be made from soft flax.
Since the threads are thick, as is seen from the operation of stupid surgeons who place their own threads twisted like a linen rope, it is a great mistake: because the structure collapses with them beyond its quality.
And fine threads do not fit again: because you do not achieve through the structure with them that which you wish.
And it is not suitable, so that it is between the star-shaped (formation) and the star-shaped (formation) less than a finger.
What if the weak place is injured through the extremities of the star-shaped (formations) after the structure in healthy places: then put under them a piece of clean linen or Carmanian wool, until nothing is injured by that.
When, however, there is a fracture with an ulcer, and disruption occurs in the skin, I describe it with remembrance of its singularity in the second chapter.
And know that not every fractured member needs to be bound with star-shaped (formations) from the first day. What is, that because the member is large, it does not need to be bound with star-shaped (formations) unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the occurrence of a hot aposteme.
And this is the method of plasterings, which the ancients used to restore, which are placed upon fractures and dislocations.
The method of common plastering, abbreviated, restoring fractures, suitable for many complexions, especially for women and children: because it is based on that which neither heat nor cold overcomes.
And it is, that you take from the dust of the mill, and it is the marrow of the flour which clings to the walls of the mill at the movement of the millstone: and you mix it as it is, except that you sieve it with a fine sieve: and you put its confection not thick, nor subtle. Then use it.
Another method of applying a plaster for restoring a fracture, and a dislocation, and a twisting. Take mess, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugath; and juice of all, a tenth X of myrrh, and aloe of both, a fifth V, and alethil, twenty times, they grind everything, and sieve, and mix with water, or with white wax, if the injured part is inflamed. Then apply this plaster. For it is of good composition, and quickly restores a fractured bone, and helps many people.
Another method of applying a plaster, which also helps fractures and twistings. Take mugath, and mess, and white plantain, of all a tenth X of myrrh, and aloe of both a fifth V, acacia a sixth VI, Armenian bole twenty times, they grind everything in many contritions, and sieve, and mix with water, or with white wax; and administer.
Another method of applying a plaster for joints and separated bones in their places; and it soothes the accidental pain, and division of bone, and fracture. Take a layer of suet, and soak it in vinegar, and oil decoction; and place it on the affected area.
And in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good for soothing hot apostemes; and it drives out pains properly.
Another method of applying a plaster that restores a fractured bone. Take leaves of figs of the forest, and leaves of silvan papyrus; and grind everything, and make a plaster from both.
A method of applying a shortened plaster for administering at the restoration of a fractured bone; and when you want to resolve the remaining inflammation. Take roots of plantain, and chamomile, and flowers of violet, and herb powder, all in one part; they grind everything, and mix with wine, if there is no inflamed member.
If it is inflamed, then apply it with water of coriander in a humid state, or with water, and use it thus.
Another mode of plastering for a stronger resolution, which is the first one, when there occurs a hard aposteme during the restoration of a limb. Recipe of roots of altea, and seeds of flax, and balaustiae, and melliloti, and samsuci, and violet flowers, and chamomile, all equal parts, they are ground into a powder, and mixed with water of willow, or sweet water, or wine. All that is applied according to the warmth of the limb, and the tranquility of its heat.
Hippocrates indeed did not say in his book that anything should be applied to a fractured limb for its restoration, unless it is made with cerates and oil; nothing else; and he narrated that it should be a medium between thickness and subtlety.
But to Galen it seemed best that those things should be applied to a fractured limb for its restoration, in which there is an extraction of warmth, such as myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense, and similar things from those which we have narrated.
A mode of plastering that is suitable for a wound and pain. Recipe of mugwort, and cicers, and hair of human incisors, or bird's feather, and altea, equal parts; they are ground into a powder, and sieved, and mixed together; and let an plaster be made from it.
The quantity however is according to what is fitting, so that the bandage remains, then it is dissolved, it is, as you should consider then, if there does not occur weakness of pain, nor itching, nor the fractured bone is moved from its place; then it is not resolved for many days. But if there occurs to it in its place a severe itching, or disturbing pain, or inflammation: then remove quickly, and dissolve it at that hour, and take away the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a gentle marine sponge, humid; and submerge it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it, until the itching is quieted, and the pain is assuaged.
But if there occurs to it in its place a severe itching, or disturbing pain, or inflammation: then remove quickly, and dissolve it at that hour, and take away the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a gentle marine sponge, humid; and submerge it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it, until the itching is quieted, and the pain is assuaged. Then let the member rest for an hour. Then put on it a compress of succidum, soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it on at night for one day, until the member is free from inflammation, and the pain is relieved.
Then apply a subtle stricture to it, and a small plaster: and do not bind it with your first constraint, but subtly, until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and inflammation, and all the accidents are now completely removed, and you have applied plaster and stricture, then apply it just as you did at first, equally.
If something from what we have said does not happen to the member, then do not release it, unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is left for twenty days altogether, according to what appears to you from the solution of the member, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and the coagulation of flesh approaches upon it: add also then in the stricture more than your first entire stricture: and add also in the thickening of food for the infirm, according to what the previous remembrance of it was.
If you see that the place of the fracture is already drying out and thinning more than it should: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Bathe it therefore with lukewarm water at every opportunity where you release it, and let it be so for three days, and loosen the stricture a little.
Through this operation, nourishment is brought to the member, and it is healed quickly.
That which foolish restorers do from a fracture of the member at another time, when it is not restored at first, according to what is proper, and is restored according to their twistedness, is an error in their operation, and great fear: and if it were right, the ancients would have written that in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have not seen any of their signs in that entire thing, and it is more correct, so that it does not become that.
Concerning the fracture of an accidental wound on the head.
The species of fractures are diverse, and its figures are many: and their causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture that occurs from a blow with a tool, and the blow carries through the whole bone until it reaches the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an axis does in wood; and on account of this, this species of fracture is called Axial.
Or the tool strikes something of the bone, and strikes its surface, and does not penetrate to the very end of it: and this species of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Absolute Elevation.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is a compression, or an abrasion: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which already approaches the membrane, which is under the bone: or it is on the surface of the bone: and it is a wound of these two fractures, also either small or large.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone in the subtlety of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and on account of this, this species of fracture is called Capillary.
And there is a fracture that occurs from a fall or a blow with a stone, and similar things, which cause the lamina of the bone to ingress inward, and it becomes concave: just as it happens to copper vessels when they are struck and a part of them ingresses inward.
And it very often occurs in the moist bones of the head, such as are the heads of children.
And there are fractures of all these kinds, separated and distinct, and I describe them with a reminder of the entire thing in its proper place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fracture through examination on it, and inquiry into them with stretched-out parts, and removal of corrupted flesh which is upon them.
The hairy species is recognized by this, that it is detected on the bone, and is scraped off, and is anointed over it with a warm poultice: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the injured person: then if you see from the accidents what they signify by manifest signs on the fear, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of intellect, and interruption of speech, and fainting, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar things from the accidents in many dispositions, it is doubtful that it will not be cured: and if you see non-threatening accidents, and hope for health: then begin its treatment.
What is, if the wounded person comes to you at first with a wound: and it is during the winter days, then it is necessary that you should focus on removing the bone splinters before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is during the summer days, then it is necessary that you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, lest it becomes corrupted by the pus under the bone, and those accidents occur which we have described.
What if the fracture of the bone has now reached the membrane touching the brain, and it is with smallness, and attraction: then it is necessary that you should cut into the comminuted part, and the attracted part, according to what I relate to you.
And it is, that you should shave the head of the wounded person: and detect the bone according to whatever method is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is lightly upon the injured part.
If it happens to you during the dissection of a bone flux with bloody discharge, or a warm aposteme: then apply to it what is appropriate. And it is, that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and rose oil: and make the wound rest, until the aposteme is settled, and you are secure from the bloody discharge. Then begin with the cure of the bone, and its ablution.
And that is done according to one of two modes.
One mode indeed is, that you cut the bone with a strict incision of the acie: and this is its form.
Then administer another instrument after the incision, wider than it previously: and this is its form.
Then administer also another instrument, even wider also than the second.
And the second mode is, that you have a number of diverse incision instruments: some of which are wider than others, and some of which are shorter than others: and they should be at the end of their sharpened extremities, and they should be made of iron, or forged: and use facility in percussion on the incision instrument, so that it does not strike the head, and hurts itself.
If the bone is strong and hard: then it is necessary, that you perforate around it, before you administer incision instruments, with drills which are called non-penetrating Drills: and they are not called thus, unless because they themselves do not transcend the boundary of the fleshy bone to that which is behind it: for this reason, because the Drill is a round extremity above that which is under its sharp head, similar to an edge, and a small circle prevents submersion, and transgresses the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take from these drills a large number, each of which is suited to the quantity of the bone's thickness, until there is present to you an entire set of drills: whose sharp extremity's quantity in length and breadth is, according to the quantity of the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three species of skull saws, or trephines.
The quality of piercing around the broken bone is such that you place the trephine on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated. Then move the trephine to another location, and put what is in every hole the quantity of the thickness of the radius, or around that.
Then cut with incisors what is in every hole from the bone: and make it as subtle as you can on that from the fineness of the levity, as we said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other instrument, which you prepared for that: such as forceps, and subtle pincers.
And it is necessary to beware with all caution, lest you touch the trephine or incisors with anything of the syphon.
When you are circumcising the bone by piercing, and it has been separated from the syphon, if it was continuous with it, and you extract it, it is necessary to scrape, and equally smooth out what remains of it in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor. However, it is necessary that it be thin and more subtle than other incisors.
What remains of small bone fragments and sawdust: then take them with ease, along with what is at hand among your instruments. Then treat the wound after that with bandages and ointments which we mentioned.
And another method of operation is easy, far removed from fear: which Galen said and praised with great praise: and this is his statement. It is necessary for you first to begin to uncover the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is most vehement and manifest: until when you have uncovered that part, and placed underneath it the extremity of this lenticular incisor, whose form this is.
And the concave part does not cut anything, and its acute part on both sides of it descends in length, as you see; so that the concave part is inclined to the siphach, with its acute part falling onto the bone of the face.
Then strike above the Incisor with one side of a small hammer, until you cut through the whole bone with ease, as it is revolved: and you should be careful with the panniculus, lest something happens to it, which you fear may occur.
And if the surgeon were not more stupid than men, and more ignorant of it, nor asleep, then if something remained attached to the bone from the panniculus in certain places, he would cut it away from there with the extremity of the lenticular incisor, and release it from there with ease. For it is released from there without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the panniculum, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and subtle projections remain in it: then it is necessary, so that you scrape off that roughness, and eradicate those projections with subtle rasps which you have prepared in a diverse quantity of numbers, so that it may be possible for you to administer them in every convenient place, and more direct ones, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the fractured bone, and its shape. And it is necessary, that you administer in your rasping, where first you scrape the bone, more broadly with those rasps. Then use afterwards a more subtle one.
And it is necessary, that you administer in your rasping, where first you scrape the bone, more broadly with those rasps. Then use afterwards a more subtle one. And do not cease to do that according to continuity, until you arrive at administering the most subtle and fine of all.
In each of the remaining small cracks and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer care, according to that which leads to its rectification.
And it is a thing that is not hidden from everyone, to whom there is any study in this art, and he stands upon what we have written, and shows in great fractures.
If, however, a piece of cloth is detected and retained, when you extract the bone: then it is necessary to take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and put it on the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in wine and rose oil, and put it on top of the first cloth: and do that which you can most easily do to it, so that it does not become swollen.
Then apply a wide bandage on top of it: and do not tighten it, unless by the amount that holds the wound together just so; and leave it with this care for one day, or two days, until you are secure from a cold aposteme. Then dissolve it, and apply afterwards medicines with which there is excessive dryness: such as the roots of lily, and herb flour, and powder of frankincense, and aristolochia, and similar things: and it is, that you make from these medicines a powder, which is powdered over the wound as they are dry.
And universally apply to it all medicine from its property that is to cleanse and not to bite. And beware according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment is clear: neither should there be foulness of ointment in it, nor foulness.
And beware according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment is clear: neither should there be foulness of ointment in it, nor foulness. Nor should you allow pus to collect in it at all: because when pus collects on the surface of the brain, it corrupts it, and putrefies it, and a great evil happens to the weak person after that; and now it happens in the siphac of the head, when the bone is cleaned from it, especially when the care of it is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Consider therefore, at that time, if an infirmity occurs which we have described, know that it is a perilous and dubious outcome.
And if blackening does not occur, unless from the medicine that is applied to it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine, so as to bring about that blackening: then it is necessary to take one part from honey, three parts from rose oil, and to apply a linen cloth with them. Then put it on the wound; afterwards treat it with the remedies of healing that are suitable, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when it is fractured.
Know that the nose is not fractured except for its two upper parts together, or one of them: because they are two bones; since its lower part is cartilaginous, it is not fractured, and does not happen to it unless there is attrition, and tortuousness, and similitude.
If one of its two parts is fractured, then it is necessary to introduce a small finger into the nostril and adjust that fracture with your index finger and thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape: and let this be done skillfully, with ease. And take care, lest it happens through your operation that the wounded part suffers pain.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and does not reach to the finger: then it is necessary to adjust the extremity of the radius, where there is a small swelling.
If indeed the fracture is in two parts, then do similarly to that: and take care with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at the time of quiet and warm application.
Then introduce into the extremity of the nose a swathe of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part: or introduce two swathes if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be in the swathe a swelling of such quantity as to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said that they should be soaked in butter and changed every day. But it does not seem to me, rather it is fitting, that the ligature be soaked in a decoction of egg white, mixed with powder of millet. Then release the ligature until the bone is strengthened, and the cartilage is induced.
And when a feather of a goose is inserted into the nostril in place of the ligature, after it has been wrapped with a soft cloth: and it happens that it holds the fracture of the nose more strongly, and so that it does not prevent the patient from breathing.
And this is not a necessary thing. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, do the ligatures.
What if the nose becomes irritated again while you are operating with a warm apostemum, apply to the nose a plaster made with cedar, or with wax infused in rose oil, or a moderate amount of dyaulion.
If the warm apostemum does not irritate, then it is fitting to apply a plaster made with flour of fenugreek, and powder of frankincense mixed with egg white. Then put a soft padding over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are broken into small fragments, or are crushed, then it is fitting to separate them from each other, and to extract them with instruments that are suitable for this. Then sew it up, and care for it with what promotes flesh growth, and consolidate it with suitable plasters or ointments.
What if a wound occurs between the nose, then it is fitting to care for it with ligatures: and apply a tube of lead, until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is dislocated.
When the lower jawbone is dislocated and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then consider if its fracture is only external, and it does not break into two pieces, but curves inwardly; then its recognition is easy.
It is necessary, therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, to insert the index finger of your left hand into the mouth of the injured person; similarly, if the fracture is on the left jawbone, then insert the index finger of your right hand: and push with it the curvature of the fracture from inside outwards with ease, using your other hand on the inner side of the bone to guide it.
When the fracture of the jawbone has already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to administer extension from both parts, according to straightness, until its alignment becomes possible.
If there has been a concussion or disruption of the teeth, then bind them with a thread of gold, silver, or silk. Then place a compress over the fractured jawbone. Then place over it a doubled cloth, and place over the cloth a large star-shaped fixture made to fit; or an equal piece of leather from the sole of a shoe, along the length of the jawbone.
