Then bind it above, according to the preparation of the ligature that is made for you, and its application is suitable, so that it does not come apart; and advise the injured person to be calm and quiet; and give their food in soft, easy-to-swallow bits.
If you think that something has already changed in the shape of the jawbone in some way, then wait and release it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered from it, and apply a plaster made of flour mill dust, with egg white, or with wheat flour, after you have removed the compress; and place a soft wool over the plaster.
Therefore, when that plaster remains applied over it and the bone's disposition is not altered, leave it, lest you disturb the healing; or it may exacerbate the fracture. For this fracture is often exacerbated during the seven days of treatment.
What if during that time a hot abscess should occur, then administer what we have mentioned many times in its treatment, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with an open wound, be careful. For now the bone fragment is separated from the ossicle, or from a splinter; then be subtle in this, so that you may remove those splinters with what is convenient to you in their removal by instruments.
If the orifice of the wound is constricted, then dilate it with a fleam according to the quantity of your necessity.
Then when you have removed the splinters, and none of them remain, thus then the orifice of the wound, if it is wide: and if not, place over it from plasters that are suitable for that, and they will heal it, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the Crural bone, when it is fractured.
The Crural bone is indeed frequently fractured from the front part of the humerus, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Or it is fractured and separated into two, apart from what happens with those splinters: and it is easier to restore it.
Or it happens as with the bone splinters: and it is more difficult to restore.
Or it is a fracture with an open wound.
And the operation in this case when the fracture is without an open wound is, to have two assistants present, one of whom holds the instrument that follows the fractured Crural bone, and the other extends the neck towards the part.
Then make an equal fracture of your fingers, so that its shape is formed according to what is needed, and it does not have prominence in it, nor concavities.
What if you need more extension, then it is necessary to place a small sphere under the cloth or wool: and its size should be according to your necessity: and extend and elevate the forearm, and bind the sphere with your hand, so that it makes an equal fracture, according to what is needed.
What if you cannot draw out the forearm to the exterior: because it penetrates to the deep: then it is necessary to rest the weak one on his neck: and place a medium-sized cushion under his shoulder, and press the ministering shoulder downwards, so that the bone of the forearm which is in the depths rises upwards: and then rectify the fracture, and make it equal with your fingers.
If you feel a crepitus coming from the forearm and it moves: then it is necessary to fix above that, and extract it as a dislocated thing with ease.
If it is retained in the bone, then cleverly make an incision with one of the incisors you prepared for that, after you have placed under the forearm an instrument that holds a splint: and the instrument is made of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It is similar to a spoon which has no concavities.
And its width should be according to what you need for it from the size of the bone and its smallness. Its length, indeed, is also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And it should have two extremities, as you see: and one extremity should be wide, and the other narrow.
If the disruption of the wound which you made when you extracted the dislocated bone is wide, and it is secure from the hot aposteme: then join the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or a warm poultice is applied: then fill the wound with lint and soft cloths according to the quantity of the wound's separation.
What if a warm poultice is applied, then soak a cloth in rose oil and vinegar and wine, and put it on top of it.
A stiffness of the bone occurs when there is no wound and no fracture, it is, that you should put on the bone a plaster made from flour of millet with albumen of egg. And put a soft padding over it. Then put a bandage under the armpit, if it is needed there. Then take a bandage, that is, a strap which the Saracens hold, which should be long indeed: and its width should be one palm, or about that. Then put a double layer of soft cloth over the padding and plaster. Then take a splint from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in a cloth. Then wrap the cloth with the splint in the place of the bandage that falls on the fracture and location. Then put the bandage on the fracture as it is wrapped, and wrap it around the neck, and under the healthy armpit, and under the diseased armpit: and reduce it over every part, and in such a way that you see that the bandage holds the fracture in place decently: and it is thus, that it is not hidden from you, and all, so that the splint does not move away from the fractured bone. And because of this, it is necessary to examine the diseased part every day. As often as the bandage becomes loosened, and you see the splint is already being removed: then straighten it out, and tighten the bandage.
Then put the patient to sleep on their back, and place a small pillow under their side near their hip at night, so that it may be raised as an aid to their lateral region. It is elevated therefore the fracture of the elbow through the elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck: and do not loosen the binding, unless it becomes irritated at the site of the irritation, or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the dressing, if you see his face is turning to that. And add a tightening, and leave it, until it is restored and coagulated.
And indeed the fracture of the elbow is tightened and comforted according to the greatest extent in twenty-eight days; and sometimes it happens in certain men in a shorter time.
On the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder blade.
The shoulder blade is rarely fractured, and it is broken in its broad part: and they are not broken except at their edges.
When therefore it is fractured at that place, and it is broken in the middle of it, and it is not recognized except by touch; then according to the shape of the fracture prepare its treatment. And reduce it to its natural shape in every way of ingenuity, as much as is possible for you.
Then put on the spot a powder of millet with egg white, and a gentle plaster, and put above it a small pillow made from folded cloth. Then put on it a broad star-shaped [bandage] from a subtle tablecloth according to the quantity of the whole shoulder blade, or slightly larger.
If indeed under the star-shaped [bandage] there is a concavity at some place of the shoulder blade, then equalize that concavity with a gentle plaster, until the star-shaped [bandage] descends according to equality.
Then the suture is placed above with a long thread, making a decent tightening, and firmness of the stars, until it does not move from its place.
And consider the ligature in every way. As often as the ligature is loosened, tighten it, and make the star equal if it moves from its place; and let it be weakened over its healthy side.
And indeed the splint is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is complete, then loosen the ligature; and you will be in security. For it is from bones whose joining is not feared, nor their separation.
If a fragment proceeds from the bone and pierces under the skin: then split above it, and remove it. And do what we said about the fracture of the ankle joint from the treatment of a hot aposteme, if something happens to it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured rib.
The breastbone when it is broken in its middle, and that rarely happens. Its extremities are indeed frequently broken, and are dislocated from accidents to it.
When its middle is broken, which declines to the lower parts, and a severe pain happens to it, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing; and perhaps it vomits blood, and the fracture in the bone is declared, and does not hide from the senses. Its restoration is, that the weakened part should be raised above its back: and place among splints of its mattress.
Then press its shoulders, and bring together the ribs from both sides or flanks with hands: and adjust it in its equilibrium, according to every method by which it is possible and convenient, until the shape of the bone returns, according to what is necessary.
Then put on top of it a plaster and a compress, and place above it a thin plate made from willow or tamarisk, or similar things in a gentle manner, after you have wrapped it in a cloth. Then bind it gently over the fractured bone, so that it is not dislocated: and take care with the binding according to the roundness of the back several times, and constrict it with a suitable strapping.
Then consider the binding at every hour, and as often as it becomes soft, constrict it: and if the necessity of it provokes a solution for its itching, or the corrosion that occurs in the place, or pain, or abscess: then be ready, and release it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has happened with the method of its cure.
Then reduce the plaster, if you see that it is returning to its former condition: and apply a bandage to it, until it is healed.
On the restoration of ribs, when they are fractured.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur, unless in thick places which follow the back, and from the extremities of these from the front part. And it does not happen to them with bruising, unless because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of the same is not hidden from the sense during examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in this way, that you equalize the fracture with your fingers according to the possible method, until it becomes equal in shape, according to what is necessary. Then apply a plaster, and constrict the fractured bone with a thin plate, if it needs that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclined towards the interior: then it happens that a severe and vehement pain occurs, and punctures, like the puncture that happens to one who has pleurisy: because, since the bone pierces inwardly, and also difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood occurs: and this is difficult to cure.
And now the ancients were ingenious in this with many ingenuities: and among them are those who say that you should place foods that generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the stomach may become inflamed and extended, and the fracture is driven outward.
We indeed reject this, lest it not make a bad cause for creating a hot abscess, if it does not happen. But if it has already happened, then he adds to it there.
And some of them said: let a poultice be applied to the windy place, then it should be pressed with force, and this is more like a rational treatment. However, it is feared that the poultice attracts windy superfluities to the location, on account of a debilitating disposition.
And some of them said: it is fitting that the location be covered with wool that has already been steeped in hot oil: and poultices are applied between the ribs, until it is full: so that there may be an equal ligature when it is wrapped around according to its roundness.
Then the patient should be treated with a treatment of pleurisy from food and medicine.
Since the severe disease oppresses the patient, which he does not tolerate, and the sharp pain of the pointed puncture wounds, and we fear for the patient: then it is necessary to dissect above the location, and to separate from the broken rib. Then we should place on it an instrument that preserves the suture, whose memory has preceded: and we should make an incision with ease, and we should cut into it.
Then we should bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture: and we should treat it with plasters, until it heals.
Since if a hot abscess happens in the meantime, then it is fitting, and inject poultices soaked in rose oil, and apply them to the location: and the patient should be treated with what is suitable for the abscess.
In the meantime, let him be positioned on his side where he slept, until he is healed.
On the restoration of fractures of the vertebral column of the back and neck.
When a fracture occurs in the neck bones, and it rarely happens to them with attrition, and similarly to the vertebral column of the back. When that happens to someone, and you want to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider it. For if you see his hands already softened, and numb, and dead: and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor constrict them: and when you constrict them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and he does not feel pain in them: then know that he will not be healed, and in many cases it is mortal.
But if he moves them, and feels the constriction with your fingers and the prick: then know that the vertebra is sound, and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
But if it happens to the vertebral column of the back similarly, and you want to know whether he will be healed or not: then consider his feet: then if you see that they are softened, and the same thing happens to them as happened to the hands: next when he lies on his back, wind and excretion come out without his will: and when he is positioned on his belly, excretion comes out without his will: and when he is positioned on his back, and he wants to urinate, but cannot: then know that it is mortal. Do not therefore labor in his treatment.
If nothing happens to him from that, the case is lighter.
And the treatment for what happened to him from that is, that you should try a gentle sedative application: thus, that you place rose oil alone on the affected vertebral column: or with egg yolks boiled in water.
Place it on the affected area several times a day, until when the aposteme is warm: then place one of the comforting, drying, and constricting plasters over the area, and bind it with a ligature: and especially for the weakened person, so that they may use quiet and tranquility. Let them not sleep on the affected part with which they find pain, until they are healed.
If it happens to be a bone worn away by friction, or something that is already separated from it: then it is necessary to sew up the skin, and remove that. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture. Then treat it with some incarnative ointment, until it heals.
If indeed the end of the bone is fractured and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and with the other hand grasp the broken bone, according to what is possible, and in a secondary adjustment.
Then place it on top, and remove it: and treat the wound with the treatment that preceded, until it heals.
On the restoration of the ankle.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the ankle are rarely fractured. When they are fractured, then the fracture of them is not without a comminution at their extremities: and they split along their length, and decline towards the interior: and there occurs weakness, pain in the place, and numbness: and the leg that is fractured becomes numb.
And its restoration indeed is, to extend your hand over it, until you stand on the fracture in whatever shape it is.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then equalize that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from the adjustment, until it is assimilated to its natural shape.
If the fracture is longitudinal or already declined inward, then make the injured person lie on their belly until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When you have aligned it, put a plaster on it; then place a wooden or leather splint on it, and tighten the bandage over it so that you do not fear a change in the fracture or a removal of the splint.
And adjust the concavity of those with what fills it, until it takes hold of it according to its equality; and order the injured person to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
If a hot abscess occurs to him: then cease from extending it and its restoration, until the hot abscess settles; and put on it what settles it, according to what has preceded, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is necessary.
If a fragment occurs in the bone, or becomes detached, extract it.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the helpers.
The helper is that which is between the elbow and the head of the Spatula (a type of surgical instrument).
If it is therefore fractured, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: to take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this shape that follows.
And two ligatures are tied at both ends.
Then it is suspended from an elevated place: and the injured person sits on a seat. Then he places his fractured arm over the wood, until it reaches the tender part of it, in the middle of which there is a curved attachment of the wood.
Then hang something heavy above it, so that it stretches it out downwards to the lower parts. Then the physician adjusts the fracture with his hands, until the fracture is rearranged, according to what is necessary.
And another method is to place the injured person on their back, and hang their hand from their neck with a bandage. Then instruct two assistants, one to hold what is above the fracture with their hands, and the other to hold below it, each extending to their respective side.
And if you wish to make the extension stronger, then tie below the fracture with a bandage, and above it with another bandage; and each of the two assistants should extend to their respective sides.
If the fracture is near the articulation of the humerus, then it is necessary to put one bandage below the nipple and another beneath the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary to put a bandage above that place and above the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease and without effort, until when the fracture is aligned according to what is necessary, and a good application is made: then tie it, if not an hot abscess occurs.
If a hot abscess occurs, then loosen the bandage until the seventh day. And put on it a piece of clean cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until the abscess quiets down: then tie it: and the method of tying is to apply a plaster over the fracture.
Then put a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster. Then add the arm to the support, and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And put on a cloth, and tie around the support arm, so that the arm stands in its place like a star: if nothing prevents you from it, nor is anything altered concerning you because of the fractured bone.
But if something is altered concerning you because of the fractured bone, then use a star-shaped bandage: and it is, that you put on the fracture itself a star-shaped bandage which is wider and stronger than other bandages. And you should place between every width of the finger's breadth: and let the length of the stellae be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers' breadth on every side.
Then tie above the stellae a constriction as I described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is, that your constriction above the place of the fracture should be stricter. And as much as the fracture is more elongated, let the constriction be less.
If you see the position of the stellae and the constriction, as we have said, at the hour when you restore the member at once, then do it: and if you apply a warm apostema, then release the constriction and stellae until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the ligament for three days altogether, so that there may not occur itching at the site, or inflammation, or be prohibited by food from reaching the member, on account of the excessive tightness of the constriction. Correct it therefore entirely, according to what we have said.
If you are confident that nothing will happen from it: then do not loosen the ligature until after three days, or more.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his hand on his stomach: and place under the supporting cushion a full layer of wool, evenly.
And consider at every hour of night or day, that the shape of the fractured member is not loosened, or the ligature softened. Correct it therefore entirely by your virtue.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have mentioned, so that the food is subtle at first, until the wound begins to consolidate: then it is necessary to increase his food.
For it is the custom of the helper and the knee to be constricted for forty days: then therefore it is necessary to loosen, and to administer a bath, and to be treated with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture was dirty and crushed, then do not release the bandage from it for 50 days, and up to two months.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two hollows.
One of them is small and it is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is the one that is placed below the small one on the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger hollow bone is fractured, or the smaller one; and perhaps they are both fractured at the same time.
When therefore the smaller hollow bone is fractured above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower hollow bone is fractured, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones are fractured at the same time.
If therefore the bone that is fractured is the smaller hollow one above, then it is fitting for the physician, in its restoration, to apply a small extension with ease, until it is equal to itself.
If indeed the larger hollow bone is the one that is fractured: then it is necessary to apply a stronger binding force.
And it is necessary to apply the shape of the hand at its restoration, and also an extended extension above the pillow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers above: and let it be lower at the joint of all the fingers, or the remaining ones: and let the weak person sit squarely on himself, and let the pillow be in front of him, or directly above him, at a height, so that the weak person does not incur labor.
Then the assistant extends the arm from the lower part, or with their hand, or with a bandage. And another assistant extends it from above similarly.
Then the physician should reduce the bone, so that he restores it to a better shape as much as possible.
What if there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then try to reduce every fragment into its own place with your strength.
If indeed there appears a separated splinter in it, and it pierces the skin, and there is no hope of restoring it: then put something over it, and remove it according to the method that we mentioned in the previous cases.
If, however, the fracture is with a wound, I have already dealt with that in a separate chapter, so receive the treatment of it from there.
What if at the beginning of your restoration a hot aposteme occurs, then apply a linen cloth with a cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be a medium between thickness and subtlety: and tie the cloth over it with a subtle strapping, until the aposteme settles down: then remove the cerate, and put on a plaster prepared from flour of millet with egg white. Then put on splints, and let the splint that is put on the fracture itself be a little broader and stronger.
And know that the number of splints on the arm is six in most arrangements: whether the fracture is in one forearm or in two forearms at once.
Then put your strapping over the place of the fracture stronger and more vehement, and a little more with the strapping above the fracture or below it: or put a softer strapping, slightly, according to what was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture be soft, humid cloths: and not rough, harsh ones. And let the thread with which it is bound be made from linen, medium between subtle and thick, as we narrated.
And consider the member and ligament after a few days. If something happens that requires rectification, such as itching that occurs in a limb, then it is necessary to bathe the affected limb with warm water until the itching subsides and release the limb without constriction, until it subsides; then tighten it again.
If the constriction is already softened and the bone is already removed, and similar things: then rectify the whole thing with your strength, and consider it again.
For if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb because of the excess constriction, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and release it for some days, until it runs to that nourishment, then constrict it.
But if none of the things we mentioned happen to the patient, then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days or around that time.
Then the hand of the patient should be suspended at his neck. And let his neck be equal, and it should be protected with its strength from agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it will be restored in twenty-eight days. All of that is according to the dispositions of the complexions, and the dispositions of strength.
Of the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that fracture rarely occurs in the pelvis of the hand and the nodes of the fingers. And it does not occur to it, unless there is much repeated rubbing.
When therefore a fracture of the sole occurs, or rubbing: then it is necessary that the patient should sit squarely: and let his seat be before him according to equality. Then put his hand on it extended.
Then the attending physician extends the broken bone, and adjusts it until they join with a good joint.
Then it is fitting to apply a poultice and a compress, if there does not occur a warm aposteme. Then put a star-shaped dressing on top according to the size of the place, and you have already wrapped it in a linen cloth.
If the fracture is around the lower part of the sole of the foot, then make a spherical shape out of cloth; and bind it to the injured person, so that it holds with the foot in its fractured state; then tie it up with a long cloth.
And let the dressing be of leather, in which there is gentleness, so that the leather may be applied to the body of the foot; and tie it up, according to what is fitting.
If the fracture is on the outer side, then it is fitting to put a dressing on top and another dressing below on the sole of the foot; and let the hand be open and straight. Then put a bandage, as the hand revolves; and extend it between the toes with a binding.
If there occurs a fracture of some nodes of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be straightened according to what is fitting. Then tie it up with the sole of the foot; and if you wish, put a straight, small dressing on it, so that the fracture may be elevated, and not moved.
If the fracture is of the remaining fingers, such as the middle finger, and the index finger, or the ring finger, then it should be straightened, and tied with a healthy finger that follows it; or they should all be tied together according to their continuity; and this is better. Or put a small dressing on top of it, as we said about the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition of a warm aposteme; and oppose with it what is fitting, when something happens from that, according to how its story is repeated.
On the Restoration of a Fracture of the Hip.
The hip joint is frequently dislocated and is made manifest to the senses, because it turns inward and outward to the front and back parts.
And its restoration is done thus, that a ligament is tightened above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And the weakened part is stretched out over its own surface.
Then every ligament is extended to its own part according to equality: and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then the ligament should be towards the part of the pubis, so that the extension falls upwards: and another ligament below the fracture.
And thus similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then the ligament should be near the knee, so that the extension is towards the lower parts.
Then the physician equalizes the bone with both his hands, until he reduces it according to the similarity of the natural figure: and he joins the bone with a good conjunction.
Then it is necessary to apply an emplaster and a bandage, if there does not occur an abscess in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it: then leave it for some days, until the warm abscess settles, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be thus tight. It is necessary to put a soft involution over the fracture, that is, a binding: and double or triple it: and let the excess remain above. Then bind the leg, until the heel bone reaches the root of the hip.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee towards the lower parts, and let the extremities of the threads be tied at the upper part of the two joints. Then roll over the leg and hip what is left over from the involution.
Then put on the hip at the place of the fracture a stellar bandage, and put one of them as a stellar bandage over the knee joint.
Then fill in the empty spaces between the thigh and hip with soft linen cloths, so that the structure is equalized.
Then start with a strong bandage from the middle over the place of the fracture, with three or four envelopings. And as you extend it more with a bandage at the place of the fracture, let your bandaging be lesser and softer, and more gentle.
Then apply two extremities of a thread, which you had inserted between the hip and thigh. Bind it therefore with three stellar bandages that are above. Then weave with the two extremities of a thread downwards, until you reach the heel of the foot. And tie with each string, also the extremities of the stellar bandages on the other side: so that the bandage is not removed from its place.
Then leave the bandaging on that, while it remains, so that there occurs no corrosion of the limb, nor abscess, nor inflammation, and similar things. If anything happens from that. Provide therefore with its treatment, and correct what has happened there entirely, according to what we have said many times.
If indeed there is a splinter of bone that pierces: then it is necessary to equalize that, if it is possible for you. And if not, find over that, and remove that: and care for it with the treatment that preceded, until it is healed.
And when this fracture of the hip is bound, besides what stellar bandages are added to it, as we said in the case of the arm and forearm.
However, in the restoration of a fracture of this kind, there does not occur a lame limping. And if it is restored alone, apart from what is applied to another wound, then it is necessary that the one who is lame should always limp.
And you should know that the hip is strained for fifty days, or a little more or less: all of that is according to the diversity of complexions and the remaining dispositions.
On the restoration of a fractured knee joint.
You should know that fracture of the knee joint rarely occurs, but it often suffers from sprains.
If a fracture does occur to it, then it is not unless there is either a crack or an opening in its parts. And that is with a wound and without a wound. And it stands upon all of that with sense.
And its restoration is thus, that what has been disrupted from its parts with fingers, until it aggregates and is joined according to what is possible from equality and facility, and construction.
Then put a plaster made from flour of sesame or millet, with egg whites: and place on it a rounded stave, if you need it: and bind above it a convenient strapping suitable to that.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have narrated about other fractures: such as a warm poultice, and similar to that: thus, so that you may oppose every accident with what is convenient for it, until it is healed.
On the restoration of a fractured shin.
You should know that there are two bones in the shin, of which one is thick and is called by the name of the shin: and the other is subtle and is called the ankle bone.
And it happens to both of them from types of fracture that happen to the two bones of the arm: and because of that its restoration is made, just like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
When two bones are fractured together, the leg is bent towards all parts.
And if a thinner bone is fractured, the leg is bent towards the front part.
And if a thicker bone is fractured, it protrudes downwards; and it is one of those things that do not hide from the thigh.
Then administer extension, and alignment, and binding of the stars according to what is equal.
However, it is necessary, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having many complications: so that the extension should be minor, and your alleviation should be gentle.
And in the leg indeed there is something extra from the operation above the arm; and it is, when you align the stars, and complete your entire operation, then take two bands of pine wood, which are administered in the fabrication of splints that are placed under the tables: and they are made from hand spats: and choose from those which have a small thickness, and not from the minute ones: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee down to the bottom.
Then wrap above each band with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and another on the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the bottom of the foot.
Then bind two bands at three places with two extremities and the middle. For when the stricture is prohibited, the leg declines to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now indeed the splint is administered from wood according to the length of the leg, and the leg is placed in it, so that it may preserve it from motion.
And it is most necessary to do this in a fracture when the wound is properly clean.
Then examine the leg every day, and strive with utmost care to avoid aposteme, or inflammation, or other things. If therefore something happens from those things, then oppose it with what is suitable until he is healed, if God wills.
On the fracture of foot bones and toes.
Indeed, a fracture of the heel bone does not penetrate deeply.
A fracture of the foot bones sometimes occurs, and a slight fracture also occurs to the toes; and it does not occur to them unless there is grinding in many settings.
What if a fracture of the foot bones occurs, and you see those bones raised above each other upon meeting, then the injured person should put their foot on the prepared ground, as one walking.
Then stand there, and put your foot on what is elevated from those bones; afterwards press or tread on it, and make it equal, until it returns to its places.
Then put on a plaster, and padding above; and place under the sole of the foot a small board, which has two flat heads.
Then bind it with the foot by a suitable strapping, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things which it needs.
When therefore three days or four have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones equal, whether they were fractured or displaced. The treatment therefore is in these things, as you see.
If some toes are broken, then restore them, and make them equal according to what I told you about the restoration of fingers on the hand. Then put a small stick on the toe according to the length of the toe, and let it be slightly wider; then place under the foot the board that I told you about, and bind it with a suitable strapping.
If however what is broken from the bones is two, or three, or more, then put on each toe a small stick of its own quantity, wrapped in a linen cloth: and bind the board to the lower foot; and let it stand on one head of the board outside the belly of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it is fitting for you, lest you should overlook, to consider all that I have told you about the remaining fractures from the accidents that we have mentioned; and to oppose each accident with what is suitable.
About the fracture of a woman's pubic bone, and the ossicle of the quadriceps muscle, and the rod of a man.
When the pubic bone of a woman is fractured, then have her sit squarely. Then incline her towards the dorsal part of her own hip, and let it be held back.
Then fill the cavity of her pubic bone with lint, until it fills it; and let it become spherical in shape within the pubic bone.
Then the woman is shaken, and her back is elevated gradually. For that cavity emerges, until it becomes a sphere before the gate of the pubic bone, and the broken bone returns to its place.
Then put a small cushion on her back. When therefore she wants to urinate, remove the cushion with ease, then let her urinate; and reduce it according to the method by which you first introduced it; then let it return to its original small cushion.
Let her do that for seven days, or around that time, for the bone itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a bladder of a goat, and tie above its opening a tube of reed, and introduce the whole bladder into her pubic bone. Then blow into the tube with strength, until the bladder is inflated within the pubic bone, for the fracture returns to its place.
Then fill it with lint, and let it stand for several days, according to what we have narrated, until she is healed.
And when the quadriceps bone of a man or woman is fractured, then administer in its restoration, and adjust it according to what we have narrated for the ankle bone.
And it is not hidden that there are fractures nearby which touch hardly anything, especially when there is something of a study or use.
And understand my book thoroughly, because the fracture itself shows you the right way in many arrangements for its restoration and alignment; therefore, understand.
When a branch of a man is fractured, take the gullet of a goose: and insert the rod into it. Then put a dressing made of cloth over it, and it is dressed, and left for three days or around that time, and it will heal, if God wills it.
On the restoration of fractures of bones, when there is a wound.
It is fitting for one who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone or the adjacent ones, that prompt and phlebotomists attend to him immediately: if the conditions of phlebotomy are suitable, as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary to apply a poultice of crushed zegi powder on it immediately; if you do not have another remedy present.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not put it off, unless an abscess occurs.
If a hot abscess occurs, then cease the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach him on the third and fourth days at all, lest you cause bad accidents to happen to him.
If the fractured bone is prominent above the detected skin: then it is necessary to try to reduce it, and to adjust it with your hands using gentle and slight extension.
If the extension of it is not prepared, and its adjustment with your hands: then reduce it with that instrument. And there is an instrument made of iron, whose length is the measure of seven fingers, or eight; and its width is according to the size of the wound. And therefore it is necessary for the doctor to take three or four of these according to the size that is needed for that cure in every type of fracture.
And let it be round; and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it is not doubled over itself during compression at the time of operation; and let it have an acute extremity, which has an edge at its extremities. And let its upper part be thickened from the middle of it to the lower parts.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that the acute curved extremity of it be placed over the prominent bone extremity; and press it with it so unitedly until, when the bone returns and is adjusted by a certain adjustment.
Therefore, adjust the extremities of the fracture to each other.
If the extremity of the fracture is subtle and does not receive the instrument with good reception, then it is necessary to cut into the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
If indeed you are not able to reduce them with what we have narrated altogether, then cut into it with what is convenient for it from the incisions that we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way possible for you.
Then rasp what remains in the bone from roughness and small cortical layers.
When therefore the bone is reduced and finds weakness after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do it therefore. For you bring great relief to the weak, when you complete the restoration that you make for the bone.
Then soak a piece of cloth in wine, black. And it is especially so in summer. And do not apply it to an ulcerated wound, nor anything in which there is oil, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption.
Then administer the remedies at the hour of your complete restoration of the bone. And reveal the detected wound: thus, that you may cut with forceps a hole in the bandages according to the size of the wound.
And take every precaution, lest you constrict the wound with a fracture. For many times foolish physicians have done this: and they have caused to happen to their weak patients either death or decay, or ulcers, that is, sanies. And let your constriction be soft, gentle, and diverse from the constrictions of remaining fractures.
For if the wound is bad or it is a large wound and there are certain evil accidents that we have narrated, and you find pain in the suspended place: then it is not suitable to apply remedies to it. And make bandages for them out of rough cloths in the place of remedies, and constrict it with them.
When, however, it is after one day or two days, and you see the wound has already begun to generate pus: then remove from above the cloth that you had applied with wine. Then administer the liniments, and ointments with which it is our custom to heal wounds: such as the four-drug ointment, and similar to that.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And examine the wound every morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary to prepare the limb in such a way that pus flows out of it easily downwards with ease.
For if many days have passed over the wound and it is not consolidated, nor is the pus separated from it: then know that there are small particles of bone remaining. It is necessary to examine the wound with a probe. What truly belongs to those separated things, remove them, and extract their own. And what of those is not separated, and pierces the member, and makes the pain happen, try to cut them away, and remove them in every way that is possible to you at that time.
What if it happens that the wound becomes infected, or festering, that is, foul-smelling, or another type of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to apply a unique remedy for each of them, which suits its cure, whose preceding narration is in its own chapter.
And from this point on, it is necessary for my speech to stand, and you should have a present mind when the bone is fractured, and it protrudes above the member, such as the hip bone, and the surrounding ones, and similar ones from the large members, that is, so you do not assume an attraction of it, for many times it happens because of that death, or rather, abandon it, until it putrefies.
Perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then, therefore, treat the wound, if it seems good to you: and if not, abandon it.
On the treatment of a swelling on a fracture of the thigh bone.
This swelling often occurs during the healing of a fracture of the thigh bone. Especially when it is near the joints, and it becomes deformed from the shape of the member. And perhaps it prevents the member from its natural function.
Therefore consider then, if the swelling is recent: administer to it medicines that have a drying quality, such as aloe, and olibanum, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar ones.
Thus, take some of these, or all of them: and mix them with wine that is drying, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and apply them to the swelling in a poultice, and bind them on with a good bandage.
And release the stricture, not loosening it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another one, until the swelling is removed.
Or wrap a layer of lead above it, not deviating from the center. For lead has the property of removing every swelling that is feared in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned to stone, and is hardened, and urgent necessity demands its removal: then make an incision above it from the upper part, and cut away the prominent excess: or shave it off with certain rasps, until it is removed: and treat it thus, until it is healed.
On the cure when it is restored, and a member remains after that subtle one, not according to its own nature.
When a fracture of the bone is restored, and a member remains after that subtle one, weak: then it does not happen unless due to many causes. Of which one is, the multitude of solution of the ligament and its binding not according to what is necessary: or due to the excess of stricture of the ligaments, until nourishment is prevented from flowing to the member: or due to the multitude of superfluous embrocation at an unsuitable time: or due to the scarcity of blood in the weak body, and its debility.
And the treatment of that is to nourish the weak person, and to make their body tender, until blood is multiplied in them. And administer a bath. And bring joy and cheerfulness upon him.
Then put pitch on the member: so that it attracts much nourishment to itself. And apply frequent embrocation with warm water, until nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural shape.
On the cure of fractured bones when they are restored twistedly.
When a limb, already restored after its healing, has twisted deformity and a prominent fracture or knot, and is deformed in that shape of the limb, yet the limb is not prevented from its natural operation: then it does not seem necessary to receive his speech, who thinks that the limb should be refractured anew.
And already there were many foolish doctors and restorers who did that in our land.
And this operation is indeed detestable, leading to death or great fear, as if it were mortal.
But if the lameness and knot are recent: then it is necessary to foment with warm water, in which are decoctions of softening herbs, such as the leaves of altaea, and its root, and melilot, and similar things. And let a plaster be made for him with a softening plaster, such as a well-made dyachilon.
Or let there be applied an ointment of the root of altaea, and mixed with the fat of a hen, or sesame oil: and let it be plastered on with that.
Or let there be applied rich figs, and crushed with dove's dung, and similar things which are mentioned in medicines that diminish consolidation.
And when the knot is loosened with gentle, long, and mild friction, which is done with moist hands. And let the movement of the limb be administered to every part at all hours.
But if the twisting has already preceded, and become strong, and turned into a bony hardness, and necessity has been provoked to cure it with iron: then it is necessary to section above it, and release the continuity of the bone, and cut out what is superfluous from the knot or bone with subtle incisions: and let facility be administered in that with solicitude and study.
On Speech about Dislocation.
A dislocation is an exit of a joint from its place. Because it is prevented from its motion, and the form of the limb is deformed. And it causes to happen severe pains and vehement injuries.
When therefore a dislocation happens, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration: and not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes putrid, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Because of this, therefore, it is not suitable to move or stretch it during the hour of its putrefaction. Since it often causes spasms and painful wounds.
But when that happens, then it is necessary to hasten to bloodletting of the affected part. Then it should be left alone until the putrefaction subsides.
Then the limb should be anointed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And the whole limb should be treated so that its memory returns to its proper place.
I have now arranged the chapters on dislocations, and also according to what preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated lower jawbone.
The two jaws rarely become dislocated.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to one of two modes. Or indeed they are removed from their places with a small removal, and are softened for a short time. Or they are dislocated with a complete and total dislocation, until they become soft towards the chest, until the saliva of the affected part flows, and it cannot hold it, nor can it close its mouth, and its tongue is entangled with speech.
When the dislocation of it is small, then it returns into many dispositions by itself with fewness.
If the dislocation is complete and entire, then it is necessary to administer the reduction quickly and not to delay at all: and it is as if the servant head of the injured person holds fast; and the physician inserts the thumb of his hand into the root of the jawbone at the place of the dislocation, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he inserts both thumbs simultaneously, if the dislocation is from two parts. And the remaining fingers of his hands are outside, which are equal.
And instruct the injured person to clean their jawbones and free them for chewing in every way. And the physician adjusts the jawbone and pushes the jawbone until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both jawbones are involved simultaneously; then administer a fomentation with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy: nor delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When they return and are adjusted, and the injured bone is closed, and not made soft; then place over them small pads of cotton cloth, with a cerate that is made from wax: and rose oil.
Then bind with ease using soft ligaments.
And the injured person should sleep on their back: and their head should be positioned between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right or left; nor should they try to chew something: rather, let their food be given in a soft drink, until when the pains are removed, and the jawbone is restored: then they can eat what they want, and administer it with ease.
Let them not strain over the operation of their mouth during eating and drinking, and kissing, until the jawbone is restored, and they are healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some hour and do not convert to their places, then many fevers often occur from it, and constant pains; and perhaps the weak belly is loosened, and perhaps pure yellow bile is vomited.
Since you have seen that, then know that it is a loss, and many times he dies who suffers that within ten days.
On the reduction of a dislocated elbow, and the extremity of the shoulder.
The elbow indeed is not dislocated from the inner side, on account of its continuity with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated outwardly, and that is made clear by sensation.
And its restoration is, that the weak person lies on his back, and extends his arms. Then bind the place with your strong palm's binding force, for it will return to its position.
Then put a plaster on top of it, and poultices, and bind it tightly.
The extremities that follow the elbow and are continued with it are not dislocated, unless rarely. If it is dislocated at some time: then it is necessary that it be reduced and adjusted according to what we have said, and what has been prepared for you.
Then put a plaster on top of it, and poultices, and a tight binding. And instruct the weak person to adhere to tranquility and quiet, until they are healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the shoulder is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
On the reduction of a dislocated shoulder.
Know that the shoulder is not dislocated except in three ways.
Of which one is, that it is dislocated downwards towards the armpit. The second is, so that it may be displaced towards the part of the chest. And perhaps it may be displaced towards the upper part of the shoulder: and that happens rarely.
And it does not dislocate backwards, because of the blade-like shape. And it does not dislocate forwards, because of the tendons.
And indeed it is most often displaced and protrudes downwards towards the buttock: especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it protrudes quickly in them, and is reduced quickly.
In those, however, whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite of that, namely because it protrudes with difficulty, and is reduced with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men that they receive a blow or a fall, and the shoulder becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and is thought to be because of that, since it was already dislocated.
Therefore, you should experience that, until you stand on certain knowledge of it, and then proceed to its cure.
And the dislocation is recognized when it is towards the buttock: and you distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a healthy one. For you find a clear difference between them, and you find a concavity at the head of the shoulder, and under the buttock upon touch, as if it were a void: and the weak one cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all its motions.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards: then you find a clear touch, and it does not hide.
And indeed the reduction of this dislocation is easy, when it is recent, or the patient is a weak boy.
And its reduction is, that the assistant lifts his hand to the upper parts. Then you place your thumbs under the buttock, and lift the joint with strength upwards to its place: and the assistant lifts his hand, and extends it upwards. Then he should put it underneath, for he himself returns quickly.
If, however, he does not return through what we have said, and there are many days from the beginning of the dislocation: then it is necessary that the injured person be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation be administered which softens and soothes, such as if they were soaking the roots of altaea, and fenugreek, and melilot in water, and administering it.
Then the injured person should be laid down on his back: and a sphere and wool should be placed under the bandage, which is equal in softness and tightness.
Then the physician should place his heel on the sphere, and lift the head of the shoulder with strength, and draw the hand of the injured person downwards.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method: and it is, that you should make the strong man who is longer than the injured person present, and stand at his side, and insert his shoulder under the bandage of the injured person: and lift his bandage upwards, until the injured person is suspended in the air. And another should draw the hand of the injured person downwards towards his own belly. If, however, the injured person is weak: then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced also according to another method: and it is, that a long wooden beam is fixed in the ground, whose head has a rounded figure: like a mason's hammer: which should not be thick, nor too subtle. Then soft cloths should be placed under the injured person after they are placed on top of the head of the beam: and the injured person should stand along the length of the beam.
Then his hand is extended downwards on one side and his body is stretched out from the other side with strength: for the joint returns to its place with swiftness.
If his reduction is difficult through all that we have mentioned, then use this care: and it is to receive a stick whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness the quantity of two fingers: which has a round head, so that its entrance may be alleviated into the deep wound, according to this form.
Then fine linen cloths are tied over the round head, so that they do not hurt the weak limb.
Then it should be placed under the weak limb, so that the hand or arm is stretched out over the stick up to the bottom: and the stick is tied over the assisting limb and arm and extremity of the hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a step of a ladder in width, and the hand is stretched out downwards, and the rest of the body is suspended hanging from the other side. For the joint indeed enters immediately.
When therefore the entrance is completed according to whatever mode is possible: then it is necessary to place under the weak limb a sphere of equal quantity made of wool. Then put the prepared plaster made from mill dust with olive oil, and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it turns out to be on top.
Then bind underneath the weak limb a suitable bandage: and the ligament is turned back over the plaster on top: and his hand is attached to his neck, and left there, so that his hand is not moved for seven days.
And it is necessary to place nourishing food near the weak limb, until the member is comforted: because it is quicker for its healing.
Then a larger bandage should be placed after seven days, or five, and exercised with movement. If it has become firm and not soft, then it is already healed.
If the joint is dislocated frequently, either because of the moisture that happens to it or due to another cause: then it is necessary to apply a cautery with three probes, according to what has been previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
If this is done and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not confirmed, and the plaster and bandage are applied many times over it, and it does not become firm, and falls apart, and cannot be elevated to its proper place: then know that the nerves which are at the head of the humerus have been cut or stretched or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its proper position.
But dislocations which occur towards the chest and nipple, or to the back, are reduced with a push and extension using the hands; until it returns to its place, and is treated with the remaining bandage and care, until it heals.
If it happens that after healing, there is heaviness in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then let the patient use a bath many times, until that heaviness is alleviated, and it returns to its original nature.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty also: and it indeed dislocates to all parts, and especially to the front or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: since it is evident to the sight and to the touch, according to whatever shape it has been dislocated. And when you compare a dislocated elbow with a healthy one, that is clearly demonstrated to you. For the joint is injured, and it cannot bend the arm, nor touch the shoulder with it.
And it is necessary, that you straighten and reduce the dislocation quickly, before a warm aposteme (abscess) forms.
For if a warm abscess forms, its reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it will not be completely healed, especially when it is on the back. For it is worst in all kinds of dislocations, and a more violent pain accompanies them: and many times it ends with death.
And the restoration of it when it is one of those that can be reduced, is to extend his hand with both hands: and his arm should be stretched out, and the doctor's hands are above the elbow and below it, and he himself presses the joint with his thumbs or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is to the front: then he returns it thus, that he doubles his hand with his own hand, until he strikes with the base of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not obey to reduction, then he administers a strong and forceful extension: and it is, that two assistants stretch out the arm: and two assistants hold it also, so that it does not move back during the extension.
Then the arm is revolved to its entire part after a double-layered long bandage or a wide ligature is applied above his hand.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he anoints it with oil: so that it is one of those things that help to lubricate the joint and facilitate its movement. Then he presses the joint with a forceful impulse, until it returns to its place.
And after its reduction, it is necessary to put a plaster on it, in which there is a stimulant, and an evacuation with a white ointment: and to bind with a suitable strapping. And the arm should be suspended from the neck of the injured person: and left for some days.
Then it is dissolved. If therefore it becomes firmly fixed in its place, then loosen the ligament from it, and leave it alone.
And if you see the joint is not well strung, then apply a plaster, and a bandage, and leave it alone for several days, until it becomes strong. Then loosen it.
If, however, there occurs a heaviness after the reduction of the joint, and the arrangement is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and subtle friction, and compression, until it becomes smooth, or place over the joint a poultice made from a rich, fatty substance, that is, a ripe, fatty poultice. Then bind it, and leave it alone on it for a day and night. Then remove it, and introduce it into a bath. When it sweats, then rub the joint with a tempered friction. Then apply to it a first application, and a second, and a third, at the time of entering the bath, until it becomes smooth.
And if you wish, place on it a warm poultice made from dung, moistened with butter, and bind over it.
Do that several times. It becomes smooth, and returns to its own arrangement.
On the treatment of a dislocation by Almahassem.
Almahassem's hand is often dislocated, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Unlike other joints. However, it is necessary that there be speed in the reduction of its dislocation at the hour when it becomes dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed; or a hot aposteme may occur in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is, that you place Almahassem's weak hand on a table, and extend the serving hand; and the physician places his palm over the prominence of the joint, and pushes it, until it returns to its place.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm has already receded to the ventral side of the hand: then the weakened back of the hand should be placed on a table at the point of extension and reduction; and if the dislocation proceeds to the back of the hand, then the position of the hand itself should be inside on the table, that is, the inner part of the hand should be placed on the table, so that the doctor's hand falls upon the same prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then bind it with a plaster, applying an emollient poultice, and leave it, without repeating the treatment.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you put it in its reduced state, after the days have passed, yet the joint remains according to its own twist; and it does not harm the weakened part, unless the hand is softened, and cannot grasp anything: then you should know that the nerves are cut or worn away.
There is no ingenuity in it, except to bind it with a caustic near the site of the injury. And perhaps it does not benefit from that either.
Since therefore it does not return to its proper alignment, then put on it the plaster that we have described. Then bind it and leave it for five days; afterwards unbind it and leave it, and exercise the hand.
If however its movement is restricted, and something serious happens to it: then soothe it with warm water, and rub it gently several times, until it becomes relaxed.
On the Treatment of Dislocations of Fingers.
When a finger is dislocated from its joint, whether it be a finger to the back of the palm or to its ventral side, then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns to its place.
Then sustain the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part from which it was dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then hang him upside down and stretch him out until he becomes straight on that his own day.
When therefore the night has fallen, bind him according to the same narrative; and do not cease to release him during the day; and exercise him with movement; and bind him at night.
Do that for several days, until it is strengthened.
And do similarly with him, if the hand is dislocated to the belly; and bind him to that same part, and perform your first operation with him, until he is healed.
And do similarly with him when it is dislocated to the other parts.
On the treatment of a dislocation of the dorsal vertebrae.
When there is a complete dislocation of the dorsal vertebrae, or a complete dislocation of the neck, or many vertebrae are removed from their places; then there is no treatment for it, because death quickly follows for the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's evacuation occurs without his will, and he cannot hold it; and often certain members of his body become weak because of it, such as one of his legs, or two arms, or one of them; or a vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it is removed, because often the removal occurs to the four parts.
Therefore, that which is removed to the back, is called Gibbosity.
The treatment of it is, that you should consider then, if Gibbosity occurs from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor is there any healing from it at all.
But in that which occurs from a fall, or a blow, or similar things, the ancients have already spoken of methods of treatment in a lengthy discourse. Whose benefit does not return with profit.
I have already abbreviated it from that, whose little excuse is due to much of it from my proximity to the intention: and I have exposed it in my exposition, and I have formed its instrument in a diverse way from what they showed and exposed.
I therefore say that what happens in cases of gibbosity which occur on the front side is not an ingenuity, nor a healing from it.
And similarly in those cases that are directed towards both sides as well.
And it is not treated from them, unless those cases that occur on the back properly, as I describe: and it is so that the weak one is stretched out on his face on a level pedestal near the wall; and a moist cloth is spread underneath, so that his chest is not hurt.
Then a straight stick is placed fixed in the ground at the edge of the stool, near his head: and another stick near his feet at the other edge of the stool: and the attendant holds the stick, and it is not fixed to the ground. And another attendant holds the other one.
Then a wrapping is placed over the head of the weak one, and under his eyebrows a soft bandage, firmly: and extend the limb's bandage to the stick that is near his head: and bind it in it.
Then tie another bandage above his haunches, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages together, and bind them to the other stick that is near his feet.
Then all the attendants stretch out to the part of the stick with the bandage, and the two sticks are not moved from their places where they are fixed: but they are not fixed, as we have said.
And the doctor should place his hands on the vertebrae with strength, until it returns to its place: or he should place them on a tablet. Then he should attach his feet to the tablet, until it returns to its place.
If, however, it does not return to its place through this treatment, then take a tablet whose length is approximately three arms. And let it be placed on the wall from which we spoke, which is near the weak one, the place into which the extremities of the tablet will enter. Then place the middle of the tablet on the spine. And let the doctor place his feet on the other extremity. And let him tighten well, until the spine is straightened, and returns to its own place.
Then if you wish, operate with a turning instrument that is wrapped with your hand: and it is, to fix in the ground at the head of the patient two wooden blocks, whose length is one arm; and at his head on the back of the two wooden blocks.
And let the length that is between each wooden block be one palm; and now it has been made a hole in every place where the turning instrument runs; and let all the wood be firmly fixed to the ground, so that it does not move at all.
And a round wood is inserted; and it is a turning instrument on which the ligament is wound around in the two holes of the two wooden blocks.
And on its extremity is a hole, in which the wood is fixed, whose length is one palm with that which is wound around; and in the two other wooden blocks it is similarly arranged.
Then tighten the ligaments that you have wound around on the chest of the patient with the turning instrument that is at his head; and the ligaments that you have wound around on his legs with the turning instrument that is at his feet.
Then stand at every turning instrument a servant who will twist his hand with the strap, with which the turning instrument is wound around; and the doctor straightens the spine, according to what we have said.
This is the form of the turning instrument, which follows.
Then after the spine has been straightened and the place is equalized, then it is necessary to apply an evacuating poultice with white of egg. Then a compress. Afterwards place on top of the poultice a small plate made from a tablet, whose width is three fingers, or around that. And its length is the quantity that occupies the space of gibbosity, and above the healthy part of the spine.
Then it should be tied with a ligature which we have mentioned. And let the weak person be nourished with subtle nutrition until he is healed.
What if something of the swelling remains at the site at the end of healing: then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and soothe, with the administration of a regimen which we have described for a long time. And now let it be applied on that plate of lead.
And when a swelling occurs at the last vertebra of the back: and it is thought to be displaced by that, and it is that bone which now protrudes. Therefore it is not suitable, lest a fatal assumption be made with this medicine: for perhaps it may bring about death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
You should know that the joints of the ankle and the joints of the shoulder do not occur unless there is a dislocation only: nor does it occur to them what occurs to other joints from small removals and concavities.
And indeed, the ankle joint is dislocated according to four parts. Which is, because it is dislocated forward and backward.
And indeed, it is most often dislocated inward: and it is dislocated little to the front and to the back.
And the treatment of its inward dislocation is this: because when you join the weak leg with the healthy one, it becomes longer: and its knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the weak person cannot bend their foot at the groin: and there is a place that follows the inflamed groin, an aposteme, hot from the fact that the head of the hip joint has already arrived there.
And the sign of the one to whom a dislocation occurs outward is that they are contrary accidents to these accidents.
And the sign of one to whom a dislocation happens in the front is that it stretches out a healthy leg according to its complement; however, it does not duplicate it without pain which is in the knee. And if it is constricted for walking, it cannot step over it at the front; and urine retention is held back by it, and the groin is affected, and during walking there is bruising on the heel.
And the sign of one to whom a dislocation happens at the back is that it does not stretch out the knee, nor can it duplicate it until it duplicates the groin; and the leg is also shorter than the other: and the groin is softened, and the head of the hip is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this type of dislocation is thus, that you should consider then, if the dislocation is old, which has already become chronic with the one having it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its natural disposition: then there is no cure for it at all. Therefore it is not fitting that a deep supposition should be made about it.
In that case, however, where the dislocation is recent and is one of the four species of dislocations, then it is proper to proceed thus, so that you may join the ends together, and stretch it inward and outward, and move it right and left: for perhaps it will return into place, and not require further treatment.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant, and stretch out the leg from the lower part: either with his own hands, or with a bandage, which is tied above the knee of the leg: and another assistant stretches out his hand from above, so that he may insert his hands under the thighs. Then he should bind with a soft bandage around the root of the hip, and hold the end of the other bandage.
And let the extension be either from the front of the groin to the part of the flanks, or from the back to the part of the back.
And let there be one extension of all their limbs, until the weakened patient is lifted with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this indeed is a type of extension that is a common type of four types.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type of that which I shall speak of regarding proper cure.
In the reduction of this very thing, when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the weakened patient be placed on his healthy side. Then a bandage should be placed over the root of the hip, where it is between the head of the hip, and the place that is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the part of the groin to the higher part of the body, towards the armpit.
Then another strong assistant should take his arm, and reduce the large mass of the hip of the weakened patient, and extend it outward with a vigorous extension. Now indeed it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the remaining types of cure, by which this limb is reduced.
If it is raised above you, and does not obey you at the entrance with this type of cure altogether: then it is necessary that both feet of the weakened patient be bound together with a strong bandage, gentle, over both heels, and over both knees: and let the length of each be compared to its own quantity of four fingers; and let the weakened leg be extended more by the quantity of two fingers.
Then the weakened patient should be suspended from the top of a beam, which is in the house: and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then order a strong youth to support the head of the hip: and let him be suspended with the weakened youth: and another youth should push, supporting the hip with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
In the reduction of this proper part, when there is a dislocation to the outer parts, it is necessary that the weakened person lies on a couch, according to what we have described in the case of having a hump. And let the bandage be tightened above his leg, properly weakened, and above his chest.
Then two wooden splints are placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then a wooden splint is placed in the middle of the stool, fixed well; on which are wrapped humid cloths, so that they do not harm the weakened person: and let the wooden splint be between his hips, so that it does not pull towards the lower parts during extension.
Then everyone assisting attends to their own part, and the physician completes the reduction with his hand.
If it then obeys to reverting, it will be good.
And if not, place on the same tablet, and press upon it, as we said in the case of hump-backedness, equally. However, it is necessary that the weakened person lies on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the weakened person's leg is extended quickly; and this is according to the same narrative on the couch: and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the groin of the weakened person. Then he tightens it with his other hand, and with that tightening he places it extended downwards towards the knee joint.
And if the dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary that the weakened person is extended downwards, and he should be raised above the ground: on the contrary, it is necessary that he is placed on a hard surface, as it is necessary if his ankle is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about his reclining on two anchors: and this is on his face, and the extended bandages: according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a tablet also on the place to which the joint returns, with completion of reduction according to what is necessary.
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you: and it is, that you stretch out the two legs of the injured person: and when you see them equal, and the injured person draws in his leg, and expands it without excuse: then you know, because the limb has already returned according to what is necessary: then join the two hips, and put on a plaster, and bind with a bandage, that is, a wheel: thus, so that it does not move to any part: and let the injured person remain quiet for three days or four.
Then loosen the bandage and plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in stature, then you know that the dislocation is now firmly established: therefore dismiss the injured person to walk.
And if you see any softening: then return and plaster it, and bind it according to your first tightening: and let it go for three days also.
Then loosen it and make it walk slowly on both legs for some days, until it is well comforted.
Of Dislocations of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, that is, backward.
And it is not dislocated forward.
